Foreword to the English Edition
When I started playing chess in March 1979 in Riga, everything about the game was associated with Mikhail Tal in my city. Especially a few months later, after Tal’s victories in Montreal and the Riga Interzonal.
However, when you start you’re not necessarily connected to the top yet. You play in your family, go to chess school and learn simple things about the game.
Being passionate about reading I tried not to miss any book or chess magazine from my father’s library. For some reason, Tal and Damsky’s book
Attack with Mikhail Tal
was not there but... the small book on Tal by Alexander Koblenz that you now hold in your hands turned out to be the perfect substitute!
A special memory from the book is those lines that might easily go unnoticed:
After playing a tournament game, he would stay in the Riga chess club until late evening, giving improvised simultaneous displays and playing innumerable casual games.
Leaving the club late, Misha was full of excitement. With his cap askew and his coat fluttering in the wind, he would walk about the Riga streets with his friends, who were chess lovers as passionate as him.
“Of course, you should have played knight f3 to e5; if he captured that knight, a sacrifice on f7 would decide things...” The words, intelligible only to other chess players, rang in the air. The occasional passers-by would stop, look at those oddballs, and shake their heads in confusion...
I vividly recall my own post-mortems. Did I stay in the chess school until everyone had finished? Of course I did! Would I also walk back with friends? Yes! But something was different. More than 30 years after Tal’s teenage years Riga had grown into a pretty big city and the young players mainly lived in different districts. So we only managed a short exchange of words with an ice-cream and... tram number 6 or bus number 16 and so on. Until the next day. No deep analysis, everyone was just relying on their knowledge.
The most important part of Koblenz’s book for me was my impatient desire to repeat Tal’s path to the top, which reading the book sounded simple: even the game annotations looked easy. OK, today we see with the computer that those sometimes brief comments are not always accurate, but I believe that’s of minor importance.
It was another 2-3 years before I got to know Koblenz, and a little later (still being extremely shy) Mikhail Tal himself. However, my path had been marked out much earlier and it was thanks to this book on Tal’s street fights.
Thinking about the past, I realize that the biggest treasure that chess players possessed (and I was one of the last players to get the grandmaster title without computer preparation) was chess emotions. When searching for the truth, you knew that it might not be the ultimate truth, so emotions were the direct bridge from your knowledge to intuition.
The union of Alexander Koblenz and Mikhail Tal was a very emotional one, and reading the book you barely notice how hard their work together on chess was. You read short notes on Tal’s almost irrational chess and only between the lines realize how accurately Koblenz and Tal analyzed positions of adjourned games (for example, against Spassky or Botvinnik), and how serious their opening preparation was. Koblenz didn’t provide any concrete insights on how to become a grandmaster, he only wrote a sincere story which highly inspired me.
I am very happy that this old but highly instructive book is finally out in English. The old Russian edition has always been a special treasure for me and I truly enjoyed reading it through again (yes, the original version without editor’s notes, though I have seen those as well) before writing these few lines.
Alexei Shirov
, Riga,
17 February 2021
Introduction
The name of the ex-world champion Mikhail Tal is famous in the chess world. His games, rapid ascension and world championship win, and then the return match loss, have been widely discussed. The interest in the Riga grandmaster is understandable – no chess player before him has managed to climb to the very top of chess Olympus so soon.
Tal’s successes were explained in various ways. Some talked about his special playing style and exceptional luck that accompanied him in tournaments. Others pointed out his “Mozart-like” lightness and even “demonic strength”, which supposedly allowed him to achieve great success without much effort. I must immediately disappoint those who opened this book in search for sensations: no “Tal enigma” has ever existed. The talented player’s way to the top was difficult and full of trials and tribulations. This book will tell you about the stages along this way, the wins and losses, the fulfilled hopes and disappointments.
Twelve Years Ago
Assembly hall of Riga Middle School number 22. A thin boy with a shy smile approaches the demonstration board. He quickly moves a piece. Black’s reply, another move – the solution is correct!
I remember that it wasn’t the quickness of the solution that most impressed me, but a drastic change I saw in the boy as soon as he approached the board. He transformed completely. The smile on his face immediately disappeared, he became incredibly serious.
Later, I learned that Misha was a goalkeeper in his class football team, school checkers champion and one of the most active members of the Young Pioneers’ Palace chess circle. He was also an avid piano player, his favorite composers being Tchaikovsky and Chopin.
The young Tal studied well, but his mathematics teacher was unhappy: Misha preferred to solve everything in his mind, without writing anything in the exercise book. However, he would make exceptions for the boys from senior classes who’d approach him during the break.
“Misha, quick, help me solve this!”
And, even though it was much more interesting to play football in the courtyard, he would pick up a pencil and quickly write down columns of numbers.
Tal’s memory has been exceptional since childhood. During the 24th Soviet chess championship, master Aivars Gipslis and I jokingly asked Misha, “Do you remember which line Keres played in the Queen’s Gambit against Boleslavsky in the third round of the 20th Soviet championship?”
“Stop pulling my leg,” the grandmaster answered. “Boleslavsky played Keres in round 19, not round 3. It was Boleslavsky who had white, and he played the Ruy Lopez.”
The passionate, selfless enthusiasm for chess struggle has never left Tal ever since he learned chess. After playing a tournament game, he would stay in the Riga chess club until late evening, giving improvised simultaneous displays and playing innumerable casual games.
Leaving the club late, Misha was full of excitement. With his cap askew and his coat fluttering in the wind, he would walk about the Riga streets with his friends, who were chess lovers as passionate as him.
“Of course, you should have played knight f3 to e5; if he captured that knight, a sacrifice on f7 would decide things...” The words, intelligible only to other chess players, rang in the air. The occasional passers-by would stop, look at those oddballs, and shake their heads in confusion...
And his mother would call the club yet again: “Do you know if Misha has finished his game? He hasn’t eaten his lunch yet...”
In one such evening, Misha managed to win a truly brilliant game. Here’s the finale of the game played at the 1949 youth tournament in Riga.
No. 1
M. Tal – A. Leonov
1949, Riga, Junior Tournament, round 7
19.Rf6!
A great blockading move. White parries the threat f7-f5 and, in his turn, threatens 20.Rxh6.
19...Qf8
19...Nxf6 didn’t help, because after 20.exf6 gxf6 21.Qxh6 f5, white wins with 22.Ng4!. After 19...gxf6, there’s 20.exf6 Nxf6 21.Qxh6 Qf8 22.Qxf6 Rd7 23.Ng4 Qg7 24.Qh4, with an unstoppable attack.
Editor’s note:
According to the computer, in the line shown by Koblenz black’s play can be radically improved with 21...Ne4!! (instead of 21...Qf8, which is actually a mistake) 22.Bxe4 f5, and after 23.Bxf5 exf5 24.Nxd5 Re6 25.Qg5+ Rg6 26.Ne7+ Kg7 27.Nxg6 Qxg6 28.Qe7+ Qf7 black can hold. However, instead of 20.exf6, white should play 20.Bxh7+, which is winning – 20...Kxh7 21.Ng4 Qf8 22.Rf1 f5 23.Nf6+ Kh8 24.Rf3 (threatening Rf3-g3-g8+) 24...Qg7 25.Rg3!+–.
20.Rf4! Bd7 21.Ng4 Be8 22.Nf6+! Nxf6 23.exf6 Rc7 24.fxg7 Kxg7 25.Qe5+ Black resigned.
The passion for chess didn’t stop Tal from graduating from high school ahead of time (he absorbed the whole elementary school program in just one year), and then from Latvian State University, where he majored in literature.
Tal already realized in his youth that to be successful in chess, it’s necessary to work very hard. This means studying theory, analyzing games of masters and grandmasters, playing through your own games, and learning from your mistakes.
But he had no time for that. School during the days, tournaments in the evenings, so he could only study theory at night.
“Come to our home,” Misha’s father would invite me, “perhaps you can reason with him? He read chess books until morning again.”
Once, during a walk, I seized the moment and tried to prove the importance of a correct daily regimen to Misha. He listened intently, keeping a polite silence.
“I’m sure you’ll become a master,” I said in the end, “but to achieve real success in chess, you need health.”
We said our goodbyes, and I sighed with relief: I had seemingly managed to convince him.
However, Misha’s father called me again the next morning...
The worries of his father, an old, respected doctor, were understandable. We had to influence the young enthusiast in some way. However, the circle of the talented teenager’s interests was very wide, and the desire to understand the mysteries of chess art became a real passion. Later, I came to the conclusion that this full mental burden gave Tal what he wanted the most – a feeling of deep internal satisfaction.
Even now, Tal loathes any kind of “quiet” rest. In all the years I’ve known him, I’ve never seen him at rest. Tal is busy at any time of day: he writes an article, or discusses the new issue of
Sahs
, or analyzes some game, or prepares for a lecture...
Before the 25th Soviet chess championship, Tal went to Kemeri to rest a bit. However, he only managed to stay at the resort for three days and then “fled” back to Riga.
This mental overexertion, this inability to spare himself, was always Tal’s Achilles heel. Perhaps this was one of the main reasons for his collapse which left both specialists and casual fans of the young grandmaster mystified.
Years of Trials
After Tal defeated Botvinnik and became world champion, many were sure that his brilliant chess abilities must have manifested themselves when he was a small child. However, Misha’s first steps in chess were no different from anyone else’s. He would constantly lose to his cousin, a regular chess opponent of his, and get very frustrated afterwards.
Misha achieved fourth category at the age of 11, and second category two years later. Soon, Mikhail was called up to the Latvian youth team. He played on last board, and the result – one win, two draws, and one loss – showed that he didn’t deserve a higher place in the team.
In 1950, the young player earned first category. The next year, he took part in his first adult tournaments – the championships of Latvia and Riga – and only shared 11th and 14th places.
Tal was especially “unlucky” in the openings. He would often get a difficult, hard-to-defend position right from the outset.
However, even back then the young chess player showed his ability to quickly and precisely calculate lines, attack ingeniously and search for hidden tactical resources in a difficult position.
The finale of the game played in the 1951 Latvian championship was very characteristic.
No. 2
A. Strautmanis – M. Tal
1951, Riga, Latvian Championship
White has put a lot of pressure on the c6 pawn. It looks like black will have a hard time avoiding defeat, however, he finds a way to save the game.
17...Ne4! 18.f3
18.Rxc6 is of course bad due to 18...Nc5!
18...Nd6 19.Rxc6 Nc4
Looks like a blunder...
20.R6xc4 b5! 21.Rxc8 bxa4 22.R8c7 Qe8 23.Rxa7 Bd8
It was better to put the bishop on d6.
Editor’s note:
Koblenz’s recommended move 23...Bd6 is better because it prevents white from invading the eighth rank with his rooks.
24.Ra8
Editor’s note:
24.Rc8 was worth considering, to split the duties of the two rooks: one of them would seize the eighth rank, while the other attacked the a4 pawn.
24...Qb5 25.Rcc8 Be7 26.Rxf8+ Bxf8 27.Kf2
, and the game ended in a
draw.
In the autumn of the same year, Misha played in an All-Union youth tournament and scored just 3.5 points out of 9. He performed better in the 1952 Latvian SSR Championship: he took 7th place, and became the republic’s champion the following year.
“You shouldn’t make so many sacrifices,” the team captain advised Tal before he headed to Kharkov for a youth tournament. Tal knew that his penchant for daring attacks and risky combinations would cause concern.
“At first, I tried not to take risks,” Tal recalled, “and played quietly. But then I just couldn’t help it. I got an inviting opportunity in a game against an Uzbekistan player...”
International master Rashid Nezhmetdinov told me what happened in that game.
“During that tournament, I acted as the Russian SFSR team coach. Then master V. Tarasov ran up to me and said, ‘Look what the little Tal is doing! He sacrificed a queen. Incredible!’ We rushed up to the table.”
No. 3
I. Birbrager – M. Tal
1953, Kharkov, 20th Soviet Team Championship, round 5
25...Qxf3
Editor’s note:
Analysis shows that after 25...Bxg4!! 26.Rxg4 Qxf3+ 27.Rg2 Qh3, threatening f4-f3, black gets a decisive attack.
25...Qxf3 26.Nd2 Bxg4!
Editor’s note:
According to MegaDatabase, this capture followed only after the players repeated the position once (26...Qe3 27.Nf1 Qf3 28.Nd2, and only then 28...Bxg4), but we’ll go along with Koblenz’s version. Concerning his exclamation mark for black’s last move, we can say that it was awarded for the pure playing value of Tal’s move, because it poses his opponent serious practical problems. However, from the objective point of view, the queen sacrifice is incorrect.
Black only gets a knight for the queen. However, the white king feels uncomfortable.
27.Nxf3 Bxf3 28.h4
(defending against the threat 28...
R
e5, 29...
N
g3+ and 30...
R
h5#)
28...Rf8 29.Be2
(white could resist more tenaciously with 29.b3).
Editor’s note:
The wording suggests that Koblenz thought that even after the strongest move 29.b3 white’s position would still be difficult. However, computer analysis shows that, with this move, white could get his a2 rook involved in defense and, with precise play, even convert his material advantage to a win. Nevertheless, the game move was the decisive mistake.
29...Ng3+ 30.Kh2 Bxg2 31.Kxg2 Nxe2 32.Qxe2 f3+ 33.Qxf3 Rxf3 34.Kxf3 Rf8+ 35.Kg3 Be5+ 36.Kg2 Bf4 White resigned.
The young player’s sharp combinational vision and his ability to find the most unexpected attacking resources started to garner respect. Even back then, Tal hated passive defense. And when he had to defend, he would use any opportunity to counterattack immediately.
If a young player has good defensive skills, this shows a true chess talent. Tal’s play confirms this rule of thumb.
No. 4
K. Klasups – M. Tal
1952, Riga, Latvian Championship
The young Tal’s tenacity and persistence in defense can be illustrated with numerous examples. Here’s a game from the 1952 Latvian Championship. Trying to prevent the freeing move f7-f6, white played
14.Qb3
There followed
14...f6!
Nevertheless! Black is trying to disrupt the blockade with any means possible. The idea of this counter-attack will become clearer several moves later.
15.Bxe6+ Kh8 16.Ne4
Editor’s note:
16.Na4, with the idea to drive the b6 bishop away or trade it, could give white an advantage. The game move, however, leads to a position where white has to struggle for equality.
16...fxe5 17.Kh1
Editor’s note:
The correct move was 17.f5, for instance, 17...Nf6 18.Nxf6 Bxe6 19.Qxe6 Qxe6 20.fxe6 Rxf6 21.Rxf6 gxf6 22.a4 Re8 23.a5 Bc7 24.e4 Rxe6 25.Be3 a6 26.Bc5 Bd6 27.Rd1 Bxc5+ 28.bxc5 Re7 29.Kf2, and, despite black’s extra pawn, the ending is drawn.
17...exf4 18.exf4 Nf6 19.Ng5 Ne4!
20.Nf7+
White accepts the challenge. After 20.Nxe4 Bxe6, the black bishops will seriously harass his opponent.
Editor’s note:
However, the capture on e4 was a much lesser evil than the game move, which simply loses.
20...Rxf7 21.Bxf7 Nf2+ 22.Kg1 Nd3+ 23.Kh1 Qe2 24.Bb2 Bh3!
How quickly the position has changed! Black’s counter-attack crushes his opponent.
25.Bxg7+
Desperation. After 25.gxh3, there was 25...Qe4+, winning.
25...Kxg7 26.Bd5 cxd5 27.Qxd5 Kh8 28.Qg5
28.gxh3 still didn’t work due to 28...Rg8.
28...Bd4 29.gxh3 Rg8 30.Rae1 Nxe1 White resigned.
And here’s what happened in the 1953 Latvian SSR championship semi-final:
No. 5
J. Mileika – M. Tal
1953, Riga, Latvian Championship
14...Qd7!
This move begins a well thought-out defensive plan. Black takes control over the f5 square. The natural 15.Rfe1 is met by 15...Nxg2 with a dangerous attack.
Editor’s note:
It seems that Koblenz was too optimistic here. Analysis shows that after 15.Rfe1 Nxg2? 16.Kxg2, black can’t create a real attack – for instance, 16...Qh3+ 17.Kg1 Ng4 18.Nf1 Qf3 19.Be3, and white repels all the threats and keeps the material. Perhaps Tal wouldn’t actually have sacrificed the knight and would have chosen some other move instead, accepting that his position is worse and needs protection. In any case, the immediate 14...b6 (Tal only played that on the next move) looks better – it attacks the c5 pawn that cramps black’s position and also intends a7-a5 and Bc8-a6, trading the opponent’s active bishop.
15.f3 b6! 16.cxb6
Editor’s note:
With this move, white loses all the advantage. The correct continuation was 16.Qc1! bxc5 (16...Ng6 17.Bxh6!, and if 17...gxh6 18.Qxh6 Qc6, then white has a pleasant choice between two winning moves: 19.Rae1 with the idea Re1-e5-g5, and 19.Bxg6 fxg6 20.Qxg6+ Kh8 21.Nh5) 17.Bxh6! with a big advantage (black can’t capture the bishop: 17...gxh6? 18.Qxh6 Ng6 19.Nf5! Ne8 20.Rfe1!+–).
16...axb6 17.Rfe1
Editor’s note:
Now the idea Qc2-c1 and Bd2xh6 doesn’t work: 17.Qc1 Ng6! 18.Bxh6 gxh6 19.Qxh6 Qd6!, and black holds (20.Rae1?? Qf4!–+; 20.Bxg6 fxg6 21.Qxg6+ Kh8, and white has nothing decisive, because the f6 knight isn’t pinned).
17...c5 18.Re5 c4 19.Bf5 Qa4 20.Qc1 Nxf5 21.Nxf5 Bxf5 22.Rxf5 Qd7!
Tal provokes his opponent to sacrifice an exchange. A bold decision, because this weakens the black king’s position.
Even back then, despite his youth, Tal was completely sure: to win, you should be ready to take risks.
23.Rxf6 gxf6 24.Bxh6 Rfe8 25.Qf4 Kh7 26.Qh4
26.Qxf6 was met with 26...Rg8 27.Qh4 Rg6! with counterplay. We should note that white could still draw with 26.Bf8! Rxf8 27.Qh4+ Kg6 28.Qg3+ etc.
26...Kg6!
We shouldn’t forget that the king can play an active role in defense as well!
27.g4
Editor’s note:
A mistake. White could still force a draw with the same idea of 27.Bf8 (there are also other equivalent resources) 27...Rxf8 28.Qg3+.
27...Rh8 28.Qh5+ Kh7 29.Kh1 Kg8 30.Rg1 Qe6
Editor’s note:
This decision could have cost Tal half a point. The correct move was 30...Re8!, and after 31.g5 f5 32.g6 f6 33.Qh4 Re6, black’s position is won.
31.g5 f5
A natural reaction. The attacking side is trying to open lines. Black, however, is trying to keep the position closed as much as possible.
32.g6 f6 33.g7
Editor’s note:
The immediate transfer of the queen to the h2-b8 diagonal could save white – 33.Qh4! Rxa2 34.Qf4! (threatening 35.Qb8+; 34.g7? Rh7 is a mistake that transposes into the actual game) 34...Re2 (after 34...Ra8? 35.h4, only black is at risk) 35.g7 Rh7 36.Qb8+ Qe8 37.Qd6! Rxh6 38.Qxd5+ Qe6 (38...Re6? 39.Re1) 39.Qa8+ Qe8 40.Qd5+ with perpetual check.
33...Rh7
(white’s attack fizzles out)
34.Qh4 Rxa2 35.Qg3 Re2 36.Ra1 Qe8 37.Qh4 Re6
, and
white resigned
after a few more moves.
The game below is a great example of the young Tal’s dynamic style.
No. 6, Sicilian Defense
M. Tal – M. Pasman
1953, Riga, Latvian Championship
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 d6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 Nf6 5.Nc3 a6 6.f4
This active move was fashionable back then, but is now quite rare.
6...e5 7.Nf3 Nbd7 8.Bd3 Be7 9.0-0 0-0 10.Kh1 b5 11.a3 Qc7 12.fxe5 dxe5 13.Nh4
13...Nc5
Editor’s note:
In a modern grandmaster game J. Geller – B. Savchenko, Moscow 2011, black preferred 13...Nb6!?, covering the d5 square and intending to put the knight on c4 at some point.
14.Bg5
Until now, the game had followed theory. Tal played his first moves quickly – the opening studies paid off. White has an open f-file, his pieces are concentrated on the kingside, and the strategic plan is clear: attack the king’s position.
14...Qd8 15.Nf5 Bxf5 16.Rxf5 Nfd7 17.Bxe7 Qxe7 18.Nd5 Qd6 19.Qg4
Starting a multi-move combination with a sacrifice of two pieces. This game brilliantly shows the young player’s exceptional ability to create incredible combination complications. The difficulty of the combination lies in the fact that the following play isn’t forced: white methodically attacks while being two pieces down. One of Tal’s greatest strengths first manifested itself in this game. Even if his opponent gets counterplay, he rarely loses the initiative, finding more and more targets for attack. He attacks incessantly, creating one threat after the other.
19...g6
Editor’s note:
Black could defer this committal move for a while, choosing, for instance, 19...Rac8 instead, activating the rook.
20.Raf1 f6 21.h4
Editor’s note:
As we shall see, had black played correctly, such a sharp continuation could have come back to hit white like a boomerang. 21.R5f3 was a good move, with the idea of Qg4-h4.
21...Kh8 22.R5f3
Editor’s note:
The computer insists on 22.R5f2 f5 23.Qe2, going into passive defense, but Tal obviously didn’t play 21.h4 for that.
22...f5
White, of course, saw this counter-strike.
23.exf5! Qxd5
After 23...gxf5, white had 24.Bxf5 Qxd5 25.Bxh7!
Editor’s note:
The computer continues this line with a hard-to-find move 25...Qc4, and since 26.Qh5 loses to 26...Qxf1+ 27.Rxf1 Rxf1+ 28.Kh2 Nf6, white has to trade queens and go into an endgame materially down.
24.fxg6 Rxf3 25.g7+ Kg8 26.Bxh7+
The d3 bishop has been “revived” after great effort from white. However, to destroy the opposing king’s position, this bishop needs to be sacrificed.
26...Kxh7 27.Rxf3 Ne4!
Editor’s note:
27...Qe6! is even stronger, preventing white from pushing the h-pawn.
28.h5 Ndf6 29.Qg6+ Kg8 30.h6
White’s combination ends here. To save the game, black needs to play just one correct move
30...Ra7
The decisive mistake; it was hard for black to foresee all the consequences. He could have drawn with 30...Nh7! 31.Rf8+ Nxf8 32.gxf8=Q+ Kxf8 33.Qg7+ Ke8 34.h7 Qd1+ 35.Kh2 Qh5+ etc.
Editor’s note:
Actually, black’s position is won after 30...Ra7, and he had other good resources as well, such as 30...Rd8–+ or 30...Rc8–+.
31.Kh2!
This quiet move begins a hidden maneuver.
31...Re7
Editor’s note:
Only this move is the losing mistake. After 31...Qd2! black repelled the attack and won, because 32.Rh3 is refuted by 32...Qf4+ 33.g3 (33.Kg1 Ng5–+) 33...Qf2+ 34.Kh1 Ng5! 35.Qxg5 Rd7 36.Qc1 Rd2 with a mating counter-attack, while after 32.h7+ Nxh7 33.Qxe4 Qd6, black simply gets a decisive material advantage.
32.Rh3! Nh7 33.Rd3 Qa8 34.Qxe4!
That’s it! After the check on d8, white promotes a new queen and pushes the black king to the center of the board.
34...Qxe4 35.Rd8+ Kf7 36.g8=Q+ Kf6 37.Rd6+ Kf5 38.Qg6+ Kf4 39.g3+ Ke3 40.Rd3+ Qxd3 41.Qxd3+ Black resigned.
Leningrad, autumn 1953. The strongest of the strongest arrived for the national team championship. Once the Latvian team had sat down at their tables, the Lithuanian master I. Vistaneckis approached me.
“Listen, Koblenz, did you bring the republic’s first team or a schoolboy team? In the autumn, the boys should be studying, not traveling!”
Indeed, with the exception of I. Zhdanov and myself, all the players from Latvia were younger than 20!
And still, the decision of the Latvian Chess Federation to send young players to an important competition was correct. To become a strong chess player, you should face serious tests. The 1953 Soviet team championship turned out to be just such a test for many currently well-known players.
The dangerous-looking experiment turned out alright. The young Latvian players – Janis Klavins, Mark Pasman, Israel Zilber, Aivars Gipslis and Mikhail Tal (the latter two still looked like kids) – were at the center of attention. The “schoolboys” got ahead of many experienced opponents and qualified for the final.
The match against the Russian SFSR was the most exciting. The result of the 5-hour battle was sensational: Latvia won 5-4. There were some chances to take third place. The result of the adjourned game between candidate master Tal and international master G. Ilivitsky was very important (see diagram).
No. 7
M. Tal – G. Ilivitsky
1953, Leningrad, 3rd Soviet Team Championship
Here’s how the game ended.
46.Ke3! Rxg3+ 47.Kf4 Rg2 48.c4 Rc2
48...Kc5 was of course bad due to 49.g6+ and 50.Rg5.
Editor’s note:
Black could have drawn, for instance, with 48...e5+ 49.Kf5 (49.Ke4 Rg4+ 50.Kf5 Rxc4 51.g6 Rf4+ 52.Kg5 Ke7; 49.Ke3 Rg4) 49...Rf2+ 50.Kg6 e4; 48...Rg1 or 48...Rf2+ 49.Kg3 Rf1 was also enough.
49.g6 Ke7 50.c5 Kf6 51.Rg5!
The winning move.
51...e5+ 52.Ke4 Kg7
52...Kxg5 53.g7
53.Kd5 Black resigned.
Tal played second board in the competition and scored 4.5/7. For this success, the Highest Qualifying Commission decided to allow candidate master M. Tal to play a match for the master’s title.
The Leningrad competition made Tal more confident in his abilities. He prepared for that match very seriously. The first stage of preparation was the 1954 Latvian SSR championship. During the draw, Janis Klovans asked the other participants to uphold an old tradition. The fact of the matter was, that in the last three years the championship had been won each time by a first-year student of the Latvian University, and Klovans had just enrolled...
The tradition wasn’t broken. Klovans played brilliantly and won the Latvian championship. Tal had to settle for 2nd–3rd place.
In the summer, Tal played a qualifying match against the multiple champion of the Belorussian SSR, experienced master V. Saigin. There were 14 games in the match, and the Riga player won 8-6. For this victory, the young player was awarded the honored title “USSR Master of Sports”.
Game 8 was Tal’s best creative achievement in the match.
No. 8, Catalan Opening
V. Saigin – M. Tal
1954, Riga, Master Title Match, game 8
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 c5 3.Nf3 e6 4.g3 cxd4 5.Nxd4 d5 6.Bg2 e5 7.Nf3 d4!
White can’t capture on e5 due to 8...Qa5+, and the advanced pawn disrupts communication lines between the flanks.
8.0-0 Nc6 9.e3 Be7 10.exd4 exd4 11.Nbd2
Editor’s note:
In modern times, the main line is 11.Bf4.
11...Be6 12.Re1 0-0 13.b3 Qd7 14.Bb2 Rad8 15.a3 a5 16.Ne5 Nxe5 17.Rxe5 b6 18.Nf3 Bc5
19.Qd2
After 19.b4, Tal intended to play 19...axb4 20.axb4 Bd6!, and the white rook has no good squares: 21.Re2 is met with 21...Bxc4, while after 21.Re1, there’s 21...Bxb4.
19...Ng4
After bolstering the center, black goes on the offensive.
20.Ree1 d3!
(opening the diagonal for the c5 bishop)
21.Rf1
After 21.Ne5, black intended to play 21...Nxe5 22.Rxe5 Bd4! After exchanging the bishops, his passed pawn will become dangerous.
21...Qd6!
This move should have been anticipated beforehand. Black parries two threats at the same time – 22.b3-b4 and 22.h2-h3. If now 22.h3, then 22...Nxf2! 23.Rxf2 Qxg3.
Editor’s note:
Koblenz’s line leads to unclear play after 24.Be5 Qg6 25.Kh2 Bxf2 26.Qxf2, so white should have gone for this position.
22.Qc3
Editor’s note:
This move is a serious mistake, because it allows black to create a strong initiative. 22.Bc3 keeps the position equal.
22...f6 23.Rad1
White couldn’t play 23.Ng5 due to 23...Nxf2! 24.Rxf2 Bxf2+ 25.Kxf2 fxg5+
Editor’s note:
White still had some defending chances after 23.Bc1!, intending Bd2 or Bf4.
23...Rfe8 24.Rd2 Bf5 25.Ng5
25...Ne3!
(the decisive blow)
26.fxe3
If the rook retreated, black traded the g2 bishop – an important kingside defender.
26...Bxe3+ 27.Kh1 Bxd2 28.Qxd2 Re2 29.Qc3 Rxg2! White resigned.
If the rook is captured, black wins by pushing the d-pawn – 30.Kxg2 d2 31.Rd1 Bg4 32.Nf3 Qd3!
However, Tal’s play wasn’t always so consistent. The desire to complicate the position and create conditions for some kind of combination once cost him an important point.
No. 9
M. Tal – V. Saigin
1954, Riga, Master Title Match, game 11
White had a simple win: 40.Nxh5 Rxh5 41.Bd6! Because of the h5 rook’s poor position and the decisive weakening of the kingside, black would just have to resign. Instead, Tal went for a complicated combination, but the spectacular finishing move turned out to be impossible!
40.Rd7 gxf4 41.Qd5+ Rae6 42.Qc5 Reg6 43.Rxc7
Editor’s note:
White could have forced a draw by repetition with 43.Qd5+ Re6 44.Qc5.
43...Rxg2+ 44.Kf1 Qd8!
Black threatens to turn the game around with 45...Be2+ 46.Kxe2 Rxf2+. Tal thought that after 45.Qf8+ Qxf8 46.Bxf8 Rhg6, he would force his opponent’s resignation with the elegant 47.Bg5! When he started the combination, he simply forgot that the bishop couldn’t move to g5 from f8!
The ending was miserable:
45.Qd5+ Qxd5 46.Rc8+ Kf7 47.Rc7+ Ke8
, and
white resigned.
Tal remembered this loss for a long while.
In this period, Tal’s number-one enemy was hasty decision-making, so characteristic of young players.
No. 10
M. Tal – J. Klovans
1954, Riga
Black is in zugzwang. After 22.g3
or 22.Re3 and Rf3 he should lose.
Editor’s note:
Actually, black is not in zugzwang just yet (right now, only the e7 bishop cannot move), but after the strongest 22.Nc6! (now all black pieces are tied down with the bishop’s defense), he gets very close to it and loses quickly: 22...h5 (after 22...Qd6 23.g4 Qf6 24.g5 Qd6 25.Re3 it is indeed zugzwang) 23.h4 Qd6 24.g3 Qf6 25.Re3 Qd6 26.Kg2 Qf6 27.Rf3 Qg7 28.Re6 f6 29.Ne5+–.
However, Tal, almost without thinking, played
22.Qc6?
After
22...Red8 23.Qe4 Bd6
(
Editor’s note:
23...Bxb4, winning a pawn, is even stronger – it’s now white who should struggle for a draw)
24.Re8+ Kg7 25.Rxd8 Rxd8 26.Nc6
, white still has an advantage, but he needed a much bigger effort to convert it than in the original position.
Editor’s note:
Koblenz’s evaluation looks too optimistic. The game continued
26...Rd7 27.c4
(best)
27...bxc4
(the immediate 27...Bf8! is even better)
28.Qxc4
, and instead of
28...Qf4
(after which white doesn’t have any advantage either, by the way), black could play 28...Bf8! with the idea of Rd7-d2, seizing the initiative as a result. The game ended in a
draw
after 50 moves.
Back then, Tal often played in blitz tournaments, which, of course, affected his game. Endless blitz games are one of the reasons for “automatic” rashness in important games. Tal occasionally couldn’t stand the intense struggle and tried to force events.
“Automatic play” is especially dangerous in better and won positions. Emanuel Lasker, the ex-world champion, would say, “A won position is the hardest to win.” The fact that Tal managed to overcome his impatience and nervousness in a relatively short time raised my hopes.
Filled with battle fervor, Tal, as the leader of the republic’s youth team, travelled to Leningrad for the USSR youth championship. He came second in individual scores (after B. Spassky).
No. 11
M. Tal – J. Visockis
1954, Leningrad, Soviet Junior Team Championship, Latvia-Lithuania Match
21.Nd5
Editor’s note:
This move is actually bad and could have led to a worse position for white.
21...cxd5 22.Qxd5+ Nf7 23.Qxa8 Bc6 24.Bb6!
The point of the combination. Tal left his queen deep behind enemy lines for a purpose!
24...axb6 25.Rc1 Bxa8
(black could resist better with 25...
B
f8)
Editor’s note:
Actually, after Koblenz’s move 25...Bf8, black is simply better. For instance: 26.Bb5 Bxa8 27.Rxc7 Ncd6 28.Bd3 g4!, seizing the initiative.
26.Rxc7 Rc6
Editor’s note:
Black shouldn’t have gone for the rook exchange, because after it, his counterplay chances are close to zero. 26...Ncd6 was much better, planning kingside activity after g5-g4 at some point.
27.Rc1 Rxc7 28.Rxc7 Ncd6 29.Nd2! Bf8 30.Bc4
, and
white won
, converting his advantage.
In autumn 1954, the Soviet team championship was held in Riga. In this competition, Tal played against a grandmaster for the very first time. He was pitted against Yuri Averbakh, the current Soviet champion, in the match Daugava – Zenit.
The game was very tense. Tal played white and sacrificed a pawn in the opening. His opponent found the correct defensive plan and seized the initiative. It seemed that Tal would definitely lose. However, Averbakh made a blunder in time trouble, and the position changed drastically. The grandmaster couldn’t make his 40th time-control move and lost on time in a roughly equal position.
Tal was, of course, glad that he had brought a valuable point to his team, but on the way home, he was downcast. This didn’t feel like a true victory. Trying to seize the initiative out of the opening with dubious gambit lines was an unreliable method, especially against grandmasters.
How should he play against grandmasters? This question bothered him a lot. And so, against Taimanov, Tal decided to play differently, “the grandmaster way” – quietly and methodically. This ended badly – Taimanov scored an easy win. It turned out that tactical inventiveness alone wasn’t enough, and in a positional game Tal was still much weaker than the top players.
His sporting results in this competition weren’t brilliant: 4/10, sharing next-to-last place on first board. However, his opponents were also much stronger than those he had faced before.
The game against international master Lev Aronin especially attracted my attention. Even though Tal lost, I think that the exceptional talent of the future grandmaster really shone through in this game.
No. 12, Caro-Kann Defense
M. Tal – L. Aronin
1954, Riga, 2nd Soviet Team Cup, round 6
1.e4 c6 2.Nc3 d5 3.Nf3 dxe4 4.Nxe4 Nf6 5.Nxf6+ gxf6
(a double-edged continuation. After 5...exf6, the game simplified considerably)
6.Bc4
Editor’s note:
These days, 6.d4 is by far the most popular move.
6...Qc7
Editor’s note:
This move occurs rarely in current practice. Black plays 6...Bf5 more often.
7.0-0 Bg4 8.d4 e6 9.Re1 Nd7 10.h3 Bh5
11.Bxe6!
Launching a combination with a rook and knight sacrifice.
Editor’s note:
This sacrifice is objectively dubious.
11...fxe6 12.Rxe6+ Kf7
If 12...Kd8, then 13.Ng5! Bg6 (13...Bxd1 is met with 14.Nf7+ Kc8 15.Re8+) 14.Rxf6!
Editor’s note:
The correct move is 12...Be7 – after 13.Qe1 Nf8 14.Rxf6 Bxf3 (but not 14...0-0-0? 15.Bf4) 15.Rxf3 Ng6 16.Qe6 Rf8 17.Rxf8+ Nxf8 18.Qg4 Bf6 (there are other good moves as well), black is out of danger and at the very least no worse.
13.Ng5+ fxg5 14.Qxh5+ Kxe6 15.Bxg5 Ne5! 16.Re1 Qg7
17.Qg4+?
A pity! After the game, Tal showed that white could maintain the attack with 17.dxe5! For instance, 17...Rg8 (best) 18.Qg4+ Kf7 19.Qd7+ Kg6 20.Qe6+ Kxg5 21.Re4 with the threat 22.Rg4. If 21...Kh5, then 22.Qf5+ Qg5 23.Qxh7+ Bh6 24.Qf7+ and Qf3+.
Tal decided against the move because he thought it wasn’t enough to win. However, black now repels the attack.
Editor’s note:
After 17.dxe5, instead of Koblenz’s move 17...Rg8?!, black could have put up much stiffer resistance with 17...Be7 – even though the position after 18.Bf6! Qg6 19.Qf3 Rhd8 20.Qb3+ Rd5 21.Qxb7 Qe8 22.Bxe7 Rb8 23.Qxa7 Ra8 24.Qb7 Rb8 25.Qa6 Kxe7 26.e6, where white has five pawns for the rook, a dangerous passed e-pawn and an attack, is evaluated as lost for black by the computer, it’s still unclear for a human. However, instead of 17.dxe5, white had a good alternative 17.Rxe5+, and after 17...Kd7 (or 17...Kd6 18.Bf4 Kd7 19.Rg5 Qxd4 20.Qg4+ Ke8 21.Re5+ Be7 22.Qg7+–) 18.Qg4+ Kc7 19.Qg3 Kd7 (19...Kb6 20.Qb3+ Kc7 21.Rf5!+–) 20.Qb3 b5 (20...Kc7 21.Rf5!+–) 21.Qe6+ Kc7 22.Bf4 Qd7 (22...Kb7 23.d5!+–; 22...Kb6 23.c4! bxc4 24.a4+–) 23.Rd5+ Bd6 24.Qxd7+ Kxd7 25.Rxd6+ Kc7 26.Be5 Rhe8 27.Rh6+ Kb6 28.Rxh7 white obviously wins.
17...Kd6!
Aronin defends precisely. After 17...Kf7 18.Rxe5 Kg8 19.Qh4 white maintained dangerous threats.
Editor’s note:
Actually, black’s move is a mistake. 17...Kf7 was the correct move, even though after 18.dxe5!? (Koblenz’s line 18.Rxe5 Kg8 19.Qh4 leads to threefold repetition after 19...Qg6 20.Bf6 h6 21.Re3 Kh7 22.Rg3 Qf5 23.Rf3 Qg6) 18...Be7 19.Qf3+ Ke8 20.Bf6 Qf8 21.Bxh8 Qxh8 22.Qg4, white has the initiative, and his next plan involves activitating the rook along the third rank.
18.dxe5+
Editor’s note:
After 18.Rxe5! with threats 19.Qe6+/19.Qf4/19.Qg3/19.Rf5 white won. For instance: 18...Qg6 19.c4 (intending 20.Re7 Bxe7 21.Bf4#) 19...Kc7 20.Re6 Qf7 21.Bf6 Rg8 22.Qf5 Qd7 23.Be5+ Kd8 24.c5 (threatening 25.Bf6+) 24...Be7 25.Bf6! Bxf6 26.Rd6 Qxd6 27.cxd6 Rf8 28.g4 h6 29.h4 Rc8 30.g5 hxg5 31.hxg5 Bg7 32.Qh7+–.
18...Kc7 19.e6 Rg8! 20.Bf4+ Bd6 21.Bxd6+ Kxd6 22.Rd1+ Kc7 23.Qf4+ Kc8 24.g4 Rd8! 25.Re1 Qc7
Editor’s note:
The correct move was 25...Qe7. After the game move, however, white should have played 26.Qf6! with the subsequent e6-e7, f2-f4 and, according to the computer, he has enough compensation for a draw.
26.Qf5 Qe7
The attack has fizzled out, and
black won
, converting his material advantage.
Tal’s next tournament was the 1955 Latvian SSR championship.
“A tradition is a tradition” – a Latvian University freshman should win. That year, it was Aivars Gipslis. Tal finished second...
In the same year of 1955, Tal achieved his first serious success in All-Union competitions. In the Soviet championship quarter-final in Vilnius (the Baltic zone tournament), he shared 3rd–4th place and qualified for the semi-final.
No. 13
A. Gipslis – M. Tal
1955, Vilnius, Soviet Championship Quarter-Final
Tal played his first games without much effort, and the results showed. By the middle of the tournament, his chances of qualifying for the semi-final were minimal. However, at this critical moment, the young master suddenly transformed. He treated every game with the utmost importance. Showing great composure and tenacity, he defeated some serious opponents and finished among the prize winners.
44...h3 45.Kg1 e4 46.a6!
White’s best counter-chance is to create a passed pawn. 46.fxe4+ Kg4 47.a6 was worse due to 47...f3 48.axb7 f2+ 49.Kf1 h2 50.Rg3+ Kh4!, and the h-pawn is unstoppable.
46...e3 47.axb7
The strongest reply was 47.Rc1, which black intended to meet with 47...Rxb3 48.axb7 Rxb7 49.c6 Rc7, winning.
47...Rb1+ 48.Kh2 e2 49.Re3
49.b8=Q is bad due to 49...Rh1+
49...fxe3 50.b8=Q Rh1+!
With this tactical blow, black wins the game.
51.Kxh1 e1=Q+ 52.Kh2 Qf2+ 53.Kxh3 Qxf3+ 54.Kh2 e2 55.Qf8+ Ke4 56.Qe8+ Kd3 57.Qb5+ Ke3 White resigned.
A complicated position occurred in Tal’s game against Karlis Klasups, who also competed for a top place in the tournament.
No. 14
K. Klasups – M. Tal
1955, Vilnius, Soviet Championship Quarter-Final
15...Bd7!
(avoiding the rook exchange)
16.Rac1 Nh5 17.Ne4 Ne6 18.Nd6
It was hard to predict that after winning a pawn on the queenside, white would have to withstand an attacking storm on the opposite flank. It was better to double rooks on the d-file.
Editor’s note:
Actually, an attempt to double the rooks leads to a difficult position for white: 18.Rc2? (the moves with the other rook lose immediately: 18.Rd2?? f5 19.Nc3 e4 20.Nd4 f4 21.gxf4 Nexf4–+, 18.Rd3?? f5 19.Neg5 Nf8!–+, 18.Rd6?? f5–+) 18.Rc2? f5 19.Nc3 (19.Nd6? e4 20.Nh2 f4 with a strong and probably decisive attack) 19...Be8 (threatening 20...e4) 20.Rcd2 Rxd2 21.Bxd2 (21.Rxd2?? f4–+) 21...e4 22.Nh2 Rd8 with a great positional advantage.
18...b6 19.c5 Rab8 20.cxb6 axb6 21.Nc4 e4 22.Nfd2
Editor’s note:
22.Nd4 (threatening 23.Nxe6 Bxe6 24.Nxb6) was safer: after the trades on d4, the position is equal, and if 22...Nc7?!, then after 23.g4! c5 (23...Nf6 24.Bf4 with the initiative) 24.gxh5 cxd4 25.Bxd4 Bxd4 26.Rxd4 white is better.
22...b5 23.Na5 f5! 24.Nxc6 Bxc6 25.Rxc6 f4! 26.gxf4 Nexf4 27.Nf1 Qg5 28.Rxd8+ Rxd8
29.Bxf4
Editor’s note:
29.Ng3 could have saved the game — after the subsequent 29...Nxg3 30.Bxf4 (30.fxg3 Nxh3+ 31.Bxh3 Qxe3+ 32.Kg2 doesn’t lose either) 30...Nxe2+ 31.Qxe2 Qxf4 32.Qg4 Qxg4 33.hxg4, there’s a drawn endgame on the board.
29...Nxf4 30.Ng3 e3! 31.Rc2
31.fxe3 would’ve been met with 31...Nxg2 32.Kxg2 Qd5+
31...exf2+ 32.Kxf2
32.Qxf2 doesn’t help due to 32...Bd4 33.e3 Bxe3 34.Qxe3 Nxh3+.
32...Bd4+ 33.Kf1 Nh5! 34.Bf3
after 34.Nxh5, black wins with 34...Rf8+
34...Nxg3+ 35.Kg2 Ne4+ 36.Bg4 h5 37.e3 Bxe3 38.Qb4 Qe5 39.Qe1 Bd2 40.Qh4 g5
The time trouble ended, and further resistance was futile.
White resigned.
At the Soviet team championship in Lugansk I worked as Latvia’s coach. I arrived in the city at 3 in the morning, but Misha wasn’t in his room. I found him in the room where the Ukrainian players lived. A blitz tournament had just ended. It was easy to see by my facial expression that the coach wasn’t exactly thrilled by the exhausting night competition.
Tal was very lively. Holding a sheet of paper and a pencil in his hand, he asked with a smile, “Maestro, can I enroll you in the next blitz tournament?”
However, his opponents were already out of energy.
“Maybe it’s time to stop, Misha? The games are tomorrow, we should catch some sleep.”
I shared a room with Tal in Lugansk. Each day in the morning, and sometimes in the evening as well, other players from the team gathered in our room. The game analyses were endless.
Tal obviously looked tired, and I asked the hotel administrator to move him to a single room. But the young master didn’t even want to hear about that. He wanted to take part in the team work and help his friends. When team interests were at stake, he didn’t think about himself.
No. 15
I. Lipnitsky – M. Tal
1955, Lugansk, 4th Soviet Team Championship Semi-Final
Tal played on the second board and shared 1st–3rd place in the semi-final.
He saved the game against Isaak Lipnitsky with an exceptionally tenacious defense.
In his annotations to the game, Lipnitsky wrote, “The entire Ukrainian team analyzed the adjourned position. To win, we decided to send the white king to the kingside, towards the h-pawn, or prevent the black king from reaching the g6 square. For instance, 45...Kd5 46.Be3 Bb8 47.Bf4 Ba7 48.b8=Q Bxb8 49.Bxb8. If now 49...Ke6, then 50.Kf4 Kf6 51.Be5+ Kg6 52.Bb2, and the game is won. (
Editor’s note:
Instead of 49...Ke6, black should play 49...a3, and after 50.Kf4 Kc4 51.Be5 Kd3! 52.g4 Ke2 53.g5 a2 54.Kg4 Ke3 55.Kh5 Kf3 56.Kh6 Kg4 57.Bf6 Kf5, it’s a draw.)
45...Kd7!
The Latvian players also did great work! Now white can’t win a tempo with 46.Be3 Bb8 47.Bf4, because after 47...Bxf4 48.gxf4, the b7 pawn can’t be defended.
46.Bf4 a3 47.b8=Q Bxb8 48.Bxb8 Ke6 49.Kf4 Kf6
That’s the point behind the subtle move 45...Kd7! If the black pawn were on a4, white would win easily after 50.Bd6.
50.Be5+ Kg6 51.Ba1 a2 52.Bb2 h5 53.Ba1 Kh6
If 54.Kf5, then 54...h4! 55.gxh4 Kh5, with a draw. So, a
draw was agreed
.”
Tal’s best achievement in Lugansk was his win against the young Moscow champion, Evgeny Vasiukov.
In this game, white’s play was very precise and determined.
No. 16, Sicilian Defense
M. Tal – E. Vasiukov
1955, Lugansk, 4th Soviet Team Championship Final, round 9
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 g6 5.c4 Nf6 6.Nc3 Nxd4 7.Qxd4 d6 8.Be2 Bg7
9.0-0
Editor’s note:
In modern top-level games, white usually doesn’t hurry with castling: the current main line is 9.Be3 0-0 10.Qd2 Be6 11.Rc1 Qa5 12.f3 Rfc8 13.b3 a6 14.Na4 Qxd2+ 15.Kxd2.
9...0-0 10.Qd1
Editor’s note:
This decision to return the queen to the starting square didn’t find many followers among strong players. The most popular moves in subsequent grandmaster practice were 10.Qe3, 10.Qd3, and 10.Bg5.
10...Be6 11.Be3 Qa5
Both sides have completed their development. White controls the central squares and has some positional advantage. Black’s goal is to prepare a queenside attack.
12.Rc1 Rfc8 13.b3 a6 14.f4!
White needs to be energetic, otherwise black will be the first to launch an attack with b7-b5.
14...Ng4
Inventive, but leads to white’s advantage. It was better not to deviate from the marked out plan and play 14...b5.
Editor’s note:
Koblenz’s move 14...b5 is also a suboptimal decision because of the reply 15.f5!. After 15...Bd7 (perhaps 15...gxf5 16.exf5 Bd7 is the lesser evil, even though black’s position is still very suspect) 16.fxg6 hxg6 17.e5! dxe5 18.Rxf6 and 19.Qxd7 (which has happened several times in practice), white has a big advantage. However, 14...Bg4!? is worth considering, as played in the game Haznedaroglu – Malakhov, Tripoli 2004. There followed 15.Bxg4 Nxg4 16.Qxg4 Bxc3 with approximate equality.
15.Bd4 Ne3
Editor’s note:
Perhaps it was better to go for passive defense with 15...Nh6!?, intending to trade on d4 and then play f7-f6 and Nh6-f7.
16.Bxe3 Bxc3 17.f5 Bd7 18.c5 Rxc5
The exchange sacrifice was evidently forced. Black is assuming that the pawn and bishop pair are adequate compensation for the small material deficit. Actually, this is not true.
Editor’s note:
Black could play 18...Bb5, holding onto the material for a while, but after 19.Bxb5 axb5 20.Qd5, black’s position is difficult.
19.Bxc5 Qxc5+ 20.Kh1 Qa5 21.Bc4 Rf8 22.fxg6 hxg6 23.Qd5! b5
Editor’s note:
23...Bc6 still gave some chances to save the game.
24.Rxf7 bxc4 25.Rcf1! Rc8 26.Rf8+ Kh7 27.Qg8+ Kh6 28.Rxc8 Bxc8 29.Qxc8 cxb3 30.axb3 Bf6 31.Qf8+ Kh7 32.Qf7+ Kh6 33.Qd5! Qa2 34.Qd3 a5 35.Qc4
White improves his queen’s position and goes for the adjournment, hoping to find a precise path to the win during home analysis.
35...Kg7 36.g3 Qb2 37.Qd3 Qa2 38.Rc1 Qf2 39.Rf1 Qa2 40.Qf3 Qc2 41.Rf2
The game was adjourned here. In the resumption, there followed
41...Qb1+ 42.Kg2 Kf7 43.h4! Ke8 44.h5! g5 45.h6 Kf8 46.Qf5 Qxb3
(desperation)
47.Qc8+ Kf7 48.h7
, and
black resigned.
The semi-final of the 23rd Soviet championship was approaching. It was a very difficult exam. Playing in the final is every master’s dream, so the semi-finals are true battles to the death. After the draw, Tal thought about the tournament table for a long time: there were very experienced players with deep theoretical knowledge among the Riga semi-final participants. These included grandmaster Viktor Korchnoi, the winner of the Bucharest international tournament, grandmaster Isaak Boleslavsky, and international master Semyon Furman, one of our best theoreticians.
Tal achieved a good start – 4.5/7. Then he won 4 games in a row and joined the leaders’ group. Like in the Vilnius quarter-final, his play in the second half of the tournament was most inspired, and he finished first.
He had reached the final!
Here’s one of the games from the Riga semi-final.
No. 17, Sicilian Defense
M. Tal – S. Furman
1955, Riga, 23rd Soviet Championship Semi-Final
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 Nf6 5.Nc3 d6 6.Bg5 e6 7.Qd2 a6 8.0-0-0 Bd7 9.f4 Rc8
Editor’s note:
This seems to be a bad move. In modern grandmaster practice, black usually plays 9...b5, 9...h6 or 9...Be7.
10.Nf3 Qa5
By leaving the king in the center, black hopes to organize a kingside attack. White prepares a central breakthrough.
11.Kb1!
Before the attack, white makes a necessary prophylactic move. Had he played 11.e5 immediately, black would have counterattacked with 11...Nb4 12.exf6 Rxc3 13.Nd4 Ra3!.
11...b5 12.Bxf6
Editor’s note:
As a number of subsequent games showed, the move 12.e5 poses a lot of problems for the opponent. Play usually continued 12...b4 (perhaps 12...Nd5 13.Nxd5 Qxd2 14.Rxd2 exd5 is the lesser evil, even though after 15.a4, black’s position is quite unpleasant) 13.exf6 bxc3 14.fxg7 Bxg7 15.Qxd6 with a big advantage.
12...gxf6 13.f5 Nb4 14.a3 Nc6 15.Nd4 Nxd4
An inaccuracy. Black should have played 15...b4. For instance, 16.Nxc6 Bxc6 17.axb4 Qxb4 18.Qd4 Rb8! 19.b3 a5 (
Editor’s note:
this move loses to 20.Qxf6, so the correct continuation is 19...Qxd4 20.Rxd4 a5=).
16.Qxd4 Be7 17.Be2 Kd8
White threatened 18.Bh5. Perhaps 17...0-0 was better, which could be met with 18.Rd3 Kh8 19.Rh3, with dangerous threats.
Furman hopes to find a shelter for his king in the center, but his position quickly deteriorates.
18.Rhf1 Rb8?
Tal intended to meet 18...Rg8 with 19.fxe6 fxe6 20.Rxf6 Rxg2 21.Rf7 Rxc3 22.Rxe7 Rc6 23.Rxh7 Rxe2 24.Rh8+! Be8 25.Qa7 (
Editor’s note:
25.Qf6+ wins immediately) 25...Qc7 26.Qa8+ Qc8 27.Rxd6+! Ke7 28.Rxe8+ Qxe8 29.Qxc6 Qxc6 30.Rxc6, and the rook ending is hopeless for black.
Tal demonstrated this difficult line right after the game.
19.fxe6 fxe6 20.Rxf6 b4
After 20...Bxf6, white had 21.Qxf6+ Kc7 22.e5! d5 23.Nxd5+ exd5 24.Qd6+ Kc8 25.Rxd5 Rb7 26.Rc5+ Rc7 27.b4!, winning easily.
21.Rf5 e5
An exceptionally complicated position. Three white pieces are en prise, but he still manages to obtain a decisive advantage.
22.Qa7 Qb6
After 22...Rc8, black could’ve been checkmated: 23.Nd5! bxa3 24.b4 Qa4 25.Qb6+ Ke8 26.Bh5#.
23.Qxb6+ Rxb6 24.Nd5 Bxf5 25.exf5!
The rest is simple:
25...Rb8 26.f6 Bf8 27.axb4 Kd7 28.g4 Rg8 29.h4 h6 30.Rg1 e4 31.g5 hxg5 32.hxg5 Ke6 33.Bc4 Ke5 34.g6 Bh6 35.Ne7 Rgd8 36.g7
, and
black resigned.
The years of hard work paid off.
Now it was time to think about preparing for games against the strongest grandmasters, coming up with the right strategy for a competition which would attract the attention of millions of chess fans.
The First Final
And so, we’re in Leningrad. The debutant of the 23rd Soviet chess championship is all excited. He can’t wait until the moment he’s finally able to play against the renowned grandmasters and masters.
Tal played at ease and quickly in this important competition. He never got into time trouble. While his opponents were calculating complicated lines, he walked around the stage, looking at the unfolding events in other games with interest.
Meanwhile, there was a lot of drama in his own games. Tal crossed the safety barrier far too often, and was close to losing on many occasions. For instance, Byvshev won two pawns against him, but overlooked his opponent’s disguised maneuver in time trouble, and the Riga player forced a perpetual check. In the first-round game against the Moscow master Vladimir Antoshin, Tal got into a difficult position. The young master even got a lot of flak over his wins against Vyacheslav Ragozin and Anatoly Bannik.
“An accidental success,” some pundits quickly said of Tal’s results.
However, we shouldn’t forget that, unlike pure analysis, chess struggle has many factors – psychological considerations, time trouble, a loss of concentration because of nervous strain, etc. The fact that Tal managed to use the smallest chances and maintain his composure in difficult and sometimes even hopeless positions only speaks in his favor.
Let’s remember that the young Botvinnik heard the same kind of criticism. For instance,
Shakhmaty v SSSR
wrote in 1936: “It’s hard to find a master who’d managed to save as many lost and even hopeless positions as Botvinnik.”
Tal’s game against Vladimir Simagin was very intense. White sacrificed a knight, then a rook. Simagin had to make thirteen moves with his king, with danger lurking everywhere.
At the end of the game, the Leningrad chess fans met Tal with an ovation. With their applause, they thanked the Riga master for the true aesthetic pleasure brought by this work of chess art.
No. 18, Pirc Defence
M. Tal – V. Simagin
1956, Leningrad, 23rd Soviet Championship, round 3
1.e4 c6 2.d4 d6
Entering the players’ room, the international master joked: “I lacked enough strength to push the pawn one square further.”
The move 2...d6 is, of course, entirely playable and was made by black to avoid the trodden paths.
3.Nc3 Nf6 4.f4! Qb6
Editor’s note:
Today, the most popular move by far is 4...Qa5.
5.Nf3 Bg4 6.Be2 Nbd7
Editor’s note:
In modern times, top players as a rule prefer 6...Bxf3 7.Bxf3 e5.
7.e5! Nd5 8.0-0 Nxc3 9.bxc3
9...e6
An error. It was necessary to eliminate the dangerous knight with 9...Bxf3.
10.Ng5! Bxe2 11.Qxe2 h6
(black provokes his opponent to sacrifice a knight)
12.Nxf7! Kxf7 13.f5 dxe5
Editor’s note:
13...Nxe5 is met with 14.Be3!+–
14.fxe6+
Editor’s note:
There was a more convincing line — 14.Qh5+ Ke7 15.fxe6 Kxe6 16.Be3 Qa5 17.dxe5 Nxe5 18.Qf5+ Kd6 19.Bf4+–.
14...Kxe6
15.Rb1!
Simagin didn’t expect this move. He calculated the line 15.Qg4+ Kd6, thinking he would be able to put up stiff resistance.
15...Qxb1 16.Qc4+ Kd6
Editor’s note:
16...Ke7 17.Ba3+ Kd8 18.Rxb1 Bxa3 19.Rxb7 Rc8 20.Rxa7 Bd6 was more tenacious
17.Ba3+ Kc7 18.Rxb1 Bxa3 19.Qb3! Be7
Black has enough material compensation for the queen, but his king is under attack.
20.Qxb7+ Kd6 21.dxe5+?
Tal thought for a long time back on his twelfth move, but then started playing very rapidly. The consequences of this dangerous approached showed quickly. Had Tal been more careful (or rather calmer), he would’ve noticed the simple line 21.Rd1! e4 22.d5! Ne5 23.dxc6+ Ke6 24.Qb3+ Kf5 25.Rf1+. After this mistake, white had to put a lot more effort into this attack.
21...Nxe5 22.Rd1+ Ke6 23.Qb3+ Kf5 24.Rf1+ Ke4
25.Re1+
Editor’s note:
The simplest way to victory was 25.Qe6!, threatening Re1+, for instance: 25...Bc5+ 26.Kh1 Rae8 27.Re1+ Be3 28.Qh3 Nc4 29.Qd7! Ne5 30.Qd4+ Kf5 31.Rxe3 h5 32.Qe4+ Kf6 33.Qf4+ and curtains.
25...Kf5 26.g4+ Kf6 27.Rf1+ Kg6 28.Qe6+ Kh7 29.Qxe5 Rhe8 30.Rf7 Bf8 31.Qf5+
Editor’s note:
31.Qd4 is much stronger (the queen stays in the center, maintaining the pressure on both flanks), intending h2-h4 and g4-g5. The point is that black won’t be able to either activate his bishop with Bf8-c5 or double rooks on the e-file (31...Re1+ 32.Kf2 Rae8?? 33.Rxf8+–).
31...Kg8 32.Kf2
An original idea! The king goes to help its major pieces. The simpler move was 32.Qg6 Re1+ 33.Kg2 Rae8 34.Rf1. (
Editor’s note:
After the subsequent 34...R1e2+ 35.Rf2 R2e5 36.Qxc6 Bc5, it would be quite difficult for white to win. However, 32.Qg6 was indeed preferable to the game move.)
32...Bc5+ 33.Kg3 Re3+ 34.Kh4
34...Rae8
After 34...Be7+, there was 35.g5! At first, instead of 35.g5, Tal wanted to play 35.Kh5 Rd8 36.Rxg7+, sacrificing the second rook. However, he then noticed that after 36...Kxg7 37.Qg6+ black could avoid defeat by putting his king on f8: 37...Kf8! 38.Qxh6+ Kf7 39.Qxe3 Rh8+
35.Rxg7+ Kxg7 36.Qxc5 R8e6
The last, decisive mistake. Later, grandmaster V. Ragozin showed that after 36...R8e7 37.Qxc6 Rf7, threatening 38...Rff3, black could still draw.
37.Qxa7+ Kg6 38.Qa8 Kf6 39.a4
(the passed pawn assures white’s victory)
39...Ke5 40.a5 Kd5 41.Qd8+ Ke4 42.a6 Kf3 43.a7 Re2 44.Qd3+ R6e3 45.Qxe3+
and
black resigned.
After a month of battles, Tal shared 5th–7th place, just one point behind the winners.
In summer 1956, Tal travelled to Sweden with the USSR team to take part in the students world team championship. In his first competition abroad, he scored 4/5.
Tal’s game against the Yugoslav grandmaster Borislav Ivkov made the rounds in the world chess press. “Tal! Remember the name!” wrote the
Jugoslovensik Sahovski Glasnik
magazine back then.
Soviet Champion
In late November 1956, the semi-final of the next, 24th Soviet championship, was played in Tbilisi. Tal played there again. Grandmasters Petrosian and Korchnoi, and masters Polugaevsky, Furman, Antoshin and others, competed for the right to take part in the main competition.
Tal’s start was disappointing. The first setback happened in the very first round. Analyzing his adjourned game against Kotkov, Tal found a subtle maneuver that should’ve won the game for him. However, during the play-off, he made a rash move that threw away all the hard-earned advantage, and the game ended in a draw.
Three more draws followed, and in the fifth round, Tal blundered and lost to master Vladlen Zurakhov. And then another three draws.
In round 9, Tal managed to outplay the “king of endgame studies”, international master Genrikh Kasparyan, in a difficult endgame. It looked like a turning point. However, he then suffered a new blow. In the game against Alexander Buslaev, Tal went for a spectacular mate in an overwhelming position, blundered and lost.
Indeed, Tal’s play was far from perfect at the start. Miscalculations in the middlegame and bad advantage conversion technique were the main causes for concern. His physical form was also far from perfect.
But all these failures didn’t break Tal, and in the second half of the tournament, he started to win one game after another. The turning point was round 13, when Tal played Yakov Yukhtman.
Yukhtman, who usually plays rather quickly, spent more than an hour on his 17th move. Indeed, there was a lot to think about!
No. 19
M. Tal – Y. Yukhtman
1956, Tbilisi, 24th Soviet Championship Semi-Final, round 13
Black is hoping to get things going on the kingside, but Tal’s unexpected tactical blow showed that he had other trouble coming.
17.Bd5!
Editor’s note:
Perhaps 17.Nd5! was even stronger.
17.Bd5! Nxe5
After 17...exd5, white, of course, would have played 18.exd6
18.Nxe5 exd5
It was necessary to eliminate the bishop, but now new weaknesses emerge in black’s position.
Editor’s note:
18...dxe5 19.Qxe5 0-0 20.Bxc6 bxc6 21.Rd7 Rfe8 was the lesser evil.
19.Nxd5 Bxd5 20.Rxd5 dxe5
If 20...0-0, then 21.Nd7 Rfe8 22.Qxe7! (22...Rxe7 23.Nf6+)
21.Rxe5 0-0 22.Rxe7
The rest is simple.
Editor’s note:
If we study the final part of the game more closely, it turns out that black still had good chances of saving the game.
22...Rac8 23.Qe4 b5 24.Re1 Rcd8 25.c4! bxc4 26.Qxc4 Qg6 27.Ra7 Rfe8 28.Rxe8+ Rxe8 29.g3 Re6 30.Kg2 Rf6 31.Ra8+ Kh7 32.Rd8!
32...Rxf2+
Walking straight into a trap. After widescale simplifications, white gets a won pawn endgame.
33.Kxf2 Qf6+ 34.Ke3 Qxd8 35.Qd3+! Qxd3+ 36.Kxd3 Kg6 37.Ke4 Kf6 38.g4 Ke6 39.b4 h5 40.gxh5 f5+ 41.Kd4 Kf6 42.a4 g4 43.b5 Black resigned.
Losing just half a point in the last four rounds, Tal shared 5th–6th place and again earned the right to play among the strongest in the 24th Soviet championship final.
Before I start the story about the 24th Soviet championship, I’d like to say a few words about the so-called psychological method of struggle.
It’s no secret that the individual approach to opponents – i.e. studying the particular features of their playing style, creative tastes and inclinations, and using that knowledge in games – has brought Tal many valuable points. The main feature of this method, which is not exactly new in chess, is the ability to impose unfamiliar, unpleasant playing conditions on the opponent. For instance, an opening they don’t like to play or a position that doesn’t meet their tastes and chess temperament.
“War is war!” Let the opponent struggle against the unfamiliar conditions!
In the first round of the 24th Soviet championship, Tal was due to play the Leningrad master Lev Aronson. After studying his games, we figured out that the Leningrad player preferred quiet maneuvering and tended to simplify the position. And so we decided to play for complications.
No. 20, Dutch Defense
L. Aronson – M. Tal
1957, Moscow, 24th Soviet Championship Final, round 1
1.d4 e6 2.c4 f5 3.Nf3 Nf6 4.Nc3 Be7 5.g3 0-0 6.Bg2 d6 7.0-0 Qe8
8.Re1
Editor’s note:
An important alternative is 8.b3, intending to develop the bishop to a3 after Qe8-g6 or Qe8-h5, seriously hindering the standard pawn push e6-e5.
8...Qg6 9.e4 fxe4 10.Nxe4 Nxe4 11.Rxe4 Nc6
Avoiding the trap 11...Qxe4? 12.Nh4!
12.Qe2 Bf6 13.Bd2 e5 14.dxe5 dxe5
Of course, Tal could just capture on e5 with his knight. That was more solid, but after 14...Nxe5 15.Bc3, the game would have quickly ended in a draw.
Editor’s note:
14...Nxe5 is now usually met with 15.Nxe5 (instead of Koblenz’s 15.Bc3) 15...Bxe5 16.Bc3!? Bxc3 17.bxc3 – practice shows that, despite his compromised pawn structure, white has a small advantage because of his more active pieces and pressure along the long light-squared diagonal.
Black’s last move is risky – the e5 pawn can become weak. However, black’s 14th move was the turning point of the game. The weakness of the e5 pawn is rather relative. The main factor is that Tal managed to complicate the game, which probably didn’t feature in his opponent’s plans.
Now Aronson thinks he’s obliged to play for a win.
15.Bc3 Bf5 16.Nh4 Bxh4 17.Rxh4 Rae8 18.Qe3 h6 19.b4 Qf6 20.b5 Nd8 21.Bd5+
The beginning of an erroneous plan. Had Aronson kept his cool, he would have no doubt found the prosaic move 21.c5 with the subsequent 22.Ra4.
21...Kh8
This is stronger than 21...Kh7 22.Be4, with simplifications that are advantageous for white.
Editor’s note:
Instead of the simplifying 22.Be4, white had a more unpleasant move 22.Re1, increasing the pressure on the e5 pawn. If now 22...g5, then 23.Re4! Bxe4 24.Qxe4+ Kg7 (after 24...Qg6 25.Qe3 black’s position is even harder) 25.Bxe5 Rxe5 26.Qxe5, with an extra pawn in the endgame.
22.f4?
White thinks that the position is already ripe to play for a win. 22.c5 was best. After 22...c6, there was a complicated position with mutual chances.
Editor’s note:
After a long think, the computer prefers the move 22.Re1 with the main line 22...g5 23.Re4 Bxe4 24.Bxe4 b6 25.Bg2 Nf7 26.Bd5 — it thinks that white’s compensation (the bishop pair and pressure on the e5 pawn) is more than adequate. Another good alternative is 22.Rd1 – for instance, 22...c6 23.Bg2 g5 24.Rhd4! exd4 25.Bxd4 Rxe3 26.Bxf6+ Rxf6 27.Rxd8+ Kg7 28.fxe3 with an extra pawn in the endgame. But even after Koblenz’s 22.c5 c6, white is still somewhat better after the subsequent 23.bxc6 Nxc6 (or 23...bxc6 24.Bg2) 24.f4 because of the two bishops.
22...exf4 23.Qd2
Editor’s note:
After 23.Bxf6 fxe3 24.Rxh6+ Bh7 25.Bc3 c6 26.bxc6 bxc6 27.Bg2 the position is dynamically equal – white’s bishop pair is compensated by black’s active rooks and the passed e3 pawn.
23...Qb6+
With this unexpected check, Tal makes the white bishop lose control over the e1 square, which will be important later. The initiative is in black’s hands already!
24.Bd4
Editor’s note:
24.Qd4 is more solid, with a roughly equal position – for instance, after 24...Qg6 25.Qxf4 (threatening to capture on h6) 25...Kh7 26.Bb4 Rf6 27.Bc3 Rff8 28.Bb4 Rf6, the game ends in a threefold repetition.
24...Qg6 25.Qxf4 Kh7 26.Qxc7
Editor’s note:
This is an error – the queen gets too far away from the king. 26.Qd2 was the correct move.
26...Bb1!
(isolating the opponent’s rook)
27.Be5
Editor’s note:
White still had some chances after 27.Rf4 Re1+ 28.Kf2 Rfe8 29.Qxg7+ Qxg7 30.Bxg7 Kxg7 31.Bf3 – black has a piece for two pawns, but it’s not easy to get out of the pin along the first rank.
27...Ne6! 28.Qd6 Qf5 29.Bf4 Ng5
29...g5 was also possible, but Tal decided not to weaken his king’s position in time trouble.
30.Qb4 Be4 31.Bxe4 Rxe4 32.Rf1 Re2 33.Qd6
33...Rxa2!
Of course, 33...Nh3+? was bad due to 34.Rxh3 Qxh3 35.Qd3+.
34.Qd5 Qc2 35.c5 Rd8! 36.Bd6 Re8 White resigned
, as the threat 37...
R
e1 cannot be stopped.
Tal’s victory over Taimanov in the second round wasn’t convincing. The Leningrad grandmaster got an undeniable advantage in the opening. Tal only won because of a simple tactical blow overlooked by his opponent.
In the next round, Tal faced David Bronstein, whose games he’d always admired. He couldn’t wait for the game to begin.
No. 21, Grunfeld Defense
M. Tal – D. Bronstein
1957, Moscow, 24th Soviet Championship Final, round 3
1.d4 c6 2.c4 Nf6 3.Nc3 d5 4.Nf3 g6 5.cxd5
The strongest move; after 5...cxd5, black has no c-pawn anymore and so can’t bring his dark-squared bishop to life with c6-c5. Bronstein, of course, knew that white would be better, and acted out of psychological considerations: the game would be simplified, and his opponent would lose the opportunity to go for tactical complications which he normally pursued.
So, Bronstein thought that the subsequent position would not be to Tal’s taste.
5...cxd5 6.Bf4 Nc6 7.e3 Bg7 8.Be2 0-0 9.0-0
9.h3, preventing the trade of the dark-squared bishop, was worth considering.
Editor’s note:
Koblenz’s proposed move 9.h3, which provides the h2 square for the dark-squared bishop and prevents Bg4, leads to one of the main positions in this line. Even more often, white plays h2-h3 a move earlier and then develops the f1 bishop to e2 (like in this game) or d3 (which leads to another main position).
9...Nh5 10.Be5
Editor’s note:
10.Bg5 h6 11.Bh4 g5 12.Nd2!?, as played in Ruzele – Mozeris (Plateliai 2001) and Ernst – Appel (Germany BL 2014), was perhaps even more promising.
10...f6 11.Bg3 Nxg3 12.hxg3 Be6 13.Qb3!
White has to play actively, otherwise black will seize the initiative with Bf7 and e7-e5.
13...Na5 14.Qb4 Bf7 15.Rfd1
This prophylactic move is necessary. Black can’t play 15...e5 now due to 16.dxe5 fxe5 17.Nxd5! Bxd5 18.e4.
15...e6 16.e4! Rb8 17.Qa3 b5
The grandmaster spent 40 minutes thinking on this move!
18.b4 Nc4 19.Qxa7
When white grabbed the a7 pawn, Bronstein shook his head in surprise: if a grandmaster offers a sacrifice after a long think, he has obviously calculated everything very well. But Tal wasn’t inclined to believe anybody at face value!
19...Be8
Editor’s note:
The game move doesn’t give black enough compensation for the pawn; meanwhile, after 19...Ra8 20.Qc5 (20.Qb7?? Nd6 21.Qc6 Rc8–+) 20...Rc8 21.Qxb5 Nb2 22.Rdc1 Bh6 23.exd5 Bxc1 24.Rxc1 exd5, there was a complicated position evaluated as equal by the computer. 19...Re8!? was also interesting, with the ideas of Re8-e7 and Bg7-f8.
20.Bxc4 Rf7
Following the game, Tal said that after 20...Ra8 21.Qc5 Rc8, he was going to sacrifice the queen with 22.Bb3!? (
Editor’s note:
22.Bxd5! Rxc5 23.Bxe6+ Kh8 24.dxc5+– is much stronger), while if 21...Rf7, he intended to play 22.exd5!
21.Qa3
Tal breathed a sigh of relief: the white queen has come back home.
21...bxc4 22.exd5 exd5 23.Qb2 g5!
There are new unexpected threats: black is getting ready to launch a kingside attack with 24...g4 and f6-f5-f4.
24.Qd2! Rxb4
Now the above plan would not work, because after 24...g4 25.Ne1 f5, white would play 26.Ne2. (
Editor’s note:
Black has an unpleasant reply 26...Ba4, and the bishop makes itself useful, blocking the a2 passed pawn. 26.Nc2 was stronger, intending a2-a4 – the move 26...Qd6 with the ideas f5-f4 and Bg7-h6 is not dangerous for white because of 27.Qf4.)
25.Nxd5 Rb8 26.Ne3 Rc8 27.Rac1 Rfc7 28.Rc3 Bg6 29.Rdc1 Bf7 30.d5 Bf8 31.a3 Bc5 32.Rxc4
The technical stage of the game begins – white converts his material advantage.
32...Bxa3
If 32...Bxe3, then, of course, 33.Rxc7
33.Rxc7 Rxc7 34.Rxc7 Qxc7 35.Qd4 Qc1+ 36.Kh2 Qb2 37.Qe4 Bf8 38.Nd4 h5 39.Ndc2 Qb7 40.Nd4 Qb2
41.Ne6!
(the sealed move)
41...Qxf2.
If 41...Qe5, then 42.Qc4! or 41...Qb4 42.Qf3!
42.Nf5 h4 43.Nxf8 hxg3+ 44.Kh3 Qg1 45.Kxg3 Kxf8 46.Qe7+ Black resigned.
The games against Keres and Petrosian were played in an impeccable positional style by Tal – he beat the grandmasters with their own weapon.
The game Keres – Tal was played in round 16, when the tournament atmosphere was very heated. Keres led the tournament with 10 points. Tal, Bronstein and Tolush were half a point behind.
No. 22, Anti-Nimzo-Indian
P. Keres – M. Tal
1957, Moscow, 24th Soviet Championship Final, round 16
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 e6 3.Nf3 c5 4.e3
Keres avoids the double-edged 4.d5 and chooses a quiet development plan.
4...d5 5.a3 cxd4 6.exd4 Be7 7.Nc3 0-0 8.Bf4
Editor’s note:
A rare continuation. The main line is 8.Bd3.
8...Nc6
Editor’s note:
In the game Mamedyarov – Adams (Bilbao 2013), black instead played 8...b6 9.Bd3 dxc4 10.Bxc4 Ba6 11.Bxa6 Nxa6 12.Rc1 Nc7 13.0-0 Ncd5 14.Nxd5 Nxd5 15.Bg3 Qd7 16.Ne5 Qb7, and the game soon ended in a draw without any problems for black.
9.Rc1
Editor’s note:
After 9.c5?! Ne4 10.Qc2 g5 11.Be3 f5 12.h3 Bf6, black had the initiative (Grigoryan – Fridman, Kallithea 2008).
9...Ne4 10.Bd3 Nxc3 11.Rxc3 dxc4 12.Rxc4
After 12.Bxc4 Bf6 13.Rd3 Qb6 black is in no trouble.
12...Qa5+ 13.Bd2
Keres is still playing quite quietly. However, this does not mean that he’s playing for a draw. In that case, he would’ve played 13.Qd2, offering to exchange queens.
13...Qd5 14.Qc2 f5!
Black bolsters his queen’s position in the center, not fearing potentially weakening the e-pawn.
15.0-0 Bd7 16.Rd1
Editor’s note:
In the game Mishuchkov – Sakaev (St. Petersburg 1997) there followed 16.Rc1 Rac8=.
16...Rac8
Winning the d4 pawn would have cost black too much: 16...b5 17.Rc3 Nxd4 18.Nxd4 Qxd4 19.Be3 Qf6 20.Bxf5!
Editor’s note:
Actually, Koblenz’s line is not dangerous for black: after the subsequent 20...Qxf5 21.Rxd7 Rad8 22.Qxf5 exf5 23.Rcc7 Bf6, there’s a drawn endgame on the board. However, instead of 19.Be3?, white has a much stronger move 19.Be2! – despite the lack of a pawn, white has a big advantage because of the great positioning of his major pieces and active bishops.
17.Be3 Na5! 18.Rxc8 Rxc8 19.Qe2 Bd6 20.Ne5
A pointless sacrifice. (
Editor’s note:
Koblenz probably meant that Keres was already intending to transition to an opposite-colored bishop endgame, giving up a pawn in the process.) After 20.Rc1 Rxc1+ 21.Bxc1, white was not under any threat.
20...Ba4 21.Re1 Bxe5 22.dxe5 Rd8 23.b4
Editor’s note:
The resulting endgame turned out to be very difficult for white. He had to play 23.Bc2 Bxc2 24.Qxc2
After 24...Qxe5 25.f4 Qb8 26.Bd2 Nc6 27.Qc4 Nd4 28.Be3 Qd6 29.Bxd4 Qxd4+ 30.Qxd4 Rxd4 31.Rxe6, there’s a rook ending that he can easily hold – 31...Kf7 (31...Rxf4 32.Re7=) 32.Re5 Kf6 (32...Rxf4 33.Rc5=) 33.g3 with a draw.
Instead of 24...Qxe5, black had a stronger continuation 24...Nc6 25.f4 a6 with a positional advantage, but white still had more chances to hold than in the actual game.
23...Bc6! 24.f3 Qxd3 25.Qxd3 Rxd3 26.bxa5 Rxa3 27.Bxa7 Rxa5
Black is a pawn up, but this advantage is quite difficult to convert because of the opposite-colored bishops.
28.Bd4 Ra2 29.Rb1 Rd2 30.Bc3 Rc2 31.Bd4 Kf7 32.h4
A new weakness. However, 32.h3 wasn’t much better.
32...Kg6 33.Rb4 h6!
White is in time trouble, and Tal provokes his opponent’s next moves, so that after 37.Bf8 the black pawns are vulnerable.
34.Rb2 Rxb2 35.Bxb2 Kh5 36.Ba3 Kxh4! 37.Bf8 Kg3 38.Bxg7 h5 39.Bh6 Bxf3!
An elegant bishop sacrifice that quickly decides the game.
40.gxf3 Kxf3 41.Kf1 b5 42.Bd2 h4 43.Bb4 h3 44.Kg1 Ke2 White resigned.
No. 23, French Defense
M. Tal – T. Petrosian
1957, Moscow, 24th Soviet Championship Final, round 13
1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.Nc3 Bb4 4.e5 c5 5.a3 Bxc3+ 6.bxc3 Ne7 7.Qg4
7...Nf5
Editor’s note:
From the point of view of modern theory, the main moves here are 7...0-0 and 7...Qc7.
8.Bd3 h5 9.Qh3
Editor’s note:
In subsequent tournament practice, this move has occurred only rarely at grandmaster level. The main line is 9.Qf4.
9...cxd4 10.Nf3 Nc6 11.g4 Nfe7 12.gxh5
Editor’s note:
12.cxd4 has also occurred, after which 12...Qa5+ looks good, for instance, 13.Bd2 Qa4 14.c3 b6 15.Ng5 Ba6, and black has no complaints (Lettieri – Navaratham, Paracin 2019). Another possible continuation is 12...Qc7 13.Bd2 Bd7 (intending 0-0-0 and f7-f6) with mutual chances (Dorawa – Uhlmann, Schkopau 1958 and O. Jacobsen – Bagirov, Helsinki 1961).
12...Qc7 13.Bf4 Ng6 14.Qg4 Nxf4
A serious mistake. Black should have played 14...Ngxe5 15.Nxe5 Nxe5 16.Qxg7 Nf3+ 17.Kf1 Qxf4 18.Qxh8+ Ke7 with a sharp, hard-to-evaluate position.
Editor’s note:
Actually, the game move is not bad at all.
15.Qxf4 dxc3
16.Qg5!
Editor’s note:
16.Rg1!? is worth considering; black would meet it with 16...f6 or 16...f5 with unclear play.
16...Qe7 17.Qxg7 Qf8
Editor’s note:
17...Rxh5!? looks interesting, as played in a correspondence game Hugentobler – Dambrauskas, ICCF 1999. There followed 18.Rg1 Qf8 19.Qf6 Rh6 20.Qf4 Bd7 21.Ng5 0-0-0 22.Nxf7 Nxe5 23.Qxe5 Qxf7 24.Qxc3+ Kb8, and black is no worse.
18.Qg5 Qh6
The white queen cannot be allowed to occupy such a strong position.
Editor’s note:
18...Rh6 was a stronger move, with the threat 19...Qh8. For instance, 19.Rg1 Qh8 20.Qg8+ Qxg8 21.Rxg8+ Ke7 22.Rg5 Bd7 with roughly equal chances.
19.Rg1 Qxg5
19...Qxh5 would have been met with 20.Qf6!
20.Rxg5 Bd7
Editor’s note:
Black should have first played 20...Ke7. Now, white could put his opponent in a very precarious situation after 21.h6!
21.Ke2 Ke7 22.Ke3 Raf8 23.Rag1 Be8
24.Nd4 f5
Black could’ve put up better resistance after 24...f6.
Editor’s note:
Actually, the game move is the decisive mistake, whereas after Koblenz’s move 24...f6, black doesn’t just “put up better resistance” – he shouldn’t lose at all, for instance: 25.Rg7+ Bf7 26.Nxc6+ bxc6 27.f4 Rxh5 28.Bg6 Rh3+ (28...Rxh2?? 29.Rb1+–) 29.Kd4 Rh4 30.Ke3 Rh3+ with threefold repetition.
25.Nxc6+ Bxc6
Editor’s note:
Black can’t hold after 25...bxc6 either: 26.Rb1! Rfg8 (any capture on h5 (26...Bxh5) loses to 27.Rb7+ with the subsequent 28.Rgg7) 27.Rxg8 Rxg8 28.Kd4, then getting the king to d6 after Rb1-b7+.
26.Kd4 f4 27.Rg7+ Rf7 28.Rg8 Rxg8 29.Rxg8 Rf8 30.Rxf8 Kxf8 31.h6 b6 32.Kxc3 Kg8 33.Bg6 a5 34.Kd4 Kf8 35.c3 Kg8 36.h7+ Kg7 37.Bf7 Bd7 38.Bg8
Black’s position is hopeless, and the
grandmaster resigned
on move 51.
The sixth-round game was truly breathtaking.
This is a position from the game Nezhmetdinov – Tal. At that moment, Tal led the tournament.
No. 24
R. Nezhmetdinov – M. Tal
1957, Moscow, 24th Soviet Championship Final, round 6
13...Ncxe5
This leads to great complications. It was impossible to fully calculate the numerous lines. Tal saw that after 13...Nxc5 14.Bd3 Ne4! 15.Qe1 Bb7 white couldn’t capture the pawn due to 16.Bxe4 dxe4 17.Qxe4 Ne7, and black is better. However, instead of 16.Bxe4, white could’ve played 16.Bb2, and Tal didn’t see how to improve black’s position after that.
Editor’s note:
After 16.Bb2, black could get a comfortable position, for instance, with 16...a5, intending Bb7-a6
13...Ncxe5
Tal created a pawn center, hoping to make it dynamic afterwards.
14.Nxe5 Nxe5 15.Qd4 f6 16.f4 Nc6 17.Qe3 Rd8 18.Rad1 e5
Editor’s note:
This active move leads to big trouble. Black should’ve made some waiting, neutral move, such as 18...Rb8.
19.fxe5 fxe5
Tal executed his strategic idea: he has created a dynamic pawn center. However, the continuation shows that it didn’t bring black anything good.
20.Bb5! Bb7
After 20...d4 21.Bc4+ Kh8 22.Qg5! white had an attack.
Editor’s note:
Instead of 21.Bc4+, white had an even stronger continuation: 21.Qe4 Bb7 (21...dxc3 22.Qxc6+–) 22.Bd3 g6 23.Bc4+ Kh8 24.Rf7 Rd7 25.Rdf1 dxc3 26.Qh4+–, so black shouldn’t have played 20...d4 in any case.
21.Qg3!
Editor’s note:
This move is actually not so good, because black could now play 21...Qe7!, getting good chances to hold the position after 22.Bxc6 Qxc5+ 23.Rf2 Qxc6 24.Qxe5 Qd7 (the pawn sacrifice 24...d4!?, activating the pieces, also deserves consideration). So, a stronger move was 21.Qg5 with a big advantage (in this case, 21...Qe7 doesn’t work due to the obvious 22.Qxe7 Nxe7 23.Bxe5+–).
21...Rd7
22.Rf2!
A strong move. White uses the f-file to his advantage. Also, the aggressive push of the d-pawn is now impossible. For instance, 22...d4 23.Bc4+ Kh8 24.Rdf1!, with pressure along the f-file.
22...Re8 23.h3 Ba8
23...d4 still doesn’t work due to 24.Bc4+.
24.Ba4 Bb7 25.Kh1 Ba8 26.Rf5
White goes for an all-out offensive.
26...e4
There was no defense against the threat of 27.Bxc6 and Rxe5.
27.Qxc7 Rxc7 28.Rfxd5 e3 29.Rd7 e2 30.Bb3+ Re6 31.Bxe6+ Kf8 32.Bxg7+ Black resigned.
The game with Nezhmetdinov further convinced Tal that chess principles have only a relative value. Why did black’s plan to create a strong center end in failure? Here’s how Tal himself explained the reason for his loss:
“The evaluation of any position is like scales. One of the heaviest weights, a dynamic pawn center, in this case ended up being lighter than the open f-file and the coordination between two white bishops and the queen.”
This comparison helps to reveal the essence of Tal’s creative method: he often allows his opponent to get a tangible positional advantage, but doesn’t forget about his own “weights” either, always achieving some kind of trade-off. In the Nezhmetdinov game, Tal’s “weight” was too light. Well, that happens.
I must admit that, the next day, I was somewhat worried: how would the loss affect my ward? But as soon as I saw Tal, my anxiety was gone. He was cheerful and happy, because his loss was deserved!
We discussed how to play against Antoshin and then went for a walk.
“Time goes so slowly,” Tal said nervously. “I already want to start the game!”
In this game, Tal’s play was very dynamic. Everything seemed to go well, but then the Riga master made an inexplicable mistake.
Here’s the game.
No. 25, Ruy Lopez
M. Tal – V. Antoshin
1957, Moscow, 24th Soviet Championship Final, round 7
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 a6 4.Ba4 Nf6 5.0-0 Be7 6.Re1 b5 7.Bb3 d6 8.c3 0-0 9.h3 Nd7
Editor’s note:
Currently, black most often plays 9...Na5, 9...Bb7 or 9...Nb8.
10.d4 Nb6
11.Be3
Editor’s note:
In this position, the main line is 11.Nbd2 with the ideas Nd2-f1-g3(-f5) or Nd2-f1-h2-g4.
11...exd4
Theory prefers 11...Rb8.
Editor’s note:
The move 11...Rb8 counters dxe5 with the subsequent Bxb6 – now black can simply take on b6 with the rook, avoiding doubled pawns. Also, in such positions white often tries to play a2-a4, which may lead to the opening of the b-file, and the rook on b8 may be useful in this case as well.
12.cxd4 d5 13.Nc3 dxe4 14.Nxe4 Bf5 15.d5 Na5
A serious mistake. With 15...Bxe4 16.Bxb6 Bxf3 17.Qxf3 Ne5!, black could still resist. (
Editor’s note:
After the subsequent 18.Rxe5 cxb6, the position is almost completely drawn: there are opposite-colored bishops on the board, white has no attacking chances, and his passed pawn can’t move further.)
16.d6 cxd6
If 16...Bxd6, then, of course, 17.Bxb6. After 16...Bxe4, there’s 17.dxe7 Qxe7 18.Bxb6 cxb6 19.Nd2, winning a piece.
17.Bxb6 Qxb6 18.Ng3 Be6 19.Bxe6 fxe6 20.Rxe6 Bf6
Black decides to part with the weak d-pawn and put pressure on the b2 pawn.
21.Rxd6 Qb8 22.Nh5!
The signal to attack!
22...Bxb2 23.Rb1 Nc4 24.Rd7 Ra7 25.Rxb2!
Eliminating the guardian of the g7 square!
25...Nxb2 26.Qd5+ Kh8 27.Qd4 Rxd7 28.Qxd7 Rg8 29.Ng5 h6 30.Nf7+ Kh7
There were, as always, lots of masters and journalists in the press center. The door suddenly opened, grandmaster Geller briskly walked in, and said, “Tal has a beautiful forced win!”
And he quickly showed the line: 31.Qf5+ g6 32.Qd7 gxh5 33.Ng5+ Kg6 34.Qe6+ Kxg5 35.g3, and black has to resign, because after 35...h4, he gets checkmated: 36.f4+ Kh5 37.Qf5+ Rg5 38.g4#.
“A spectacular finish! Let’s go to the hall, there’ll probably be applause,” said master Evgeny Zagoryansky, the press center administrator.
We went to the box seats; several grandmasters and journalists were already there.
Time went by, however, and Tal still continued to think, leaning close to the board. Almost quarter of an hour passed.
“Why is Tal thinking for so long, doesn’t he see the win?” Salomon Flohr asked.
“He doesn’t,” Igor Bondarevsky replied.
“You really think that Tal doesn’t see it?” grandmaster Andor Lilienthal argued.
However, Tal was still feverishly calculating the lines, and his opponent walked about the stage, feigning indifference. The minutes seemed endless to me. Finally, there was a flurry of whispers in the hall: the spectators also noticed the winning line.
Almost an hour passed. Finally, Tal raised his hand to make a move, and the whole hall held their breaths.
But what happened? There was no applause. The spectators groaned in disappointment.
31.h4?
How could Tal miss a simple mating combination?
“I saw it, but I thought that it wouldn’t work,” Tal explained afterwards. “I thought that the pawn was on g2, rather than g3, and so, g2-g4 was not a checkmate, because the opponent had 38...hxg3.”
In the actual game, there followed:
31...Qc8! 32.Nf6+ Kg6 33.Nxg8 Qxd7 34.Ne5+ Kh7 35.Nxd7 Kxg8 36.Nc5 a5 37.Kf1 Nd1 38.Ne4 b4 39.Ke2 Nb2 40.Kd2 a4
, and a
draw was agreed.
I tried to console Tal, to take his mind off that game, but all my efforts were in vain: he was depressed, even though this setback didn’t hurt his standings in the tournament – he still led after that draw.
