But the young master wasn’t thinking about the lost half-point: he couldn’t forgive himself for the mistake that tarnished the beauty of a creative idea. Like an artist, Tal was deeply hurt by the very thought that a work of art was corrupted.
The ill-fated seventh-round game had far-reaching repercussions. Tal was psychologically exhausted and played without inspiration; he agreed four pallid draws and then lost to Boleslavsky. Only after defeating Petrosian and Klaman did he manage to finally overcome the crisis.
“Do you want to know how Tal wins? It’s very simple: he places the pieces in the center and then sacrifices them somewhere,” Bronstein once said.
This joke did have a grain of truth in it. Tal usually prepares his combinations quite thoroughly.
No. 26
M. Tal – K. Klaman
1957, Moscow, 24th Soviet Championship Final, round 13
This is the position from the game Tal – Klaman, played in round 13, right after the loss to Boleslavsky.*
* According to other sources this game was played in round 15
The white pieces are positioned perfectly: the rooks and the knight are ready to land a decisive strike, and the queen has disorganized the defense. Black’s pieces are positioned quite awkwardly, and the king is stuck in the center. A beautiful combination follows:
23.Bxf5!! exf5 24.Rxe7+ Kxe7 25.Re1+ Kd8 26.Qh4+ f6 27.Qh6! Qa5
The moves from 23 to 27 were made very quickly: both players obviously wanted to reach the same position.
28.Nb3!
This Zwischenzug is the point of white’s combination. Klaman expected the obvious-looking 28.Qxf8+ Kc7, after which there’s no 29.Qxf6 due to 29...b3! Now, however, black is quickly crushed.
28...Qd5 29.Qxf8+ Kc7 30.Qxf6 Re8 31.Rc1 Ba4 32.Qd4! Qb7 33.Rd1 Re6 34.Qc4+ Black resigned
(34...Kd7 35.Nc5+).
The game Tal – Aronin, from round 17, was called “the most interesting draw in the history of chess” by the ex-world champion Max Euwe.
No. 27
M. Tal – L. Aronin
1957, Moscow, 24th Soviet Championship Final, round 17
Here’s the starting position of this captivating tactical struggle.
25.h5!
The only attacking resource. After the natural 25.f3, black avoided any danger with 25...Bh5. (
Editor’s note:
In this case, white’s position would be difficult because of the pawn’s weakness and the king’s compromised position. The game move leads to complicated tactical play and is best from the practical point of view, even though it still loses if the opponent plays perfectly.)
25.h5! Rad8
Editor’s note:
With 25...Red8!, Aronin could refute his opponent’s plan and win. The point is, after 26.hxg6 Rxd3 27.Rxd3, black plays 27...fxg6!, repelling the attack (the move 28.f7+ makes no sense if the rook isn’t on e8) and retaining a decisive material advantage. 27.gxh7+ (instead of 27.Rxd3) doesn’t help either: 27...Kh8 28.Rxd3 (hoping to create some serious threats along the g-file) – for instance, after 28...Qe8! (intending to activate the queen if possible) 29.Rg3 Be2! 30.Rhg1 Bh5, there’s no way for white to continue the attack.
26.hxg6! Rxd3
26...hxg6 is bad because of 27.Qxd8 Rxd8 28.Rxd8 Qxd8 29.Rh8+ Kxh8 30.Nxf7+ Kg8 31.Nxd8 with a won endgame.
27.Rxd3
White gets only a rook for the queen, but the black king’s precarious position allows him to create strong threats.
27...hxg6 28.Rh7!
Threatening 29.f3. If the bishop retreats to h5, there follows 30.Rd7, and if it goes to e6, the other rook can be transferred to the h-file through d2.
Aronin decides to eliminate the dangerous adversary, the f6 pawn, which leads to a draw by force.
28...Rc8! 29.f3 Rc6! 30.Rxf7
30.fxg4 Rxf6 31.Rdh3 was dangerous due to 31...Rf2+ and then Qd6. (
Editor’s note:
White is simply lost after that.)
30...Qxf7 31.Nxf7 Kxf7 32.fxg4 Kxf6 33.Rd7 Rb6 34.Kc3 Kg5 35.a4 a6 36.Kc4 Kxg4
The opponents
agreed a draw
because of the unavoidable repetition: 37.Kc5 Rc6+ 38.Kd5 Rb6 39.Kc5 Rc6+.
Before the last round, Bronstein, Tolush and Tal all led the tournament. Chess fans anxiously waited for the answer to their most important question: who’s going to be the national champion?
“I’m glad to be playing Tolush! He’s a true fighter, he won’t avoid a sharp struggle,” said Tal happily before the game.
On the way to the playing hall, he joked a lot and quoted from
The Twelve Chairs
extensively. (Analysis of this novel was later the topic of Tal’s university degree dissertation.)
I walked silently and thought, “Such an important day for him, and he just jokes around!”
There were a lot of people at the entrance of the Rail Workers House of Culture. We somehow managed to squeeze into the building.
“This is a good sign!” Tal laughed when he saw that almost all the buttons were torn from his coat.
No. 28, King’s Indian Defense
M. Tal – A. Tolush
1957, Moscow, 24th Soviet Championship Final, round 21
1.c4 Nf6 2.Nc3 g6 3.e4 d6 4.d4 Bg7 5.f3 e5 6.Nge2 Nbd7
Editor’s note:
In modern times, strong players don’t usually play this line for black. 6...0-0 is more flexible, not committing to any move of the Nb8 just yet. However, black usually castles even one move earlier (5...0-0), getting maximum freedom in choosing the subsequent plans.
7.Bg5 c6 8.Qd2 0-0 9.d5
9...c5
Editor’s note:
This move is a big mistake that leads to a strategically lost game: by closing off the c-file, Tolush loses all chances of good counterplay and allows his opponent to fully concentrate on attacking the king. The correct move is 9...cxd5 10.cxd5 a6 or 9...h6 10.Be3 (10.Bxh6?! Nxe4! 11.Nxe4 Qh4+; 10.Bh4? Nxe4!) 10...cxd5 11.cxd5 a6, intending b7-b5 in both cases.
10.g4 a6 11.Ng3 Re8 12.h4 Qa5
This foray is too hasty. Black should have first finished his development with 12...Nf8 and Bd7.
13.Bh6 Nf8 14.h5 Qc7 15.Bd3 b5
Tolush launches a counter-attack. He hopes to create threats on the queenside after the pawn sacrifice.
16.0-0-0 b5xc4 17.Bb1!
After 17.Bxc4 Bd7 and Bb5, black gets counterplay. (
Editor’s note:
Actually, after 17.Bxc4 Bd7 18.Nf5!, white has a decisive attack, so black just has no time to get any counterplay. Instead of 17...Bd7, black has a stronger move 17...Bxh6 18.Qxh6 Kh8 with the subsequent Nf6-g8, even though his position is still very bad. However, it’s possible that Tal already thought that the light-squared bishop would be more useful for him on the b1-h7 diagonal.)
17...Bh8
Editor’s note:
Here too, 17...Bxh6 18.Qxh6 Kh8, with the idea Nf6-g8, was more resilient.
18.Rdg1
White’s artillery takes up position.
18...Rb8 19.Nf5!
The cavalry goes on the attack.
19...N6d7
Black shouldn’t accept the sacrifice: after 19...gxf5 20.gxf5+ Ng6 21.hxg6 hxg6 22.fxg6 white opens lines for a decisive attack.
20.Bg5 Bg7 21.Nxg7 Kxg7 22.Bh6+ Kg8
White already has a considerable positional advantage: the dark-squared bishop, an important defensive piece, has been eliminated, and the h-file is open for the attack. However, this advantage is not enough for a win in itself. With his next moves, white redeploys the necessary reserves to the kingside for a final blow.
23.f4!
To prevent the threat of 24.f5, black has to take the pawn.
23...exf4 24.Qxf4 Qd8! 25.hxg6
25.Qxd6 was bad. After 25...Rb6 and 26...Ne5, black fortifies his position.
25...Nxg6 26.Qh2 Nde5 27.Bf4
Editor’s note:
This move is a mistake that gives his opponent some chances to save the game. The strongest continuation was 27.Bd2! (not allowing the opponent to trade that bishop) 27...Nf8 28.Qh6, intending to get the knight to the kingside through d1 or e2. If necessary, the d2 bishop can move to c3, protecting the b2 pawn and immobilizing the e5 knight.
27...Nf8
As analysis subsequently showed, black could have put up more resistance with 27...Nxf4. After 28.Qxh7+ Kf8 29.Qh6+ Ke7 30.Qxf4 Kd7, white has got nothing concrete, while after 28.Qxf4 he would have to continue the attack without the important dark-squared bishop.
28.Qh6
(threatening
B
f4-g5-f6)
28...Neg6 29.Bg5 f6
30.e5!!
This pawn move is like a stick of dynamite that blows up black’s position. The bishop and the knight join the attack, and this swiftly decides matters.
30...Rxe5
After 30...fxg5, Tal intended to play 31.Bxg6 hxg6 32.Qh8+ Kf7 33.Rh7+!
31.Bxg6 Rb7
Black is desperately trying to defend a hopeless position. 31...hxg6 didn’t help due to 32.Qh8+ and 33.Rh7+!
32.Ne4! fxg5 33.Rf1 Rxe4
To prevent the threat 34.Nf6, black has to give up an exchange.
34.Bxe4 Rg7 35.Rf6 Bxg4
If 35...Ng6, then 36.Rxg6.
36.Rhf1 Nd7 37.Rxd6 Qe7 38.Rxa6 Kh8 39.Bxh7! Nb8 40.Bf5+ Kg8 41.Be6+! Bxe6 42.Rxe6
Tolush stopped the clock
after this move.
In the eight games against grandmasters, Tal got a brilliant result – six points. His last victory earned him the grandmaster’s title as well.
Several minutes later, applause rang out in the hall again: Bronstein had agreed to a draw with Kholmov. And so, Mikhail Tal became Soviet champion.
The thunderous ovation seemed unending. The spectators wouldn’t leave the hall for ages, celebrating the young player’s victory.
The next morning, the new champion’s room was full of journalists and newspaper photographers. Telegrams of congratulations poured in from many cities of the Soviet Union; most of them were from Riga, of course. Tal was very excited. When he saw me, he showed me one of them. It contained greetings from the Latvian Communist Party Central Committee.*
* Tal’s relations with the Central Committee would become a lot more complicated a few years later, as recounted in his first wife Sally Landau’s memoir
Checkmate! The Love Story of Mikhail Tal and Sally Landau
(Elk and Ruby, 2019, short-listed for the English Chess Federation Book of the Year prize) – publisher’s note
And then the routine days came, with mundane tasks and concerns. Tal had to finish his degree dissertation and then get a school internship as a literature teacher. He also had a lot of public duties: simultaneous displays at affiliated organizations, and lectures at factories, state entities and schools. He also had to find some time to read books and go to the theater and cinema. Should we add that the Soviet champion also had to train...
In summer 1957, Tal played in the students’ world team championship and European team championship, and in the autumn he took part in the Latvian players’ tour in Italy. In all these competitions, Tal posted the highest individual score.
The following two games were played in the European team championship.
No. 29, Ruy Lopez
M. Tal – R. Teschner
1957, Vienna, 1st European Team Championship, round 2
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 a6 4.Ba4 Nf6 5.0-0 d6 6.c3 Be7 7.d4
Editor’s note:
The main line here is 7.Re1.
7...b5 8.Bb3 Bg4 9.h3
Editor’s note:
In later games, the strongest players usually chose 9.Be3.
9...Bxf3 10.Qxf3
10...exd4
Black accepts the pawn sacrifice. However, the quiet 10...0-0 also allowed white great prospects in the center and on the kingside.
11.Qg3 g6
Editor’s note:
In most subsequent games with this line, black played 11...0-0. In this case, the main line is 12.Bh6 Ne8 13.Bd5 Qd7 14.Qg4! Qxg4 15.hxg4. The stats are catastrophic for black, but that doesn’t correlate with an objective evaluation of the position – analysis shows that black is only marginally worse.
12.Bd5 Qd7 13.Bh6 Rb8
The king voluntarily stays in the center and falls to a crushing attack. However, after 13...0-0-0 14.a4!, white’s attack was dangerous as well.
Editor’s note:
The computer shows interesting resources: 13...Rg8!? (with the idea 14...g5, and if 14.f4, then 14...Bf8 15.Bg5 Nxd5 16.exd5 Ne7) and 13...g5!? (capturing the pawn with the queen, let alone the bishop, loses to 14...Rg8) – in both cases, black is alright.
14.f4
Editor’s note:
The stronger move was 14.Re1!, to meet 14...Na5 (the move that could give black good chances to resist after 14.f4) with the simple 15.cxd4, not fearing 15...c6, because after 16.Bb3, the e4 pawn is still protected.
14...Nd8
Editor’s note:
14...Na5 still gave some defending chances. If white plays 15.Nd2 like in the actual game, then 15...Nxd5 16.exd5 Qf5 – the knight’s position on a5 gives black an important defensive resource Na5-c4.
15.Nd2 c6 16.Bb3 dxc3 17.Qxc3 Qa7+ 18.Kh1 Qc5 19.Qd3!
Threatening 20.Bg7 Rg8 21.Bxf6, then 22.e5 and Ne4.
19...Nd7 20.e5! d5
(a futile attempt to avoid opening the files)
21.f5!
Black can’t capture the e-pawn: 21...Nxe5 22.Qe2 f6 23.fxg6 hxg6 24.Bg7, and white’s attack is unstoppable.
Editor’s note:
Actually, 21...Nxe5 was the only way for black to stay in the game. Koblenz’s line is not dangerous for him at all: after the subsequent 24...Rh7 25.Bxf6 (25.Rxf6 Ndf7) 25...Bxf6 26.Rxf6 Re7, he’s alright. Instead of the erroneous 22.Qe2, the correct move is 22.Qg3, and after 22...f6 23.fxg6 hxg6 24.Bg7 Ndf7 (24...Rh7? 25.Rxf6 Rxg7 26.Qxe5+–, 24...Rh5? 25.Bxf6 Bxf6 26.Rxf6 Ndf7 27.Rxg6+–, 24...Rg8? 25.Bxf6+– or 25.Ne4+–) 25.Rae1 Qd6 26.Bxh8 Nxh8 27.Nf3 Nhf7 28.Bc2 Nxf3 29.Qxd6 Nxd6 30.Bxg6+ Kd7 31.Rxf3 Rg8, black has some good chances in the endgame.
So, instead of Tal’s 21.f5?!, it’s much better to play 21.Rac1, first pushing the black queen to a worse position (and preventing it from reaching d6, as in the previous line), and then getting a decisive attack in all lines: 21...Qb6 22.f5+–, 21...Qa7 22.f5+–, 21...Qb4 22.a3 Nc5 (22...Qa5 23.f5 Nxe5 24.Qg3+–) 23.Qe3 Qa5 24.f5+–.
21...gxf5 22.Qxf5 Nf8 23.Ne4! dxe4 24.Rac1! Qb6 25.Rcd1 Black resigned.
International master Rudolf Teschner lost the second game to Tal as well, which was a quieter one.
No. 30, Sicilian Defense
R. Teschner – M. Tal
1957, Vienna, 1st European Team Championship, round 5
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 Nf6 5.Nc3 d6 6.g3 g6
7.Bg2
Editor’s note:
If strong modern grandmasters go for this line, they usually place their hopes of getting an opening advantage in 7.Nxc6 bxc6 8.Bg2 or 7.Nde2.
7...Nxd4 8.Qxd4 Bg7 9.0-0 0-0 10.Qd3 Be6 11.Nd5 Rc8 12.c3 Re8 13.Be3 Qa5 14.h3
14...Qa4!
Tal wrote about this maneuver: “The most difficult move in the whole game. Black constrains white’s queenside pawns even more, puts pressure on the e4 square and prepares b7-b5-b4.”
15.Rfe1 b5 16.Bg5
It was better to simplify the position with 16.Nxf6+ Bxf6 17.Bd4 Bxd4 18.cxd4 Rc7, even though black still had an advantage after this move.
Editor’s note:
Judging by Koblenz’s last note, it’s clear that he thinks that the position after 15...b5 is better for black, but this evaluation is too optimistic. For instance, his line can be continued with 19.d5 Bd7 20.e5, and white is no worse. 16.Nb4!? is also worth considering, preventing the opponent’s possible queenside initiative (16...a5?? 17.b3 Qa3 18.Bc1+–) – there’s no reason to believe that black has an advantage. However, the game move should’ve led to equality as well.
16...Nxd5 17.exd5 Bf5 18.Qd2 b4 19.c4 Qc2!
Finishing the maneuver that was started on move 14.
After the queen trade, the bishops will put a lot of pressure on the opponent’s queenside.
20.Qxc2 Bxc2 21.Rac1 Bd3
22.Re3
Editor’s note:
White’s last move is a mistake and leads to a lost position; however, after 22.b3 Bb2 23.Rcd1 Bc2 24.Re2 Bf6 (after 24...Bxd1?! 25.Rxb2, only black is at risk due to big problems with the d1 bishop) 25.Rxc2 Bxg5 26.a4 bxa3 27.Ra2, the position was completely drawn.
22...Bxb2! 23.Rxd3 Bxc1 24.Bxc1 Rxc4 25.Bd2 Rb8! 26.Be3 a5 27.Rd2 a4 28.Bf1 Rc1 29.Rb2 Ra1 30.Kg2 a3 31.Rc2 b3 32.axb3 a2! 33.Be2 Rg1+ 34.Kxg1 a1=Q+ 35.Kg2 Rxb3 36.Rc8+ Kg7 37.Rc4 Qa8 38.Bd4+ e5
In this hopeless position,
white lost on time
.
Tal is often criticized for his excessively risky opening play. Indeed, he often cedes a dangerous initiative to his opponents when he plays black.
Let’s look at a game played in a match in Italy.
No. 31, Modern Benoni
E. Romani – M. Tal
1957, Reggio Emilia, Reggio Emilia-Riga Match
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 e6 3.Nc3 c5 4.d5 exd5 5.cxd5 d6 6.Nf3 g6
In this system, black has a weakness on d6. His opponent also gets a space advantage in the center. However, black has a chance to create counterplay with the long-range g7 bishop and queenside pawn advantage. Even though the common opinion is that this line is difficult for black, Tal often employs it in important tournaments. Such “balancing on the edge of the abyss”, where even the smallest mistake made by his opponent allows him to launch a swift counterattack, always attracted Tal.
7.g3
The game can get much sharper after 7.Nd2 Bg7 8.Nc4 0-0 9.Bf4 Ne8 10.Qd2.
In this case, black would’ve played 10...b6! For instance, 11.e3 Ba6 12.a4 Bxc4 (
Editor’s note:
Another popular move in this position is 12...f5!?, seizing kingside space, but a different move worth considering is 12...Nd7!?, as played in the game Chakravarthi – Mareco, Dubai 2018. After the subsequent 13.Be2 Bxc4 14.Bxc4 the white bishop got to c4, but a tempo down) 13.Bxc4 a6 14.0-0 Nd7 15.Rab1 f5 16.Bg3 Qf6 17.f4 Nc7 with the subsequent b6-b5 (
Editor’s note:
Later, this unclear position with mutual chances occurred in the games Borisenko – Suetin, Riga 1958, and Lomholt Hansen – Kretschmer, corr. 1983).
Tal once played a paradoxical move 10...Bxc3 (instead of 10...b6), and after 11.bxc3 b5 12.Nb2, at the cost of trading off the important dark-squared bishop, got an opportunity to push his pawn phalanx. (
Editor’s note:
Koblenz probably means the game Borisenko – Tal, Riga 1955; there followed 12...a5 13.e4 Qe7 14.Bd3 b4. But in his other games that reached the position after 12.Nb2, Tal played 12...Bb7 – see Lebedev – Tal, Riga 1955, and Antoshin – Tal, Leningrad 1956. By the way, the idea 10...Bxc3 11.bxc3 b5 found adherents among other strong players.)
7...Bg7 8.Bg2 0-0 9.0-0 a6 10.a4 Nbd7 11.Nd2 Re8
12.Nc4
12.a5 could be met with 12...b5 13.axb6 Nxb6 14.Nb3 Nc4! 15.Qd3 Rb8!, and white can’t implement his plan to block black’s queenside with Na5.
Editor’s note:
Instead of the inaccurate 15.Qd3, white usually plays 15.Ra4, but even then after 15...Nb6 16.Ra2 Nc4, black is alright, and most of the games that came to this position ended with three-fold repetition (17.Ra4 Nb6). We should also point out that today’s main line is 12.h3.
12...Nb6
Preventing white from increasing the pressure on the d6 pawn with 13.Bf4.
Editor’s note:
In modern practice, 12...Ne5 is more popular.
13.Ne3
Editor’s note:
In subsequent games, white usually played 13.Na3, which is considered the current main line in this position.
13...Ng4! 14.Qb3 Rb8 15.h3 Nxe3 16.Bxe3 Nd7 17.Ne4 Qe7 18.Bf4 Ne5
Now everything is ready to execute black’s main strategic idea: the b7-b5 pawn advance.
19.Qb6
A bad attempt to prevent b7-b5.
Editor’s note:
The best continuation was 19.a5, with a complicated position.
19...Nc4 20.Qb3 b5 21.axb5 axb5 22.g4
22.Nxc5 didn’t work because of 22...g5!
22...Bxb2
Editor’s note:
The correct move is 22...f5! 23.Nxc5 g5 24.Bh2 Nd2, winning an exchange – there’s no good compensation for white.
23.Nxd6
Editor’s note:
The unexpected 23.Ra5!? looks interesting – after both 23...Nxa5 24.Qxb2 and 23...Bd7 24.Ra7, white has a considerable initiative for the material.
Both players have executed their plans. Black has gained a pawn advantage on the queenside, and white likewise in the center. In the subsequent stage of the game, tactics come to the fore.
23...Bxa1 24.Nxc4
24.Nxe8 is worse because of 24...Be5!
24...Ra8 25.Nb6
The tempting 25.Qxb5 Qxe2 26.Bf3 was refuted by the spectacular 26...Ba6!!. (
Editor’s note:
Instead of 26.Bf3, the correct move is 26.Qxc5 with enough compensation for the exchange. However, black’s play can be improved as well: instead of the mistake 25...Qxe2, he had 25...Ba6 and 26...Qxe2 with an advantage.)
25...Ra5 26.d6 Qa7 27.Bc6 Be6 28.Qc2
Editor’s note:
28.Qf3! gave adequate compensation for the exchange, keeping the possibility to meet Nd5 Bxd5 with Qxd5; also, the white queen can get to f6 from f3 in some lines. For instance: 28...Rd8 (after 28...Rb8? 29.Nd5 Bxd5 30.Qxd5, black faces huge problems because of the threat d6-d7) 29.Nd5 Bg7 30.Bg5 Rxd6 31.Nf6+ Kf8 (31...Bxf6?? 32.Qxf6+–, 31...Kh8?! 32.Qg3!, threatening both Qxd6 and Qh4) 32.Nxh7+ Kg8 33.Nf6+ Kf8 34.Nh7+ with perpetual check. However, after the weaker game move white still has some chances to save the game.
28...Rb8 29.Nd5 Bxd5 30.Bxd5 Ra4 31.e3
31...Bg7
Editor’s note:
31...Bf6 is stronger, because after the subsequent 32.Rd1 c4 white can’t play 33.d7 due to 33...Rd8. However, if the bishop is on g7, it’s all different (see the next editor’s note).
32.Bc6?
(32.
R
d1 was better)
Editor’s note:
Analysis of the position after 32.Rd1 shows that in that case white had enough compensation for the exchange to draw the game. Unlike the aforementioned line 31...Bf6, black can’t now play 32...c4 due to 33.d7 – in this case, 33...Rd8 loses due to 34.Qe4! Qxd7 35.Bxf7+ (the rook on d8 is unprotected – 35...Qxf7 36.Rxd8+).
32...Qb6 33.Bd5 Rxf4! 34.exf4 Qxd6
The dangerous d6 pawn is eliminated, and the black pawns can finally march forward.
35.Qe4 Rd8 36.Bc6 b4 37.Ba4 Qd4! 38.Qe7 Bf6 39.Qb7 Qxf4 40.Bb3 c4 41.Bc2 Be5 42.Qg2 b3 43.Bb1 c3 White resigned.
December 1957 came. The names of the semi-final winners became known, and we were able to study the games of our future opponents, their strengths and weaknesses, and plan the championship strategy accordingly.
In the week preceding the 25th Soviet championship, we played several training games. I’ll allow myself the pleasure of printing two of them.
No. 32, Dutch Opening
A. Koblenz – M. Tal
1957, Riga, Training Match, round 1
1.d4 e6 2.c4 f5 3.Nc3 Nf6 4.g3 Be7 5.Bg2 0-0 6.Nf3 d6 7.0-0 Qe8 8.Re1! Qg6 9.e4 fxe4 10.Nxe4 Nxe4 11.Rxe4 Nc6
11...Qxe4? 12.Nh4
12.Re3
At first glance, this rook retreat is illogical because it blocks the dark-squared bishop. However, as will soon become clear, the rook performs important functions on the third rank.
Editor’s note:
This move hasn’t found much traction at grandmaster level. The current main line is 12.Qe2.
12...Bf6
12...e5 is bad because of 13.dxe5 Bg4 14.exd6 Bxd6 15.c5! Be7 16.b4! Bf6 17.Bb2! It’s easy to notice that the rook defends the knight on f3.
13.d5 exd5 14.cxd5 Ne5
Editor’s note:
The aggressive 14...Nb4!? looks like a good alternative.
15.Nxe5 Bxe5 16.Rb3
That’s the reason behind 12.Re3. Now, after the natural 16...b6 17.Be3, white will play 18.Rac1 and create strong pressure along the c-file.
16...Bf5!
Launching an interesting counter-attack. Black isn’t going to defend the b7 pawn!
17.Rxb7 Bc2! 18.Qd2
The dark-squared bishop is stuck on c1 and will now hinder white’s queenside development.
Editor’s note:
The moves 18.Qe1 and 18.Qe2 were worth considering.
18...Rae8 19.Rxc7 Bd3
Black avoids the trap: 19...Bd4 20.Qxd4 Re1+ 21.Bf1 Bd3 22.Bh6!!.
20.Qb4 a5! 21.Qa4 Bxg3!
21...Rxf2 22.Kxf2 Qf6+ gave black nothing due to 23.Kg1 (but not 23.Bf3 Bd4+ 24.Kg2 Bf1+) 23...Bd4+ 24.Be3.
22.hxg3 Re1+ 23.Kh2 Be4! 24.Be3
Of course, not 24.Bxe4 due to a mate in two: 24...Qh5+ 25.Kg2 Qh1#.
24...Qh5+ 25.Bh3 Rxe3!
26.Rxg7+!
This rook counter-sacrifice saves white from losing. 26.fxe3 immediately lost to 26...Qe2+.
26...Kxg7
If 26...Kh8, then 27.Qd4! Rxf2+ 28.Kg1, and the discovered check threat is decisive.
Editor’s note:
In Koblenz’s line, instead of 27...Rxf2+, black should play 27...Rd3!, and the computer lines show that after both 28.Qxe4 Rd2 29.Rc7 Rdxf2+ 30.Kg1 Qxh3 and 28.Rxh7+ Kxh7 29.Qxe4+ Qg6, the game ends in a draw.
27.Qd4+ Kg8 28.Qxe3
The queen finally joins the defense, but black still has the initiative.
28...Bf5 29.g4 Bxg4 30.Rg1 Rxf2+!
Another rook sacrifice!
31.Kh1! Qxd5+ 32.Bg2 Qd2 33.Qxd2 Rxd2 34.Bh3 h5 35.Bxg4 hxg4 36.Rxg4+ Kf7 37.Rg2 Draw.
In the next game, our purpose was to check a line in the Sicilian Defense that Tal intended to use in the championship. I was his opponent.
No. 33, Sicilian Defense
M. Tal – A. Koblenz
1957, Riga, Training Match, round 2
After
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 Nf6 5.Nc3 d6 6.Bg5 e6 7.Qd2 Be7 8.0-0-0 0-0 9.Nb3
Editor’s note:
The modern main line is 9.f4
9...Qb6 10.f3 a6 11.g4 Rd8 12.Be3 Qc7 13.h4 b5 14.g5 Nd7 15.g6!
, the following position occurred.
15...hxg6
After capture by the f-pawn, white still has good prospects.
Editor’s note:
Subsequent practice shows that 15...fxg6 is the only correct move. After 16.h5 gxh5 17.Rxh5 Nf6, white’s compensation is substantial, but this doesn’t mean it will necessarily bring an advantage.
16.h5 gxh5 17.Rxh5 Nf6 18.Rh1 d5! 19.e5!
Editor’s note:
There’s a much simpler and quieter continuation shown by Timman: 19.Bf4! Bd6 20.Bxd6 Qxd6 21.f4 with the ideas 22.e5, 22.Qh2 or 22.Bd3 — white’s position is won.
19...Nxe5 20.Bf4
Editor’s note:
This is a mistake: for some reason, white forces the black bishop to go to d6, where its position is quite good, with the black king getting a good escape square e7 in the process. The correct move was 20.Qh2 Kf8 21.Qh8+ Ng8 (Burgess) 22.Bd4!, and, even though great complications arise, white is ultimately able to prove that his attack is decisive.
20...Bd6 21.Qh2 Kf8 22.Qh8+
Editor’s note:
The prophylactic 22.Kb1 is better, even though after 22...Ke7 white still has to prove that his activity is worth the two missing pawns.
22...Ng8
22...Ke7 was stronger, but I intended to lure the queen to h8 and get it out of play. (
Editor’s note:
22...Ke7 wasn’t just “stronger” – it led to a won position; whereas, after the game move, white’s compensation is adequate.)
23.Rh7 f5 24.Bh6 Rd7 25.Bxb5! Rf7
Editor’s note:
The only move was 25...Ng6. For instance: 26.Nd4 (also the only move) 26...Re7 27.Bxg7+ Rxg7 28.Bd7!! Qxd7 29.Nxe6+ Qxe6 30.Qxg7+ Ke8 31.Nxd5 (up until now – analysis by Burgess) 31...N8e7 32.Nf6+ Kd8 33.Rhh1 Bb7 34.Rhe1 Ne5 35.f4 Nd3+! 36.cxd3 Qxa2 37.Qf8+ Kc7 38.Rxe7+ Kc6 39.Qg7 Qa1+ 40.Kc2 Qa4+ 41.Kc1 Qa1+ with perpetual check.
26.Rg1 Ra7
Tal wrote about this position in the
Sahs
magazine: “...The white pieces aren’t positioned too well. The g7 square – the key to black’s position – bends, but doesn’t break. If black can bolster his position and launch an attack, then white will only manage to send a consolation telegram to his king. So white has to play very energetically.”
27.Nd4
Editor’s note:
As Burgess pointed out, white won with 27.Bxa6! Bxa6 (27...Rxa6 28.Rgxg7+–; 27...Nxf3 28.Nb5+–) 28.Nd4 Bc8 29.Ndb5+–.
27...Ng4!
The tempting 27...Qb6 was met with the brilliant 28.Bd7!
28.fxg4 Be5
Editor’s note:
The correct move is 28...Bf4+, for instance, 29.Bxf4 Qxf4+ 30.Kb1 Qxd4 31.Rgh1 Rfe7 32.gxf5 (32.g5? Qg4 with a clear advantage for black) 32...exf5, and white has nothing decisive (according to the computer, the position is equal), or 29.Kb1! Bxh6 30.g5 Bxg5 31.Rxg5 Qf4 32.Rgh5 Rfc7 33.Nce2 Qf1+ 34.Nc1 axb5 35.Rh1 Qc4 36.R7h4 e5 37.Ne6+ Bxe6 38.Rxc4 dxc4 with good drawing chances.
Black is a piece down, but his opponent has three pieces en prise!
29.Nc6!!
Editor’s note:
29.Nf3!! is a stronger move, with the decisive threat Nf3-h4-g6+.
29...Bxc3
With a futile hope of launching a counter-attack after 30.bxc3 axb5 31.Nxa7 Qxa7. (
Editor’s note:
After the subsequent 32.Rh1 black still can’t save the game, but instead of 31.Nxa7?!, white wins immediately with 31.gxf5 exf5 32.Rgxg7 Rxg7 33.Nxa7+–.)
30.Be3! d4 31.Rgh1!
Threatening 32.Qxg8+! and 33.Rh8#.
31...Rd7 32.Bg5!
Editor’s note:
This move gives the opponent an opportunity to defend. The correct move was 32.gxf5 (Burgess) or 32.g5 (threatening 33.Qxg8+ Kxg8 34.g6+–) 32...Qg3 33.g6! Qxg6 34.Ne5 — with a decisive attack in both cases.
32...axb5 33.R1h6!
It’s over – black can’t defend the g7 square.
After
33...d3
Editor’s note:
As Burgess pointed out, after 33...Qxc6 34.Rf6+ gxf6 35.Bh6+ Rg7 36.Bxg7+ Ke7 black could still save the game.
34.bxc3 d2+ 35.Kd1 Qxc6 36.Rf6+ Rf7 37.Qxg7+ Black resigned.
A Ticket to the Interzonal
In January 1958, the participants of the 25th Soviet championship arrived in Riga. The Science Palace became the arena for the national battle for the gold medal. Further, four representatives of the Soviet Union would qualify for the Interzonal – a world championship cycle tournament.
The decision to hold the jubilee championship in Latvia’s capital was met with enthusiasm from our chess players. Riga is a city with a great chess tradition. The outstanding grandmaster Aron Nimzowitsch lived there, while the stunningly beautiful endgame studies of Hermanis Matisons were created here – he was the first Latvian chess master. Riga is the birthplace of an original opening that was named the Latvian Gambit (its inventor was Karlis Betins). Latvian chess players won many correspondence matches against other European cities. However, all these successes were the result of individual enthusiasts’ efforts. The mass chess movement in bourgeois Latvia was an unattainable dream. Only in Soviet Latvia would chess finally achieve its deserved place in the republic’s cultural life.
The first round. All participants are full of hope and in a combative mood.
On the third hour of play, the first applause sounded: Tal had started the championship with a win! The arbiters had a hard time trying to calm the spectators down and restore order.
The critical position occurred after move 18.
No. 34
M. Tal – A. Tolush
1958, Riga, 25th Soviet Championship Final, round 1
Tolush expected his opponent to exchange queens and settle for a draw. He seemed to underestimate Tal’s next move.
19.Be5! Qb5
Editor’s note:
The move 19...a5!? looked good — it both activated the rook and prevented b4-b5, which could have both created a foothold on c6 for the knight and fixed the a-pawn in place. After, for instance, 20.bxa5 bxa5 21.Rd4 a4 22.Bc2 a3 23.bxa3 Rxa3 24.Qb1 Bc6, black’s position is totally defensible.
20.Bxf6 gxf6
20...Nxf6 was relatively better. However, after 21.Qe7 Bd5 22.Bxd5 Nxd5 23.Qb7 Rd8 24.h4, the knight pin would be very unpleasant for black.
Editor’s note:
White’s extra pawn after the subsequent 24...Qd7! 25.Qxd7 (25.Qa6?! Qe7=) 25...Rxd7 26.Ne5 Rd6 27.b5 Kf8 28.Nc6 Rd7 29.Nb4 Nf6 30.Rxd7 Nxd7 31.Nc6 Ke8 32.Nxa7 Ke7 probably can’t be converted at all. The game move is the decisive mistake.
21.Qe4 Qxb4 22.Nd4 f5 23.Qe5! Ne7 24.Qf6 Bd5
After 24...Rf8, Tal prepared the following line: 25.h3 Bd5 26.Rd3 Bxb3 27.Rg3+! Ng6 28.Nxf5, and black can’t save the game.
Now, a beautiful finale ensues.
25.Nc6! Qxb3 26.Nxe7+ Kf8 27.Re1 Be6 28.Nxf5 Black resigned.
In round 3, Isaak Boleslavsky had black against Tal. Knowing that Boleslavsky liked to play the King’s Indian, Tal opted against his favorite 1.e2-e4 and started the game with the queen’s pawn move: he found a new strategic plan in a well-known KID line which gave white an advantage in the opening.
The novelty was successful – Tal won a pawn.
The diagram shows the position after black’s 19th move.
No. 35
M. Tal – I. Boleslavsky
1958, Riga, 25th Soviet Championship Final, round 3
20.Rg2
This move looks very strong. White activates the rook and threatens to win the d6 pawn. However, this seemingly logical move dulled Tal’s attention, and he overlooked the positional counter-threat.
20...b5!
The b-pawn movement wouldn’t have been as effective had white played 20.Rb1. In this case, 20...b5 would have been met with 21.b3.
20.Rg2 b5! 21.Nf3
As Boleslavsky showed after the game, white could have taken on b5. After 21.cxb5 Nxd5 22.Nxd6, he had two extra pawns. However, after 22...Nf4 or 22...Rad8, black seized the initiative. In case of 21.Nxd6 bxc4 22.Nxc4 (if 22.Bxc4, then 22...Rab8!) 22...Nxd5, white retained the extra pawn, but black still had dangerous counterplay. (
Editor’s note:
after Koblenz’s 22.Bxc4 Rab8, there’s 23.Rxg7!, and after 23...Kxg7 24.Re1! Nb6 25.Re7+ Kg8 26.Rxc7 Rbd8 27.Rxc5 Rxd6 28.b3 Rxf5 29.Ne2, the ending is drawn. However, instead of 22...Rab8, black could play 22...Ne5! or 22...Nb6!, and white’s position is difficult.)
The game move illustrates Tal’s creative credo very well: to activate his pieces, he gladly returns the extra pawn.
21...bxc4 22.Bxc4 Rab8 23.Rb1
Editor’s note:
It was better to try 23.Rag1 Rxb2+ 24.Ke1 Rxg2 25.Rxg2, for instance, 25...Nb6 (25...Rxf5?! 26.Nfg5 with enough counterplay) 26.Be2 Nbxd5 27.Nxd6 and only a slight advantage for black.
23...Rxf5 24.Nfg5 Ne5
25.Bb3?
A failed attempt to complicate play. As Boleslavsky correctly pointed out, after 25.Nxd6 Nxc4+ 26.Nxc4 Rxd5+ 27.Kc2, black had only a minimal advantage, and white could easily draw the game. (
Editor’s note:
Instead of 25...Nxc4, black had 25...Rf6!, and after 26.Nge4 Rf4 27.Re2 Nxc4+ 28.Nxc4 Nxd5, it’s not that simple for white to equalize.)
25...c4 26.Bc2 Nxd5 27.Nxd6
Editor’s note:
It was necessary to play 27.Nc3, declining to restore the material advantage, but forcing the trade of at least one pair of pieces, which made the white king’s position much more comfortable and brought some hope of saving the game.
27...Rf6 28.Nf5
(28.
N
de4 was better)
Editor’s note:
28.Nde4 was unlikely to save the game: after 28...Rff8, black has an overwhelming advantage because of dangerous piece activity and white’s exposed king.
28...Rd8 29.Ke1 Nf4 30.Rg3 Ned3+
The white king is suddenly caught in the crossfire of his opponent’s pieces.
31.Bxd3 Nxd3+
White’s position is critical. Tal then sacrificed the exchange with
32.Rxd3
, but this didn’t help. His opponent precisely converted his material advantage and deservedly won on move 51.
We analyzed this game for a long time, creating a “case history”.
In this game, Tal fell victim to following a standard framework; he couldn’t make himself delve into the concrete features of the position, which, I should say, was a rare occurrence for him. Tal was criticized for various things, but adherence to frameworks wasn’t one of them. His high-risk approach, on the other hand, the fact that his sacrifices were, as a rule, unsound, was often discussed. However, anti-positional lunges at his opponent’s position don’t actually feature in Tal’s tournament practice all that often – they were mostly seen in the return match against Botvinnik. In actuality, the overwhelming majority of Tal’s sacrifices brought him success for the simple reason that they were justified.
Let’s take, for instance, the game against Averbakh from the same championship. Grandmaster Averbakh represents the so-called classical school in chess, preferring quiet and clear positions. He feels less confident in sharp situations and often gets into time trouble. We decided to play Tal’s “patented” sharp line in the Modern Benoni.
No. 36, Modern Benoni
Y. Averbakh – M. Tal
1958, Riga, 25th Soviet Championship Final, round 4
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 e6 3.Nc3 c5 4.d5 exd5 5.cxd5 d6 6.e4 g6 7.Be2 Bg7 8.Nf3 0-0 9.0-0 Re8 10.Qc2
10.Nd2 was more precise. White likely didn’t anticipate that the Riga player would go for an unexpected combination just two moves later.
10...Na6 11.Bf4
The passive 11.a3 Nc7 12.Re1 Rb8 13.a4 (preventing b7-b5) 13...Na6 allowed black to occupy the b4 square.
11...Nb4 12.Qb1
12...Nxe4!!?
This move was very controversial. After looking at the position, Petrosian and Krogius smiled skeptically. After the game, they were firmly on the side of white, thinking that the sacrifice wasn’t correct. Bronstein, however, had a different opinion of this move. He wrote in the championship bulletin:
“Black’s position after white’s 12th move looks difficult from the strategic point of view. Tal gave 12...Nxe4 an exclamation mark and a question mark. I myself can’t help but give two exclamation marks to this move.
In such cases, the question whether the sacrifice is correct is not as important as the fact that Averbakh, the quintessential positional player, had to make a drastic turn in a completely different direction.
After 13.Nxe4 Bf5 14.Nfd2 Nxd5, white made a decisive mistake by playing 15.Bxd6 instead of the correct 15.Bg3.
It’s also interesting to note that Lilienthal thought that the sacrifice was incorrect, while Tal thought that chances were equal after 15.Bg3.
Such positions contain an unlimited number of possibilities, and the player who navigates the position better and calculates more quickly and precisely has more practical chances.”
Editor’s note:
12...Nh5!? looks interesting (and occurred in most subsequent games), intending f7-f5.
13.Nxe4 Bf5 14.Nfd2 Nxd5 15.Bxd6
A grave mistake. Black regains the piece and has a material advantage.
On the back of this blunder, most commentators came to the conclusion that the sacrifice wasn’t correct and was only justified with white’s “help”.
But let’s consider what would have happened after the strongest move 15.Bg3.
Tal intended to play 15...Qe7 16.Bb5 Bxe4 17.Nxe4 Qxe4 18.Bxe8 Qxe8 19.Bxd6 Qc6 20.Bg3 c4, and black has a pawn and queenside initiative for the exchange.
The position Tal aimed for isn’t to everyone’s liking, of course. The move 16.Bb5 is also not the only one – there was probably a stronger continuation.
Editor’s note:
Instead of 16.Bb5, white had 16.Bf3, with big trouble for black.
That said, analyzing the position in quiet conditions is one thing – you can move the pieces as much as you want and have all the time in the world. You can clarify a lot of things, find the right reply to a risky move, and reinforce your conclusion with a concrete line. Making a decision during a tense tournament game, when thinking time is strictly limited and when you’re facing a living opponent with their inherent strengths and weaknesses, is a whole other matter.
The player who is stronger in these difficult conditions, which are nevertheless equal for both players, will achieve success. That’s how we should evaluate committal decisions that cannot be fully evaluated during a tournament game.
The fact that Tal’s combination wasn’t a bluff was proved by Euwe, who found the move 15...Bh6 for black with the following lines:
a) 16.Bd3 Nb4 17.Re1 Nxd3 18.Qxd3 d5, and black wins;
Editor’s note:
Koblenz is wrong: white plays 19.Qxd5!, and, since 19...Bxd2 is bad because of 20.Nd6! Bxe1 21.Qxf7+ Kh8 22.Nxe8 Bxf2+ (22...Qxe8?? 23.Qf6+ Kg8 24.Be5+–) 23.Bxf2 Qxe8 24.Qxb7 with a positional advantage for white, black is forced to play 19...Qxd5 20.Nf6+ and 21.Nxd5, with a small advantage at the very most. But instead of 17...Nxd3?, black should play 17...Bxd2 18.Nxd2 Bxd3, getting two extra pawns and a won position.
b) 16.Qd3 Nb4 17.Qf3 (if 17.Qxd6, then 17...Bxd2 18.Qxd2 Qxd2 19.Nxd2 Rxe2) 17...Nc2, and white can’t avoid defeat;
Editor’s note:
In the line with 17.Qxd6, Euwe’s move 17...Bxd2 is a mistake because white wins after 18.Nf6+. The correct move is 17...Bxe4 18.Nxe4 Rxe4 19.Bf3 Rd4 20.Qxc5 Nd3 with an initiative.
c) 16.f4 Bg7, and white has no defense against numerous threats.
Editor’s note:
After the subsequent 17.Bb5, the position is totally unclear. However, the strongest reply to 15...Bh6 is 16.Bb5!, and after 16...Bxd2 17.Bxe8 Qxe8 18.Nxd6 (18.Qd1!?) 18...Bxb1 19.Nxe8 Bc2 20.Nd6, white gets an endgame with a minimal material advantage and some winning chances (Petersons – Khodos, Minsk 1964).
15...Nf6! 16.Bf3
Averbakh seeks to save the game with opposite-colored bishops, but the endgame is still far away. Black has a dangerous initiative.
16...Nxe4 17.Nxe4 Bxe4 18.Bxe4 Qxd6 19.Qc2 Re7 20.Bf3 Rae8 21.Rad1 Bd4 22.a4 b6 23.b3 Re5!
The rook is excellently placed here and can be transferred to the kingside for an attack.
24.Rd2 h5 25.Re2
White is trying to simplify the position with trades, but this doesn’t make defense any easier.
25...Rxe2 26.Bxe2 h4 27.Kh1 Qf4 28.g3 Qf6 29.Qd1 Rd8 30.Bg4 Bxf2! 31.Qe2
To attack the pinned bishop, white sacrifices a pawn, but he’s in for a surprise.
31...Rd2! 32.Qe8+ Kg7 33.gxh4 Qd4 34.Bh3 Qd3!
Editor’s note:
34...Qxh4–+ was stronger.
35.Bg2
After 35.Qe5+ Kh7 36.Qf6, black won with 36...Qd5+ 37.Bg2 Qxg2+ 38.Kxg2 Bxd4+ (
Editor’s note:
38...Bxh4+ is even better.)
35...Rd1! White resigned,
since the endgame after 36.Qb5 Qxb5 37.axb5 Rxf1+ 38.Bxf1 Bxh4 is hopeless.
Editor’s note:
The bishop endgame Koblenz ends his analysis with is most likely drawn – it’s probably impossible to create a passed pawn on the queenside with this pawn structure, and one passed pawn on the kingside is not enough for a win. Instead of 37...Rxf1+, black has a stronger move 37...Rd2, keeping the rooks, but the best continuation is 36...Rxf1+ 37.Bxf1 Qe4+ 38.Bg2 Qxh4 — with queens and opposite-colored bishops still on the board, white would have to fight off the passed pawns on both flanks and also defend his king. I think that black wouldn’t have too much difficulty converting his material advantage in this case.
When beginners overlook elementary threats, they are berated by their more knowledgeable friends. The game Tal – Korchnoi showed that even grandmasters are human.
No. 37
M. Tal – V. Korchnoi
1958, Riga, 25th Soviet Championship Final, round 9
White needs to settle for a draw: 33.Qf3 Qd5 34.Qf4 Qe5.
However, in time trouble Tal played
33.h6+? Rxh6 34.Qxh6+ Kxh6 35.g7 Qxg3+!
He overlooked this Zwischenschach and so had to
resign
on the spot.
This example is a small illustration on the subject of Tal’s “exceptional luck”, which was quite popular a few years ago. As we know, “good luck” and “bad luck” usually go hand-in-hand in chess.
The losses to Boleslavsky, Korchnoi and Anatoly Bannik didn’t break Tal’s will, but to get the ticket to the Interzonal, he now had to win a lot of games.
In round 10, Tal played Kotov. He won rather convincingly with subtle maneuvering in the opening and middlegame.
Against Taimanov, Tal went for an all-out attack. Repelling the first onslaught, Taimanov offered a draw, which was declined. The game was adjourned in a rook ending with Tal a pawn up, and after an eight-hour struggle, he won.
Seeing how persistent Tal was in trying to win the rook endgame, the Moscow journalist D. Ginzburg asked, “Why is Misha trying so hard? He has no chance for fourth place!”
In the game with Geller, Tal wanted to get the initiative at all cost. He sacrificed an exchange, then a full rook.
The people in the press center said indifferently, “Sow the wind, and reap the storm.” But the moves were sent to the press center with some delay; meanwhile, there was already applause in the hall.
What happened?
No. 38
M. Tal – E. Geller
1958, Riga, 25th Soviet Championship Final, round 14
When Tal made his 25th move, the Odessa grandmaster was standing near the arbiters’ table. Seeing the move on the demonstration board, he said, “If I take on f6 with the queen, the game is drawn, but I need to win!”
He headed to the board and grabbed the pawn with an energetic gesture.
25...gxf6?
Geller was absolutely right in his comment at the demonstration board: after 25...Qxf6 26.Qxf6 gxf6 27.d7, white’s passed pawn equalized chances. Now, however, black is losing.
25...gxf6? 26.Re7 Qxd6+
26...Qxe7 didn’t help due to 27.Qg4+.
27.Qxd6 Rxd6 28.Rxe1 Rd2 29.Rc1 Rxf2 30.Be4 Rxa2 31.Rxc4 a5 32.Rc8+ Kg7 33.Rc7 Black resigned.
This loss squashed Geller’s hopes of qualifying for the Interzonal. Even scrupulous analysis couldn’t refute Tal’s “incorrect” idea.
How can we explain black’s gross mistake in this game? I think that the main reason was that Geller wanted to win at all costs, regardless of the position.
Tal managed to improve his tournament standing somewhat after winning against grandmasters. However, he was sometimes too careless when he played against opponents who weren’t competing for the top places.
In his game against Furman, Tal teetered on the brink of catastrophe.
No. 39
M. Tal – S. Furman
1958, Riga, 25th Soviet Championship Final, round 16
White’s advantage is undoubtable. Furman also had less than two minutes for 12 moves. It seems that nothing can go wrong. However, Tal unexpectedly played the suicidal
29.g4?
He was mainly counting on his opponent’s time trouble. If black plays 29...Qh3, it’s met with 30.Qxe8+, while after 29...Qg6, there’s 30.Rf8+! Rxf8 31.Ne7+ Kh8 32.Nxg6+ and Rxf8+. However, Furman managed to find the right move.
29...Qh4
White lost an exchange, but everything ultimately ended in Tal’s favor: on move 38, Furman lost on time in a won position.
In the game against Krogius, Tal also had a bad position and only managed to draw after some help from his opponent.
Before the last round, Tal caught up with Petrosian. They led the table with 11.5 points each.
He had already clinched a ticked to the Interzonal, but the question of the new Soviet champion still remained open.
In the last round, the police had a lot of work on their plates. Hundreds of chess fans – those who failed to obtain tickets to the “chess palace” – gathered around the tall building. Only when the crowd was told that the key games would be broadcast in the Great Philharmonic Hall did they finally disperse.
The last round began.
Petrosian decided not to tempt fate and quickly agreed a draw with Averbakh.
The game Spassky – Tal became the center of attention. Tal played black and went for complications. He thought that he had better chances in a sharp struggle. Spassky played coolly and strongly. On move 23, Tal offered a draw in a roughly equal position, but Spassky declined. Just three moves later, Tal made a mistake, and his position quickly worsened.
The game was adjourned with a clear advantage for Spassky. Tal’s king was threatened by his opponent’s queen and rook.
No. 40
B. Spassky – M. Tal
1958, Riga, 25th Soviet Championship Final, round 19
That’s how the most gloomy analysis that we had ever carried out began... It continued until five in the morning. The more we studied the position, the worse the problems that we saw.
The resumption was in the morning. More than a thousand chess fans filled the playing hall. Others gathered at their TVs, eagerly awaiting the outcome of this captivating game.
48.Qf8+ Kf6 49.Qg7+ Ke7 50.Ra8! Qd7
Forced, because of the threat 51.Ra7+.
Editor’s note:
Analysis shows that after the game move, white could win with 51.Qh8!, preventing the black king from leaving e7. Afterwards, he strengthens the position with a kingside pawn storm, which ultimately creates the foundation for a decisive attack; in many lines, Qh8-b8 with the threat Ra8-a7 is an important resource.
So black had to play 50...Qf4+!, and only after 51.Kh3 Qf5+ 52.Kg3, play 52...Qd7. The principal difference is that now, with the king on g3, 53.Qh8 is met with 53...Qd6+ 54.Kh3 (if the king were on h2, white would have escaped to g1) 54...Qe6+ 55.g4 (or 55.Kh2 Rxg2+!) 55...Rh2+! 56.Kxh2 Qe2+ with perpetual check.
51.Qf8+? Kf6 52.Ra6+ Re6 53.Qh8+ Ke7 54.Ra8 Re1
Editor’s note:
The correct move is 54...h5!, pointed out by Kasparov. After this, white has no win.
55.Kg3
Editor’s note:
The computer wins with 55.Rc8! (white prevents the queen check from c7, that is very unpleasant in many lines, and threatens 56.g4), and meets 55...h5 with 56.f4!!, destroying the black king’s defense with f4-f5. However, the lines are very complicated.
55...h5
Preparing a safe haven for the king on f5.
Editor’s note:
This move again allows white to win the game. With 55...Qc7+ 56.Kf2 Re6, black equalizes: for instance, after 57.Qxh7 Qc2+ 58.Kg3 Re2, white has to go for perpetual check (59.Ra7+).
56.Kf2 Re6 57.Rc8
Editor’s note:
As D. Eschbach later found, white got a decisive attack with 57.Qb8! Kf6 58.g4!. The game move is weaker, but probably still doesn’t miss the win completely.
57...Rd6
Editor’s note:
This could have led to a quick loss, but analysis shows that even better moves were still inadequate: 57...Rc6 58.Qe5+ Re6 59.Qg5+ Rf6 60.Qc1! Qd6 61.Rc7+ Kd8 62.Rb7 Qf4 63.Qa1+– or 57...Rb6 58.Qf8+ Kf6 59.g4 Rb2+ 60.Kg3 Rb6 (60...hxg4 61.fxg4 Rd2 62.Rc3+–) 61.Qh8+ Ke7 62.Kf4 hxg4 63.Qf8+ Kf6 64.fxg4 Qd6+ 65.Qxd6 Rxd6 66.Re8+–.
58.Qf8+?
Vitaly Chekhover later showed in his analysis that white could win with 58.g4!.
Editor’s note:
Actually, this move doesn’t deserve a question mark: the g-pawn could have been pushed on the next move as well.
58...Kf6 59.Re8
Editor’s note:
This is a mistake. Kasparov’s 59.g4! was correct, with an irresistible attack for white.
59...Re6 60.Qh8+ Kf5 61.Qh6 Kf6 62.Qh8+
Editor’s note:
62.Rg8! still gave white winning chances (Kasparov).
62...Kf5 63.Rd8
It’s not too late to agree a draw, but Spassky only wants to win. (
Editor’s note:
The best move was 63.Qh6 Kf6 64.Rg8!, with the same position as after 62.Rg8!.)
63...Qc6! 64.Rc8?
The Leningrad grandmaster cracks under pressure. The correct move was 64.Kg3. Now Tal goes for a counter-attack.
Editor’s note:
This is not actually a mistake, Spassky missed the opportunity to draw only later.
64...Qa6 65.Kg3
Editor’s note:
This move doesn’t lose yet, but Spassky’s line 65.Qd8! Qe2+ 66.Kg3 led to an easier draw.
65...Qd6+ 66.Kh3 Re1
67.g3
67.g4+ hxg4+ 68.fxg4+ Kf4 69.Qh6+ was bad due to 69...Ke4! 70.Re8+ Kf3
Editor’s note:
Actually, 67.g4+ is the only move that allows white to save the game. In this line, instead of 69.Qh6+, the correct move is 69.Rc3!, and after the subsequent 69...Re3+ 70.Rxe3 Kxe3 71.h5 Kf4 72.h6 f6 73.Kg2 Qe7 74.Qb8+ Kg5 75.h7 Qxh7 76.Qb3 Qg8, there occurs a position that Kasparov also reached with a slightly different move order. However, if there were some doubts about its evaluation before, the computer now removes all of them: after 77.Qe3+ Kxg4 78.Qf3+, white draws.
67...Rg1
Editor’s note:
Not a good move. 67...Qa6 won immediately.
Only now Spassky offered a draw, but Tal declined.
68.f4 Re1! 69.Rc2
Editor’s note:
Kasparov’s recommendation 69.Re8! Rxe8 70.Qxe8 is more resilient, though it is still tough for white after 70...Qe6 due to his exposed d4-pawn.
69...Qe6 70.Rf2 Rh1+ 71.Kg2 Qe4+ 72.Rf3 Kg4 73.Qc8+ f5
, and
Spassky stopped the clock.
As soon as the sign “White resigned” appeared on the demonstration board, the spectators ran to the stage. The least disciplined ones hugged Tal, then started throwing him into the air.
The whole chess world learned the news: Mikhail Tal had won the Soviet championship for the second time.
Commenting on the “final of the final”, grandmaster Salo Flohr wrote in the
Ogonyok
magazine:
“‘This is only an accident’, the losers try to excuse themselves. But don’t they think that these ‘accidents’ happen quite regularly with Tal? How else can you explain the fact that Tal again scored the best result against the grandmasters (7/10)?
Let’s summarize: Mikhail Tal became the champion of the strongest chess country for the second time. Such great success can only be achieved by a talent as bright as the student from Riga.”
Not Fearsome to his Friends
This was proved in the 1958 Latvian SSR championship. Despite Tal’s huge reputation, his opponents in the republican tournament played him without fear. Many participants had played together with Tal in All-Union team tournaments or played against him in dozens of other tournaments. And so, they didn’t play against grandmaster Mikhail Tal, Soviet champion – they played against Misha Tal.
From the sporting point of view, Tal’s result was great – 16.5/19, however, his competitors Aivars Gipslis and Israel Zilber scored a half-point more, both defeating the Soviet champion.
The loss to Gipslis was a good lesson for Tal.
Before departing for the tournament hall, Tal started leafing through new chess magazines, and a line in the Sicilian Defense attracted his attention. He liked a novelty and decided to use it in an important game without even checking it. However, it turned out that the line was faulty.
Since then, the grandmaster has never used opening lines in important games without thoroughly checking them first.
No. 41
I. Zilber – M. Tal
1958, Riga, Latvian Championship, round 10
The other loss, to Zilber, is also very characteristic of Tal.
The diagram shows a complicated position with roughly equal chances. By playing 13...a6, black could have counted on some counterplay with b7-b5.
Editor’s note:
The position on the diagram occurred in many subsequent games. Black most often played 13...Be6 and usually met 14.Nxe6 with 14...Rxe6, planning to create pressure on the e4 pawn with Ra8-e8, for instance: 15.f4 Ned7 16.Bf2 Rae8 17.Qc2 Nh5 18.Rad1 Nc5 (Volzhin — J. Piket, Ohrid, 2001).
Koblenz’s recommendation 13...a6 is not good, because it’s almost impossible to create good conditions for b7-b5. For instance, in the game Ris — Bosch (Netherlands 2014), there followed 14.Rad1 c5 (after 14...b5?, there’s 15.cxb5, and the position is bad for black after both 15...axb5 16.f4 Nc4 17.Nxc6 and 15...cxb5 16.f4 Nc4 17.e5) 15.Nb3 Qb4? (black had to accept a difficult position after 15...Qc7 16.f4 Nc6) 16.Rxd6+–.
But Tal instead decides to sacrifice a piece.
13...Qb4 14.f4 Nxc4 15.a3 Qc5 16.b4 Qh5 17.Qxc4 Bxh3 18.Bf2 d5
Editor’s note:
The game move quickly loses, however, black didn’t have enough compensation in any case.
19.exd5 Bxg2 20.Kxg2 cxd5 21.Qd3 Rxe1 22.Rxe1 Ng4
Black had placed all his hopes in this position. Tal thought that white had to play 23.Nf3, and prepared 23...Qh3+! 24.Kg1 Qh1+! 25.Kxh1 Nxf2+ and 26...Nxd3.
However, Zilber played
23.Bg1!
This prosaic bishop retreat refutes black’s combination.
Black resigned
on move 54.
This prompts the question: perhaps, when playing a direct competitor, it’s better to avoid risky continuations and quietly wait for events to unfold?
Such decisions depend on the player’s personality. If the player is bold and is not afraid to take risks, he has to follow his heart, even in the most important games.
In June, Tal traveled to Crimea for a holiday. We gradually started preparing for the Interzonal. Then one day we got a phone call. A Soviet Chess Federation representative told us that Tal had been called up to the students’ national team, and he should head to Bulgaria.
Tal said goodbye to his new Crimean friends. As he boarded the plane to Varna, he felt like the happiest man alive...
The Soviet student team leader, Misha achieved a great result – 8.5/10.
On the Adriatic Sea
A few weeks after the student Olympiad, the Interzonal tournament opened in the Yugoslavian resort of Portoroz. This was an event eagerly awaited by chess players in all countries.
Previously, Tal had only played in international team events. Now, however, he had to play in a qualifying tournament for the world championship title.
Portoroz – a quiet town with five hotels and a few bars – completely transformed itself in those days. Flags of the countries whose representatives played in the tournament were displayed on the buildings facing the sea, while shop windows dazzled passers-by with bright posters and photos of the players. The grandmasters and masters fell victim to the autograph hunters. They were pestered everywhere – on the streets, in restaurants, even on the beach.
Tal defeated the Colombian player De Greiff in the first round and faced the renowned Hungarian grandmaster Laszlo Szabo in the second. Szabo played an unassuming opening and offered a draw on move 12. Tal declined the draw, and the battle intensified.
No. 42
L. Szabo – M. Tal
1958, Portoroz, Interzonal Tournament, round 2
23...Kh7!
Editor’s note:
The purpose of this prophylactic move will become clear three moves later.
24.h3 a6
Editor’s note:
Black is planning Nc6-a7-b5, attacking the weak c3 pawn.
25.Qb6 Ne7 26.Qd6 Qe8!
Editor’s note:
If the black king were on g8 right now, white would have captured the e6 pawn with check. Now, however, 27.Qxe6?? is met with 27...R7c6, trapping the queen.
27.Rb6 R4c6 28.Rxc6 Qxc6 29.Rb1 Rd7
Black’s intentions are clear – he wants to drive the opponent’s queen away from its active position.
30.Qb4
White avoids the queen trade, because after 30.Qxc6 Nxc6, black threatened Na7-b5 and Rc7-c4.
30...Nc8 31.g4 g6 32.Qf8 Rg7 33.Kf2 Qc4! 34.Rb2
Editor’s note:
The correct move was 34.gxf5. Black can’t play 34...gxf5, because after 35.Rg1! Rxg1 36.Kxg1 he even loses due to the threats Qf8–f7+ and Bd2–e1–h4. Meanwhile, after 34...exf5, the e5 pawn becomes passed, which gives white enough counterchances. The following line is possible, for instance: 35.Ke3 Na7 36.Qb4 Qc7 (36...Qxb4? 37.Rxb4 Nc6 38.c4!! Nxb4 39.Bxb4 dxc4 40.d5+–) 37.c4 dxc4 38.d5 g5 39.Qb6 Qd7 40.Qxa7 Qxd5 41.Qd4 gxf4+ 42.Qxf4 Rg2 43.Qxf5+ Kg7 44.Be1 Rg5 45.Qf6+ Kg8 46.Rd1 Rxe5+ (or 46...Qxd1 47.Qe6+, and the king can’t hide from the pursuit) 47.Kf2 Rf5+ 48.Kg1 Rg5+ 49.Kf2 Qg2+ 50.Ke3 Qxh3+ 51.Ke2 Qh5+ 52.Ke3, and black only has perpetual check.
34...Na7
35.Rb6
Editor’s note:
It was necessary to go for the endgame with 35.Qc5 (perhaps after trading on f5 first) 35...Qxc5 36.dxc5, and even though white is clearly worse, he still has some chances to save the game.
35...Nc6 36.Ke1 Qd3! 37.g5 hxg5 38.fxg5 Nxa5 39.Rxe6 Nc4 40.Bc1 Qxc3+
41.Kd1
There was an opinion that white could put up more resistance with 41.Ke2. However, this could be met with 41...Qxc1 42.Re8 Qe3+ 43.Kf1 Nd2+ 44.Kg2 Qxg5+ 45.Kh1 Kh6.
41...Qxd4+ 42.Ke2 Qe4+ 43.Kd1 Qf3+ 44.Kc2 Qe2+ 45.Kc3 d4+ 46.Kb4 a5+ 47.Kc5 Rc7+ 48.Kb5 Nxe5+ 49.Kb6 Rc6+ White resigned.
The third round game Tal – Pachman was aptly described by grandmaster Svetozar Gligoric:
“After every knockdown punch, Pachman would get up before the referee reached the count of ten. His opponent had to start anew each time, until he, too, was out of strength.”
The opponents agreed to a draw in a rook ending on move 50.
Intense emotions awaited Tal in the fourth round. His game against Aleksandar Matanovic was adjourned in the following position:
No. 43
A. Matanovic – M. Tal
1958, Portoroz, Interzonal Tournament, round 4
We thought that Matanovic had sealed the move 41.bxa4. It was objectively the strongest one. A long search for any way to save the game gave us nothing – the position was lost. There was no time to study other continuations in detail.
However, at the resumption we discovered that our opponent had sealed a weaker move,
41.b4
Editor’s note:
It seems that the game move is also enough for a win.
41...Ne3 42.Re6!
But not 42.Kc3 due to 42...a3 43.b5 Nd1+ 44.Kd4 Bb7.
Editor’s note:
Koblenz’s line requires some corrections. Instead of 44.Kd4??, the right move is 44.Kb4, and white wins. So it’s necessary to change the move order to 42...Nd1+! (instead of the erroneous 42...a3) 43.Kd4 a3!, and after 44.Kxe4 (but not 44.b5?? Bb7, and the position from Koblenz’s note occurs, which is won for black) 44...a2 45.b5 a1=Q 46.Ra6+ Qxa6 47.bxa6 Kxa6 it’s a draw.
42...Nxc4+ 43.Kc3 Bd5 44.Rxg6 Nb6 45.h4 a3 46.b5
Editor’s note:
This move is a mistake that could have cost Matanovic half a point, whereas the h-pawn advance could have decided the game in just a few moves: 46.h5 Bf7 47.Rg5 (but not 47.Rg7? Na4+ 48.Kc2 a2 49.Rg1 Bxh5 50.Kb3 Nb6 51.Kxa2 Nd5=) 47...Na4+ 48.Kd4 Kb6 (48...a2 49.Ra5++–) 49.h6 a2 50.Rg1 Bg8 51.Ra1+–.
46...Bf7 47.Rg1
Here Tal thought for a while and... made a losing move.
“I was thinking about the line 47.Rg7 Na4+ 48.Kc2 a2,” he said after the game. “I liked this pawn spurt so much that I automatically grabbed the pawn after my opponent’s move. And so, I had to make this horrible move. After 47.Rg1, I planned to play 47...Nd5+ 48.Kb3 Kb6, with good drawing chances.”
After
47...a2?, the Yugoslavian grandmaster won.
Tal’s next opponent was the Czechoslovakian grandmaster Miroslav Filip. I knew that, after a loss, Tal would surely try and bounce back with a win, so I feared that in the game against such a careful, mature player as Filip, the desire to force matters at any cost may lead to a catastrophe.
No. 44
M. Tal – M. Filip
1958, Portoroz, Interzonal Tournament, round 5
While Tal was thinking over his position, Petrosian’s second, grandmaster Efim Geller, leaned in to me and said, “It seems that Misha can’t do much this time!”
I realized that it wasn’t easy to explode black’s solid position, but I wanted to object in some way, so I answered Geller, “If Misha has an open line, there’ll be a mate!”
At that moment, white sacrificed a bishop on h6.
28.Bxh6!
“After this move,” Matanovic wrote, “white could have faced some problems, but we can’t deny that he only won because of this sacrifice. We should respect his boldness!”
Editor’s note:
Actually, even the computer agrees that Tal’s bishop sacrifice was the strongest move. Other continuations are much weaker: 28.Bc1?? Rxd1 29.Rxd1 Nxe4–+
or 28.Be3? Rxd1 29.Rxd1 (29.Bxd1 Bxe3 30.Qxe3 Rd3–+) 29...Bxe3 30.Qxe3 Nh5, with the subsequent Nh5-f4 and an overwhelming position.
28.Bxh6! gxh6 29.Qxe5 Be7 30.Rd4 Rxd4 31.cxd4 Kh7 32.Rd1 Ne8?
It was better to play 32...Qg7!, and if 33.Qxe7, then 33...Rg8.
Actually, Tal planned to meet 32...Qg7 with 33.Qf4 Rg8 34.Qf3, with roughly equal chances.
33.f6!
The bishop joins the attack, and black’s position quickly deteriorates.
Editor’s note:
Actually, black could have saved the game here had he played 33...Bxf6 — after the subsequent 34.Qf5+ Kg8 (34...Kh8? 35.e5+–) 35.Rg1+ (or 35.e5 Qe7 36.Qh7+ Kf8 37.Rg1 Qe6 38.Rg8+ Ke7 39.Qxh6 Qd5+ 40.Kg1 Bg7 41.Qg5+ Kd7 42.Bf5+ Kc7 43.Qe7+ Kb6 44.Rxe8 Rxe8 45.Qxe8 Qxd4 46.Qxf7 Bxe5 with a drawn endgame) 35...Bg7 36.e5 Qb4 37.e6 fxe6 38.Qxe6+ Kf8 39.Qf5+ Ke7 40.Qe4+ Kf7, white only has perpetual check.
33...Nxf6 34.Qf5+ Kh8 35.e5 Qg7 36.exf6 Bxf6 37.Rg1 Bg5 38.f4 Black resigned.
In the following rounds, Tal had to settle for a draw three times. Especially frustrating was losing half a point against the Filipino player Cardoso, who was last in the tournament at that point.
Against Gligoric, Tal managed to draw the game with ingenious and inventive defense, while in the game with international master Oleg Neikirch, Tal didn’t dare to decline the draw offer in a slightly worse position.
The peacefulness of the calm, always smiling master from Sofia was boundless.
“I can’t play against a fifteen year-old boy. If I win, people in Sofia will say that I bullied the kid. And if I lose, I’d be ashamed to return to Bulgaria” – that’s how Neikirch explained his draw against Bobby Fischer.
Master Geza Fuster performed quite badly in the tournament. In time trouble, he would lose drawn or even clearly won games.
The loss to Tal upset him the least of all of his defeats.
No. 45
M. Tal – G. Fuster
1958, Portoroz, Interzonal Tournament, round 9
17.Bxe6! fxe5 18.dxe5 Be7 19.Rhf1! Rf8 20.Rxf8+ Bxf8 21.Qf3
21...Qe7
Alekhine once said, “Such a pity that the execution of a beautiful plan always depends on your opponent as well!”
Here, Tal expected 21...Bc5 and had prepared 22.b4 Bxb4 23.Rd7 Qxd7 24.Bxd7+ Kxd7 25.Qb3 a5 26.a3 Bg8 27.Qb2, and black doesn’t get enough compensation for the queen.
22.Qb3
With 22.Bd7+ Qxd7 23.Rxd7 Kxd7 24.Qf7+ Be7 25.Qxg7, white could transition into a won endgame, but Tal decided to set a trap for his opponent.
22...Rb8? 23.Bd7+ Qxd7 24.Rxd7 Kxd7 25.Qf7+ Be7 26.e6+ Kd8
After 26...Kd6, there follows, of course, 27.Qf4+.
27.Qxg7 Black resigned.
After this win, Tal advanced to second place. The leader of the tournament, Tigran Petrosian, had a half-point more.
“Where did I make a mistake?” the Argentinian master Rossetto asked Tal after their game.
It was hard to answer this question. For a time, it seemed that Tal wanted to win so much that he lost the ability to assess the position objectively. At any rate, declining a draw offer while a pawn down really looked reckless.
“Without a doubt, the analysts will find where Rossetto could have played better, which move would have caused the most trouble for his inventive opponent, but this won’t render the young Soviet champion’s victory any less valuable,” grandmasters Gligoric and Matanovic wrote in the tournament book.
No. 46, King’s Indian Defense
H. Rossetto – M. Tal
1958, Portoroz, Interzonal Tournament, round 10
1.c4 Nf6 2.Nf3 g6 3.g3 Bg7 4.Bg2 0-0 5.0-0 d6 6.d4 Nc6 7.Nc3 a6 8.d5 Na5 9.Qd3 c5 10.e4 e6
Editor’s note:
The main line here is 10...Rb8, intending b7-b5.
11.h3 exd5 12.cxd5 b5
In times past, when the leading grandmasters were much stronger than masters, the tournament leader usually didn’t have a hard time against weaker players. The player with the higher title would usually get an advantage out of the opening.
In our days, the leaders have a much harder time: opponents from the lower half of the table sometimes put up very stiff resistance against the favorites.
Some grandmasters try to defeat the weaker players with a sit-and-wait strategy. They expect their opponent to make a subtle positional mistake at some point. In tournaments with “mixed” line-ups, such tactics allow them to compete for high places. High places, but not first place. To finish first, pure technique is usually not enough. Only a brave tournament fighter who’s not afraid to face dangers can compete for first place. That’s how Tal and Korchnoi play. Even in games against weaker opponents, they face danger without fear. Of course, such a playing style requires great strength and expense of energy.
13.Bf4 b4 14.Nd1 Re8 15.Re1 c4 16.Qc2 Ra7 17.Be3 Rae7
18.Nd2
White allows his opponent to launch a queenside operation. He’s hoping for the chance to disperse and eliminate the advance party that has ventured too far ahead of the main forces.
In this critical moment, Tal finds an interesting tactical resource.
18...b3!
Editor’s note:
The computer likes 18...Bd7!? more, planning Bd7-b5, not ceding the c3 square to white and retaining the possibility of playing c4-c3 in a number of lines. After the subsequent 19.Nxc4 Qc7 20.Rc1 Nxe4 21.Nb6 Nc5 22.Nxd7 Qxd7, the position is roughly equal.
19.axb3
Editor’s note:
19.Qc1!? was worthy of serious consideration, planning Nd1-c3 and not allowing his opponent the opportunity to carry out the tactical blow that happened on move 20. In this case, it’s hard for black to find ways to develop a good initiative, while white’s space and pawn advantage in the center might help him.
19...Nxb3 20.Ra4
20.Nxb3 cxb3 21.Qxb3 Nxe4 is better for black.
20...Nxd5! 21.exd5 Bf5 22.Qxc4 Nxd2 23.Qxa6
Black has seized the initiative after sacrificing a pawn, but the crucial events are still ahead.
23...Ne4 24.Qb6 Qc8
Tal decides not to trade queens: he needs his queen for a kingside attack.
25.g4 Nc5 26.Ra7
Editor’s note:
Perhaps 26.Ra3!? was stronger – after the subsequent 26...Bc2 (26...Nd3? 27.Rf1+–) 27.Qxd6 Nd3 28.Rf1, black must prove that his activity compensates for the lack of two pawns.
26...Rxa7 27.Qxa7 Nd3 28.Rf1 Be4 29.Qa3 Bxg2 30.Kxg2 Qc4! 31.Qxd6 Bf8 32.Qc6 Qe4+ 33.Kg1
Here, white could have saved the game with 33.f3 Qe5 34.d6 Bxd6 35.f4. But Rossetto already had too little time: his flag was hanging menacingly.
Editor’s note:
The game move actually doesn’t lose either.
33...Ne5 34.Nc3 Nf3+ 35.Kh1 Qe5 36.Kg2 Nh4+ 37.Kg1 Bd6 38.Rd1
Editor’s note:
White could have salvaged the game with 38.Bf4!, for instance, 38...Qxf4 (38...Nf3+ 39.Kg2 Qxf4 40.Qxe8+ Kg7 41.Rd1 Ne5 42.Kf1 Bc5 43.Rd2 doesn’t win either) 39.Qxe8+ Kg7 40.Rd1 Bc5 (after 40...Qf3?! 41.Kf1 Qxh3+ 42.Ke2 Qxg4+ 43.Kd3 the white king escapes the pursuit, and white retains all the extra material) 41.Ne4 Qf3 42.Qe5+ Kg8 (after 42...f6? 43.Qxf6+, only black can lose) 43.Nf6+ Kf8 44.Nd7+ with perpetual check.
38...Qh2+ 39.Kf1 Rxe3 White resigned.
Until round 14, Tal shadowed Tigran Petrosian, staying half a point behind the leader. In round 15, he caught up with the Moscow grandmaster, and on the next day, they switched places – Tal took the lead.
In round 17, Petrosian played Sanguinetti. After losing in the previous round, the grandmaster looked tired, but he played very coolly, gradually increasing his positional advantage.
Tal played against the Argentinian grandmaster Oscar Panno. At first, Tal watched his rival’s game carefully, but then the events of his own game had him in deep thought.
Panno, usually a quiet player, was unrecognizable in this game. He chose an original defensive system, creating a real storm on the board.
The game was still on, but I was already receiving consolations.
David Bronstein approached me first.
“Don’t be upset if Misha loses today,” he whispered to me. “You’ll see, he’ll bounce back for sure!”
“Panno has a whole bunch of pieces for his queen!” Vladimir Simagin, Yuri Averbakh’s coach, sighed.
“Could you tell me where Tal made a mistake?” a Yugoslavian journalist asked me as he passed by. “I’m sorry, I’m hurrying to the telegraph. Panno will win for sure, and there’ll be a long queue later...”
What happened in that game, anyway?
No. 47, Ruy Lopez
M. Tal – O. Panno
1958, Portoroz, Interzonal Tournament, round 17
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 a6 4.Ba4 Nf6 5.0-0 Be7 6.Re1 b5 7.Bb3 d6 8.c3 0-0 9.h3 Nd7
Editor’s note:
In modern practice, there are much more popular moves for black: 9...Na5, 9...Bb7 and 9...Nb8 (with the idea of Nb8-d7 and Bc8-b7).
10.d4 Nb6
Editor’s note:
In subsequent games, black usually played 10...Bf6 in this line.
11.Be3
Editor’s note:
The current main line in this position is 11.Nbd2.
11...exd4 12.cxd4 Na5 13.Bc2 c5
14.e5!
White has to play energetically, otherwise black will seize the initiative, for instance, after the quiet 14.Nc3 Nac4 15.Bc1 cxd4 16.Nxd4 Bf6.
Editor’s note:
The computer evaluates the situation differently: it says that white is slightly better in Koblenz’s line (the weakness on d6 is rather glaring, and the active c4 knight can be kicked away with b2-b3), while the game move leads to a roughly equal position.
14...dxe5!
The Argentinian player avoids the trap 14...Nac4 15.exd6 Qxd6, which Tal planned to meet with 16.Bg5!.
Editor’s note:
The position from Koblenz’s line is not dangerous for black: after the subsequent 16...Bxg5 17.Nxg5 h6 18.dxc5 Qxd1 (18...Qxc5? 19.Nh7! Nxb2 20.Qe2 drops an exchange, because black can’t play 20...Rd8 due to 21.Qe8+) 19.Bxd1 (19.Rxd1?! Nxb2) 19...hxg5 (black is all right after 19...Nd7 or 19...Nxb2) 20.cxb6 (or 20.b3 Bb7=) 20...Nxb2, the position is equal. However, instead of 15.exd6, white has a stronger reply 15.Qd3, and after 15...g6 16.exd6 Bxd6 (perhaps 16...Qxd6 is the lesser evil, even though it loses a pawn after 17.dxc5 Qxd3 18.Bxd3) 17.Bg5, white has a very dangerous initiative.
15.Nxe5 Nbc4 16.Qd3
Declining the draw — 16.Qh5 g6 17.Nxg6 fxg6 18.Bxg6 hxg6 19.Qxg6+ Kh8 – and willingly diving into complications that are hard to calculate completely.
16...f5
Editor’s note:
16...g6, intending Bc8-f5, was worthy of serious consideration.
17.Bb3! f4
Editor’s note:
This move is a mistake. Instead of it, Panno had a number of good resources, including the immediate 17...Nxb3, for instance: 18.Nc6 (or 18.Qxb3 Be6, and 19.dxc5 is bad due to 19...f4 with the subsequent 20...Bxc5) 18...Qd6 19.Nxe7+ Qxe7 20.axb3 Nxe3 21.Rxe3 Qg5, and black is no worse (22.dxc5? f4).
18.Bd2 Nxb3!
A great reply! After 19.Qxb3 Bf6 20.Nxc4 bxc4 21.Qxc4+ Kh8, white faces considerable trouble, while in the line that happened in the game, black gets more than enough compensation for the queen.
Editor’s note:
In Koblenz’s line, instead of the erroneous 20.Nxc4, white has a stronger reply 20.Na3, and analysis shows that after the subsequent 20...Be6 21.Naxc4, the position is equal.
19.Nc6 Nxa1 20.Nxd8 Bf5 21.Qf3 Raxd8 22.Rxe7 Bxb1 23.Bxf4
Editor’s note:
23.Be1! was a much stronger move, with black facing great difficulties due to his uncoordinated pieces, for instance: 23...Nc2 24.Qg4 Rf7 25.Qe6 Rdf8 26.Rxf7 Rxf7 27.dxc5, and, because of the dangerous passed c-pawn and weak a6 pawn (and if it falls, the b5 pawn will likely fall as well), black might not hold, or 23...cxd4 24.b3 Rfe8 25.Bb4 Ne5 26.Qb7 Rxe7 27.Qxe7 Nf7 28.Ba5 Rf8 29.Qe1 Bxa2 30.Qxa1 Bxb3 31.Qxd4 Ng5 32.Bb4 Re8 33.Qxf4 — white has a material advantage now, and it’s black who has to struggle for a draw.
23...Rxd4
In the opinion of the Yugoslavian theoretician V. Vukovic, black should have won after 23...cxd4, however, after 24.b3 Bg6 25.bxc4 d3 (threatening 26...Rxf4), white has the move 26.Qg4, and if 26...d2, then 27.Rd7 (
Editor’s note:
The computer insists on 27.Bxd2! Rxd2 28.c5 instead and proves that the passed c-pawn wins the game for white). After 26...Rxf4, there’s 27.Qxf4 d2 28.Rxg7+! Kh8 29.Rd7!, and white wins.
Editor’s note:
In the above line, instead of 26.Qg4, white has an even stronger move: 26.Qg3! d2 27.Bxd2! Rxd2 28.Qc3 Rd1+ 29.Kh2 Bf7 30.c5+–. But, at any rate, instead of the erroneous 24...Bg6, the correct move is 24...Nd6, for instance: 25.Re1 Be4 26.Qg3 Nc2 27.Bxd6 Nxe1 28.Bxf8 Rxf8 29.Qe5 Bxg2 30.Qxe1 Bxh3 31.Qe4 Rf6 (31...Rd8? 32.Qh4) 32.Qxd4 h6 with an obvious draw. But instead of 24.b3, white has an interesting resource 24.Qg3!? – after the subsequent 24...Bg6 25.b3 Nb6 26.Be5 Rd7 27.Rxd7 Nxd7 28.Bxd4 Nc2 29.Bb2 black can be satisfied from the material point of view, but the white pieces are very active, the g7 square needs protection, the c2 knight is placed very awkwardly, and the a6 pawn is very weak and will likely fall soon, so it’s only white who can play for a win here.
24.Qg4! Bg6 25.Qe6+ Bf7 26.Qf5 Nc2
The knight is stuck deep behind enemy lines and has to be brought back into play. After 26...Bg6, Tal intended to play 27.Rxg7+ Kxg7 28.Bh6+ Kxh6 29.Qxf8+ with the subsequent 30.Qxc5+ and Qxd4.
Editor’s note:
The game move wasn’t the only move; another good reply was 26...Rd5 27.Qe4 (27.Qb1?! Bg6 28.Qe1 Nc2 29.Qe6+ Bf7 30.Qxa6 Rd1+ 31.Kh2 Nd4 gives black dangerous counterplay and is risky for white) 27...Rd4 28.Qf5 Rd5, with repetition.
27.b3 Bg6
Editor’s note:
This move is a mistake. The correct reply was 27...Rd1+ 28.Kh2 Nd2 – analysis shows that the position is dynamically equal: 29.Bxd2 Rxd2 30.Qxc5, and then, for instance, 30...h6 (a useful luft) 31.Ra7 Re8 32.Rxa6 Nd4 33.Ra7 Ree2 34.Qc8+ Re8 35.Qc3 Ree2 36.Qc8+ Re8, with repetition.
28.Rxg7+
This sacrifice doesn’t win. However, black has to be very careful to repel the numerous threats.
Editor’s note:
Actually, black is already lost.
28...Kxg7 29.Bh6+ Kxh6 30.Qxf8+ Kg5 31.bxc4 bxc4 32.g3
Editor’s note:
This is a mistake. White could have won with both 32.g4, intending Kg1-h2-g3, and 32.Kh2, with the idea g3-g4 and Kh2-g3. For instance: 32.g4 Rd7 33.Kh2 Rf7 34.Qd6 Kh6 (34...Rxf2+ 35.Kg3 Rf7 36.Qe5+ Kh6 37.Kh4+–) 35.Qxc5 Kg7 36.Qe5+ Kg8 37.f4 Bd3 38.Qg5+ Kf8 39.Qd8+ Kg7 40.Qd6 a5 41.a4 Nb4 42.f5 – white will soon capture the a5 pawn and win the game with a combined pawn push on both flanks.
32...Be4 33.h4+ Kg4 34.Kh2 Bf5!
Had black retreated his bishop with 34...Bd5, he would have gotten checkmated after 35.f3+! Bxf3 36.Qc8+ Kh5 37.Qf5+.
There were only a few minutes left until time control, but Tal was in deep thought.
“Why is he thinking?” the spectators murmured. He seemed to have only one move: 35.f3+. But Tal played a different move.
35.Qf6!
After 35.f3+? Kxf3 36.Qxf5+ Ke3 37.Qxh7 c3, it’s white who has to scramble for a draw now!
Editor’s note:
Analysis leaves no doubt that the position resulting from Koblenz’s line is indeed won for black.
35...h6 36.Qe5 Re4 37.Qg7+ Kf3 38.Qc3+ Ne3
With 38...Kxf2 39.Qxc2+ Kf3, black could have easily drawn. At this moment, both players were completely exhausted. Drops of sweat ran down Panno’s face. And, as the reader can see, there was a reason for this exhaustion!
39.Kg1! Bg4 40.fxe3 h5 41.Qe1!
41...Rxe3
The decisive mistake. Black could have drawn with 41...Re6!. (
Editor’s note:
One of the possible lines after 41...Re6! is, for instance: 42.e4 c3 43.Qxc3+ Kxe4 44.Qxc5 Kd3, and, no matter what the computer says, there’s a fortress on the board.)
The game was adjourned here. Here’s how it ended on the resumption day:
42.Qf1+ Ke4 43.Qxc4+ Kf3 44.Qf1+ Ke4 45.Qxa6 Kd4 46.Qd6+ Kc4 47.a4 Re1+ 48.Kf2 Re2+ 49.Kf1 Ra2 50.Qa6+ Kd4 51.a5 c4 52.Qb6+ Kd5 53.a6 Ra1+ 54.Kf2 c3 55.a7 c2 56.Qb3+ Kd6 57.Qd3+
, and
black resigned
, because he can’t take the a7 pawn without losing his rook.
This game shows just how energetic and inspired Tal’s play was in double-edged positions.
While Tal’s theoretical knowledge, endgame technique and persistence in defense are not superior to that of other leading grandmasters, his dynamic style, rich imagination and quickness of calculation undoubtedly make him stand out even among the most renowned chess players.
Tal is able to create more and more difficulties for his opponents. Sometimes, his opponent manages to repel a whole series of attacks and avoids numerous traps, but difficult defense exhausts him so much that he ultimately makes a mistake.
For the spectators who sit comfortably in their tournament hall chairs, this mistake may seem random and inexplicable... In actuality, this is a consequence of the great strain.
Tal drew his last three games in Portoroz. He took first place, earning the right to continue his quest for the world championship title.
The Absolutely Best Result
At the 13th Chess Olympiad in Munich, the Soviet Union team emerged victorious again, winning the world champions’ cup.
Mikhail Tal was the first reserve player, but he played 15 games. His result, 13.5/15, was the best individual result at the whole Olympiad.
Before the match with England, a journalist asked Tal which combination at the Olympiad he considered his best.
“I hope to play one soon,” Tal joked.
It’s up to the reader to decide whether this hope came true.
No. 48, Caro-Kann Defense
M. Tal – H. Golombek
1958, Munich, 13th Olympiad Final, round 8
1.e4 c6 2.d4 d5 3.e5 Bf5 4.c4
4...e6
Black is right to decline this pawn sacrifice. After 4...Bxb1 5.Rxb1 Qa5+ 6.Bd2 Qxa2 7.c5! b5 8.Ra1 Qxb2 9.Ne2 b4 10.Qa4 b3 11.Nc1! it’s quite hard to repel the threat 12.Bd3 with the subsequent Rb1.
Editor’s note:
In addition to 12.Bd3, white has other threats: 12.Ra3 (with the subsequent Rxb3) and even 12.Bb5. The computer leaves no doubt about Koblenz’s evaluation of the final position: white wins.
5.Nc3 dxc4 6.Bxc4 Ne7 7.Nge2 Nd7 8.0-0 Nb6 9.Bb3 Qd7
9...Ned5 with the subsequent Be7, completing development, was worth considering.
10.a4 a5 11.Ng3
Editor’s note:
In a modern grandmaster game Stukopin – Maceija (Greensboro 2015), white preferred 11.Be3 with unclear play.
11...Bg6
12.Bc2!
A subtle positional maneuver. After the bishop trade, the black king loses an important defender; in addition, the white knight can reach the important e4 square.
12...Bxc2 13.Qxc2 Ned5
The central pawn shouldn’t be captured. After 13...Qxd4 14.Be3 Qd8 15.Nce4 Ned5 16.Bc5!, white’s attack is unstoppable.
Editor’s note:
Koblenz’s evaluation is not confirmed by analysis: after the subsequent 16...Nb4 17.Qc3 N6d5 18.Qc4, black doesn’t even have to offer a draw by repetition with 18...Nb6 (which white should probably agree to): he can even try to prove that his extra pawn is worth more than his opponent’s activity, for instance: 18...Bxc5 19.Qxc5 b6 20.Nd6+ Kd7 with the subsequent 21...Qe7 — his strong knight on d5 hinders the development of his opponent’s initiative. One of the possible improvements for white in this line is 15.f4 (with the idea of f4-f5), and there are other possibilities as well.
14.Nce4 Nb4 15.Qe2 N6d5 16.f4! g6 17.Ra3 Be7 18.Bd2 Nc2 19.Rd3 Ndb4 20.Bxb4 Nxb4 21.Rdd1 Rd8 22.Kh1 h5 23.Nf6+
Editor’s note:
The last move is a mistake that could have led to a very dubious position for white. A better move was, for instance, 23.Rf3 (with the idea of activating the g3 knight with Ng3-f1-e3), with roughly equal chances.
23...Bxf6 24.exf6 Kf8
24...0-0 didn’t work due to 25.Nxh5!
Editor’s note:
Black had a much stronger move 24...h4 25.Ne4 Qc7, with the idea Ke8-d7-c8. In this case, white has no dangerous initiative, and his pawn weaknesses are rather obvious.
25.Ne4 h4
It was better to involve the knight in defense with 25...Nd5.
Editor’s note:
It’s quite possible that the game move, which gives black an opportunity to get the rook into the game with Rh8-h5 in many lines and also creates the idea h4-h3, was indeed the strongest.
26.Nc5
Editor’s note:
26.Rf3 was a stronger reply, activating the rook on the third rank (with resources such as Rf3-h3 or Rf3-c3-c5) and maintaining the possibility of Ng3-e4-g5, which counters both the possible activation of the h8 rook and h4-h3.
26...Qc8
27.f5! gxf5
Editor’s note:
After 27...exf5! 28.Qe7+ Kg8 29.Qxb7 Nd5 30.Rfe1 h3 31.Qxc8 hxg2+ 32.Kxg2 Rxc8 33.Nd7 Rc7 34.Ne5 Nxf6 35.Rc1 Kg7 36.Rxc6 Rb7 37.Re2 Nd5, black gets an endgame with a clear advantage because of his dangerous kingside pawns and white’s pawn weaknesses.
28.Qe3! b6
Editor’s note:
A blunder that loses the game. After 28...Nd5 (preventing Rf4) 29.Qg5 Rh7, with the idea of Rd6 and Qd8 (with the possible inclusion of b6), the game is unclear.
