29.Qg5 Rh7 30.Rf4! bxc5 31.Rxh4 Rxh4 32.Qg7+ Ke8 33.Qg8+ Kd7 34.Qxf7+ Kd6 35.Qe7+ Black resigned.
Casual chess lovers are usually enchanted by the amount of sacrificed material. More knowledgeable fans can also appreciate a subtle sacrifice of a pawn – a humble footman of the chess army.
A pawn sacrifice often serves as a signal for the attack – the spark that creates the fire for the onslaught.
No. 49
A. Beni – M. Tal
1958, Munich, 13th Olympiad Preliminaries, round 2
Black could have just completed development with 14...b6 and Bb7, but this plan gave white great freedom of action. To seize the initiative (to develop the light-squared bishop and create a base on d5), Tal sacrifices a pawn.
14...b5! 15.cxb5 Bb7
Editor’s note:
15...Nxd3 16.Qxd3 a6! led to a big advantage.
16.Bc4
(16...
N
xf3+ was a threat)
16...Nd5 17.Qd2 Nb6 18.Be2 Qc7
Editor’s note:
The best move here is probably 18...Nxe2+! 19.Qxe2 a6, with a dangerous initiative and clear compensation for the sacrificed pawn.
19.Nxd4 cxd4
A dangerous passed pawn replaces the knight.
20.Bd3 Nd5 21.a3 a6
If now 22.bxa6 Bxa6 23.Bxa6 Rxa6 24.Kh1, then 24...Qc4, and, due to the threat 25...Rfc8 and Nb4, it’s hard for white to contain black’s pressure in the center and on the queenside.
Editor’s note:
In Koblenz’s line, instead of 24...Qc4, there’s a stronger move 24...Rc6!, and black wins because of the idea Rc6-c2. However, instead of 24.Kh1?, white has 24.Qd3, and if 24...Rc6, then 25.Bd2 – black has decent compensation, but it doesn’t give him any advantage. So white did have to play 22.bxa6, whereas after the game move his position quickly unravels.
22.Qe2 axb5 23.Bxb5 Qb6 24.Kh1 Nb4 25.a4 d3
Editor’s note:
There was no need to hurry with this move. Black had a great opportunity 25...Rac8 with a strong initiative that ensured a big advantage. After the game move 25...d3, on the other hand, white could play 26.Qf2!, and the position becomes unclear (after 26...Bc5, there’s 27.Be3).
26.Qg4? Nc2 27.Rb1 Rfd8 28.Bd2 Qd4
Editor’s note:
This move could have lost all the advantage. A better alternative was 28...Nd4!, for instance, 29.Be3 (29.Nc3 can be met with 29...Nf5!, intending 30...Nh4) 29...d2!, and white’s position is difficult (30.Bxd2?? loses immediately to 30...Nxb5).
29.Bc3
Editor’s note:
White shouldn’t have called off the blockade of the d3 pawn. After 29.Qh3, with the ideas Nd1-c3, Nd1-f2 and f4-f5, the position is unclear (and completely equal from the computer’s point of view).
29...Qd5
Editor’s note:
A much better move was 29...Qe4 30.Nf2 (30.Bd2? Qe2!–+) 30...Qe2! 31.Qxe2 dxe2 32.Bxe2 Ne3 33.Rg1 Rxa4 with strong pressure.
White now had to return his bishop to d2 on his next move, getting good chances to hold, but he makes a decisive mistake instead.
30.Rf3 d2 31.f5
A desperate attempt to complicate play.
31...exf5 32.Qxf5 Ne1! 33.Rg3 Nxg2 34.Kg1 Bc5+ 35.Kf1 Nf4! 36.Rxg7+ Kf8 37.Qxf4 Qh1+ 38.Ke2 Qe1#
Petrosian’s Victory
The 26th Soviet championship was held in Tbilisi.
In the first half of the tournament, Tal lost to Yakov Yukhtman and Eduard Gufeld, but then won nine games, which almost allowed him to catch up with the leader. He trailed Tigran Petrosian by a point.
Tal won an interesting game in round 17.
No. 50, Sicilian Defense
A. Nikitin – M. Tal
1959, Tbilisi, 26th Soviet Championship Final, round 17
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 d6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 Nf6 5.Nc3 a6 6.Bg5 Nbd7 7.Bc4 Qa5 8.Qd2 e6
9.0-0
Editor’s note:
Elite players today usually castle long here.
9...h6 10.Bh4 Be7 11.Rae1
Editor’s note:
The current main line in this position is 11.Rad1.
11...Ne5 12.Bb3
Editor’s note:
12.Be2? is bad due to 12...Nxe4!, winning a pawn.
12...g5 13.Bg3 Bd7
Editor’s note:
Black’s position is also fine after 13...Nh5!, forcing the trade of his opponent’s dark-squared bishop: Firat – Sakaev (Rijeka 2010) and Padmini – Kotanjian (Manila 2010).
14.f4 gxf4 15.Bxf4 Qc7! 16.Nf3 0-0-0 17.Kh1 Rhg8 18.Be3 Bc6 19.Qd4 Rg6 20.Re2 Rdg8 21.Qa7
Black’s pieces have assumed threatening positions, and Nikitin wants to trade queens to weaken his opponent’s onslaught. But he’s in for a big surprise.
21...Nxe4
This leads to a tempest of complications, which ultimately result in black’s decisive advantage in the endgame.
Editor’s note:
Actually, Tal’s move could have led to big trouble had white played 22.Nxe4, for instance: 22...Bxe4 23.Bb6 Nc6 (23...Bxf3? 24.Bxc7 Rxg2 25.Rxf3 Rg1+ 26.Qxg1 Rxg1+ 27.Kxg1 Nxf3+ 28.Kf2 Nd4 29.Bb6 Nxe2 30.Kxe2+–) 24.Qa8+ Kd7 25.Qxg8 Rxg8 26.Bxc7 Bxf3 27.Rxf3 Nd4 (27...Kxc7? 28.Rxf7+–) 28.Rff2 Nxe2 29.Bb6! Nc1 30.Ba4+ Kc8 31.Rxf7 Rf8 32.Rxe7 Rf1+ 33.Bg1 Ne2 34.h4 Rxg1+ 35.Kh2 and a clear advantage for white. However, Nikitin makes a grave mistake instead, which leads to a lost position.
22.Bb6 Nxc3! 23.Bxc7 Nxe2 24.Bb6 Rxg2
Editor’s note:
24...Nd7! led to a quick win, forestalling the idea of Bb3-a4 and intending to capture the g2 pawn in better conditions.
25.Ba4!
The threat 26.Qa8+ and 27.Qxb7+ requires black to play very energetically.
25...Rg1+! 26.Bxg1 Rxg1+ 27.Qxg1
Not 27.Rxg1 due to 27...Bxf3+ 28.Rg2 Nf4.
27...Bxf3+!
Not 27...Nxg1 because of 28.Bxc6!
28.Rxf3 Nxg1 29.Rc3+ Kd8 30.Kxg1 d5
This is the position Tal meant to reach when he made his 21st move. White manages to repel the mating threats, but he has only minimal chances of holding the endgame.
31.Rg3 Bg5 32.b4 b5 33.Bb3 f5 34.c3 Ke7 35.a4 f4 36.Rh3 Nc4 37.axb5 axb5 38.Kf2 Kd6 39.Ke2 e5 40.Bxc4 bxc4 41.Rh5 e4 42.h4 f3+ 43.Kd1 Bf4 White resigned.
This win was loudly celebrated by the excitable Georgians.
The tournament in the Georgian capital showed that Tal’s play had become more universal. The Riga grandmaster wasn’t avoiding endgames anymore; moreover, if the position demanded, he would gladly go for the ending.
No. 51, Modern Benoni
E. Geller – M. Tal
1959, Tbilisi, 26th Soviet Championship Final, round 4
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 c5 3.d5 e6 4.Nc3 exd5 5.cxd5 d6 6.e4 g6 7.Nf3 Bg7 8.Bg5
8...a6 9.a4
Editor’s note:
Including the moves a7-a6 and a2-a4 allows black to avoid the checks along the a4-e8 diagonal, which are quite unpleasant, for instance, in the lines 8...h6 9.Bh4 g5 10.Bg3 Nh5 11.Bb5+! Kf8 (11...Bd7 12.Bxd7+ Qxd7 13.Ne5! and a clear advantage for white) 12.e5! (as we know, this pawn sacrifice is very good for white) or 8...Bg4 9.Qa4+! (9.Bb5+!?) 9...Bd7 (9...Nbd7? 10.e5!) 10.Qb3. Note that the line 8.Bg5 is rarely played these days.
9...h6 10.Bf4 Bg4
Editor’s note:
Since black has played h7-h6, the continuation 10...g5!? looks more logical (11.Bg3 Nh5 or 11.Be3 Nbd7).
11.Be2 0-0 12.0-0
Editor’s note:
This position (after a different move order) occurred in a number of later games, most notably Beliavsky – Kudrin, Wijk aan Zee 1985, and Mamedyarov – Bacrot, Skopje 2015. Both these games continued 12...Nh5 13.Be3 with complicated play; in the opinion of the Hungarian analyst Krisztian Szabo, white is slightly better.
12...Re8 13.Qc2
Editor’s note:
As the game’s progress showed, white achieves nothing with this move. 13.Nd2 Bxe2 14.Qxe2 (with the ideas f2-f4 and Nd2-c4) was worthy of serious consideration; this line was tested in some later games.
13...Qc7 14.Rfe1 Nbd7
15.h3
After 15.Rad1, there’s 15...Bxf3 16.Bxf3 Rab8, and if 17.e5!?, then 17...Nxe5 18.Bxe5 dxe5 19.d6 Qd7, and black repels his opponent’s threats.
15...Bxf3 16.Bxf3 c4! 17.Be2
(preventing 17...
N
c5)
17...Rac8 18.a5 Nc5
Easily agreeing to swap the c-pawn for the e-pawn; after this, the game simplifies, and black gets a positional advantage.
19.Bxc4 Nfxe4 20.Nxe4 Rxe4 21.Rxe4 Nxe4 22.Qxe4 Qxc4
23.Qf3
After the queen trade, black’s advantage would have still been substantial, but with 23.Ra4!, white could still hold.
23...Qb4!
Now white can’t avoid material losses. There followed:
24.Qg3 Qxb2 25.Re1 Qb5 26.Qf3 Bf8! 27.h4 Qxa5 28.Rb1 b5 29.h5 g5 30.Qg3 Qa2 31.Rd1 Qe2 32.Rd3 Bg7 33.Qh3 Rc2 34.Bxd6 Rc1+ 35.Kh2 Qxf2 36.Rf3 Qg1+ 37.Kg3 Qe1+ 38.Kh2 Be5+ 39.Bxe5 Qxe5+ 40.Qg3 Qxd5 41.Rd3 Qc5 42.Qg4 Qe5+ White resigned.
Still, Tal couldn’t catch up with the leader. Petrosian won the tournament without a single loss. Tal shared 2nd–3rd place with Spassky.
It’s Good That You Weren’t There!
Tal traveled to the Zurich international tournament alone. After his return, we, the Riga chess players, listened to his story about the “battle in Switzerland”.
“It’s good that you weren’t there,” Tal told me. “My play in Switzerland would have perturbed you too much.”
Indeed, Tal’s play in Zurich was more than risky. However, the majority of his opponents was weaker than him, and the Riga grandmaster managed to get away with a lot of things.
Let’s look at one of Tal’s games in this tournament – it’s a great illustration of his ability to use the psychological factor in his games.
No. 52, Queen’s Gambit Declined
M. Tal – D. Keller
1959, Zurich International, round 7
1.Nf3 Nf6 2.c4 e6 3.Nc3 d5 4.d4 c6 5.Bg5 dxc4 6.e4 b5
So, his opponent decided to go for the complicated Botvinnik system. Trusting that Keller was well-prepared, the grandmaster made an unexpected decision.
7.a4
“This move was obviously prepared beforehand,” Tal’s opponent seemed to think. But that was not the case. We never analyzed this line at all. When Tal moved his a-pawn forward, he knew that this move didn’t give white any advantage; its only purpose was to make his opponent face an unfamiliar opening situation.
Editor’s note:
7.e5 is a much more popular move.
7...Qb6
Tal’s unusual move 7.a4 achieved its goal. Black’s first independent move is already a mistake.
Editor’s note:
Today, 7...Qb6 is not considered a mistake – it’s one of the main lines.
7...Bb4 was worth considering, after which David Bronstein recommended 8.e5 h6 9.Bd2 Nd5 10.Ne4! Be7!
Editor’s note:
Practice showed that after 7...Bb4 8.e5 h6 9.Bd2, instead of Bronstein’s move 9...Nd5, black can play 9...Bxc3 followed by 10...Nd5, with a good game. So, instead of 9.Bd2, white usually prefers 9.exf6 hxg5 10.fxg7 Rg8 11.g3 (or 11.h4).
8.Bxf6
Editor’s note:
This move has occurred the most often in practice, but grandmaster Reiner Knaak points out that 8.Be2 is more precise – after 8...Bb7 9.0-0 a6 10.d5 cxd5 (importantly, 10...Nbd7 is bad due to 11.dxe6 fxe6 12.e5 Nd5 13.Nd4 Nc5 14.Bg4) 11.Bxf6 gxf6, we get to the same position as in the actual game after move 11. Tal’s move order, however, gives a good additional resource to his opponent – see the editor’s note to move 11.
8...gxf6 9.Be2 a6
It was better to start preparing to castle with 9...Bb7.
Editor’s note:
Modern theory does not consider 9...a6 a bad move.
10.0-0 Bb7 11.d5!
Black is behind in development, so Tal hurries to open lines for an attack!
11...cxd5
Editor’s note:
Black has an interesting alternative 11...Nd7!?. Reiner Knaak thinks that black is even slightly better after this move (that’s why he recommended 8.Be2), and stats support his point of view.
12.exd5 b4
Editor’s note:
This move is a mistake, and black is now worse. The correct move was 12...Nd7 with the main line 13.axb5 axb5 14.Rxa8+ Bxa8 15.Nd4 Bc5 16.Ndxb5 Ne5. Based on the games Gorelov – Kishnev, Barnaul 1984, and Hillarp Persson – Robson, Reykjavik 2008, we can surmise that black can likely hold.
13.a5 Qc7
14.dxe6!
Only this way! White sacrifices a piece to keep the enemy king in the center. The knight retreat 14.Na4 could be met with 14...Nd7, and if 15.dxe6 fxe6 16.Nd4, then 16...0-0-0!
Editor’s note:
In Koblenz’s line, black’s last move (16...0-0-0) is a mistake, because he can’t defend after 17.Rc1. The correct reply is 16...Rg8 17.Bh5+ Ke7 18.g3 Qxa5, with unclear play.
14...bxc3 15.Nd4 Rg8 16.Qa4+ Kd8 17.g3 Bd5 18.Rfd1
Editor’s note:
Tal puts the wrong rook on d1: the correct move was 18.Rad1, with a decisive attack. The key difference is shown in the line 18...cxb2 19.exf7 Qxf7 20.Bc4!, and then, for instance, 20...Rg5 21.Bxd5 Rxd5 22.Rfe1 Nd7 23.Qc6+–.
18...Kc8
The black king finds no safe haven here.
Editor’s note:
As grandmaster Robert Huebner noted, black should have played 18...cxb2 (attacking the rook!) 19.Rab1 c3 – computer analysis shows that white had enough compensation for the sacrificed material, but little else.
19.bxc3
It was more precise to drive the black king to the b-file first with 19.Qe8+ Kb7 and only then play 20.bxc3.
Editor’s note:
Actually, white’s last move is not just an inaccuracy – it’s a serious mistake that gives his opponent a chance to save the game.
19...Bc5!
After 19...Kb7, Tal had prepared the following line: 20.Rab1+ Ka7 21.exf7 Qxf7 22.Bxc4 Bxc4 23.Qc6!!, and black can only resign.
20.e7 Nc6
The Swiss master decides to complete development. After 20...Bxe7 21.Nf5 Be6 22.Nxe7+ Qxe7 23.Bf3 Ra7 24.Rdb1, white had strong pressure along the b-file.
Editor’s note:
Analysis shows that, for instance, after 24...Rd8 (there are other good moves as well), the pressure along the b-file mentioned by Koblenz is not enough for white to even draw. So, instead of 22.Nxe7+?, white should play 22.Nd4 with enough compensation after 22...Bd7 23.Qc2 or repetition after 22...Bd5 23.Nf5.
21.Bg4+ Kb7 22.Nb5
Editor’s note:
This is a mistake that could have led to white’s defeat. Tal had several continuations that gave him adequate compensation for the piece (but not more); one of the possible lines is: 22.Nf5 Be6 (Huebner’s recommendation 22...Qe5 is met with 23.Rxd5! Qxd5 24.Rd1+–) 23.Rab1+ Ka7 24.Bf3 Bxf5 25.Qxc6 Qxc6 26.Bxc6 Bxb1 27.Rxb1 Rac8 28.Rb7+ Ka8 29.Rc7+ Kb8 30.Rxc8+ Kxc8 31.e8=Q+ Rxe8 32.Bxe8 Bd6 33.Bxf7 Be5 34.Bxc4 Kb7, with a draw.
22...Qe5 23.Re1
23...Be4
Editor’s note:
After 23...Qg5, black won, for instance: 24.Rab1 Qxg4 25.Nd6+ Kc7 26.e8=N+ Raxe8 27.Nxe8+ Rxe8 28.Rxe8 Qf3–+ (Unzicker).
24.Rab1! Rxg4
After 24...Bxb1, instead of 25.Rxe5, white would have played 25.Rxb1 with a strong attack. (
Editor’s note:
black’s position is indefensible in this case.)
25.Rxe4 Qxe4
if 25...Rxe4, then, of course, 26.Nd4+
26.Nd6+ Kc7 27.Nxe4 Rxe4 28.Qd1
28...Re5?
Black misses his opponent’s combination. After 28...Rxe7, the battle was still to come.
Editor’s note:
Instead of 28...Rxe7, black has 28...Bxe7, and after the subsequent 29.Qd5 Re5, the capture 30.Qxf7 happens (unlike in the line 28...Rxe7 29.Qd5 Re5) without a check (this was pointed out by Huebner). The computer evaluates this complicated position as equal.
I must say that Tal’s opponents would often make such mistakes.
“Tal hypnotizes his opponents,” said those who didn’t want to delve deeper into the reasons behind the “inexplicable” blunders of the grandmaster’s opponents. And Pal Benko found a “practical answer”: at the Candidates Tournament, he played his last two games against Tal wearing sunglasses...
Let’s remember that some of Emmanuel Lasker’s opponents, including Siegbert Tarrasch, also explained the ex-world champion’s wins by hypnosis.
Keller’s mistake is similar to Efim Geller’s mistake at the 25th Soviet championship or Panno’s mistake at the Portoroz Interzonal. In these cases, the Riga player’s opponents also lost drawn position “in strange ways”.
However, we shouldn’t forget that these mistakes were made after Geller and Panno had withstood a barrage of attacks from Tal. They just cracked under the pressure, and their concentration lapsed momentarily. And it’s enough to make just one mistake in chess to nullify all the previous efforts.
We should also keep in mind that Tal forces his opponents to play positions that need a great deal of time to think over, and so they often suffer from severe time trouble.
This game illustrates all those points. Up until now, Keller had bravely fought off all these problems, but just when white’s combination resources seemed to be over, he got tired and missed a simple rook sacrifice that immediately won the game.
There followed:
29.Rb7+! Kxb7 30.Qd7+ Kb8 31.e8=Q+ Rxe8 32.Qxe8+ Kb7 33.Qd7+ Kb8 34.Qxc6
and
Black resigned.
The Yugoslav Marathon
On 2nd September 1959, Tal took the plane to Yugoslavia. In August, he had played in the USSR Spartakiad, and his performance was dismal (2.5/8). Then he went to Riga for an urgent appendicitis operation, with only two weeks before the tournament! His friends were very concerned. The Candidates Tournament required great effort. Each participant of this truly marathon competition had to play 28 games!
The first-round loss to the ex-world champion Vasily Smyslov didn’t alarm Riga too much. The fans were already used to Tal’s starting losses (such as in Tbilisi and Zurich). However, when he also lost in round 3, his friends got worried.
I came somewhat late to the tournament.
I was in Belgrade during round 4 and Tal played against Fridrik Olafsson that day. After a sharp struggle, the Riga player managed to win.
The games were held in Bled and broadcast in Belgrade. Tal’s win was met with loud applause. However, Svetozar Gligoric then resigned to the 16 year-old Bobby Fischer, and the hall abruptly fell silent.
I got to Bled the next day; Tal was despondent. The loss to Paul Keres in round 3 was especially disappointing for the grandmaster. When he showed me how it happened, he said, “This game could have been my greatest win in Bled.”
No. 53
P. Keres – M. Tal
1959, Bled/Zagreb/Belgrade, Candidates Tournament, round 3
28...Nxe3! 29.Rc1
White can’t capture the knight immediately due to the threat 29...Qc6.
29...Qe7!
Editor’s note:
Black had an even stronger reply: 29...Qd7! 30.dxe3 Rxc1+ 31.Qxc1 (31.Nxc1 Qc6) 31...Qd5 32.Rb2 f5!–+.
30.dxe3 Qxh4 31.Kf1 Qh3+ 32.Ke2 Qxg4+ 33.Kd2 Rfd8+ 34.Nd4 Qg3 35.Rf4
35...Rxc1?
A serious mistake. After 35...Qg2+! 36.Be2 Qd5, black could capture the fourth pawn and maintain the attack.
Editor’s note:
The computer thinks that 35...Qh2+ is the strongest move and evaluates the position after 36.Be2 Bd5 (with the threats Bc4 and g6-g5) as won for black.
36.Qxc1 Qg2+ 37.Be2 Qd5 38.Qc7! Rd7 39.Qc4 Kg7
39...h5 was better
Editor’s note:
Actually, Tal’s move doesn’t deserve any criticism – the mistake was made later.
40.Qxd5 Bxd5 41.Bb5 Rc7 42.e4 Ba8
The game was adjourned here. The resumption started with
43.Ke3 Rc3+ 44.Bd3
44...b3
Editor’s note:
This move leads to a lost position. The correct move was 44...Bc6, and after the subsequent 45.Nb5 Bxb5 46.axb5 a4, the position is equal, for instance: 47.Kd4 Rc8 48.Rf1 b3 49.Ra1 Rd8+ 50.Kc4 b2 51.Rxa4 Rxd3 52.Rb4 Rd8 53.b6 (there’s no time to capture the b-pawn yet: after 53.Rxb2? g5 54.b6 g4 55.b7 Rb8 56.Kd4 Kg6 57.Ke3 Kg5 black manages to prevent the white king from getting to f4 and wins) 53...g5 54.b7 Rb8 55.Kd4 h5 (55...Kg6 56.Ke3!) 56.Rxb2 (now it’s possible to capture this pawn) 56...Kg6 57.Ke3 h4 (57...g4 58.Kf4=) 58.Kf3 Kh5 59.Rb1 g4+ 60.Kf4 g3 61.Rb2, with a draw. The interesting point here is that it was white who had to achieve the draw.
45.Rf1
Editor’s note:
45.Nb5! was much stronger, to first get the knight to d6, attacking the f7 pawn, and only then take care of the b-pawn. For instance: 45...Rc8 46.Nd6 Rf8 47.Rf1 h5 48.Bc4 b2 49.Rb1 Rb8 50.Bb5+–.
45...Rc5 46.Nxb3 Rxe5 47.Rc1 Rh5 48.Rc7
Editor’s note:
The correct move was 48.Kd4, immediately activating the king and not committing the rook yet. Even so, it’s unclear whether white would win.
48...Kf6
Editor’s note:
After 48...Rh3+ 49.Kd4 e5+ 50.Kc4 h5, black got strong counterplay, which, in the computer’s opinion, is enough to draw the game.
49.Nc5 Ke5 50.Nd7+ Kd6 51.Ra7 e5 52.Rxa8 Kxd7 53.Rxa5
, and
white soon won.
Tal drew with Tigran Petrosian, and then defeated Pal Benko and Bobby Fischer; with 4.5 points after the first leg, he caught up with Petrosian and Keres, who lost one game each.
The second leg started. It was no secret to Smyslov that Tal was bent on revenge. Indeed, the position was complicated from the very beginning. The foreign journalists ran to their phones to tell their editors that Tal had sacrificed a piece. Smyslov’s second, the usually calm grandmaster Igor Bondarevsky, was anxious too.
When Tal sacrificed his queen, the spectators realized that the end was near. The ex-world champion resigned on move 26.
The Riga grandmaster’s combination became a topic for a heated discussion. The Yugoslavian master Vladimir Vukovic, dubbed “the greatest skeptic of the 20th century” by his friends, would approach grandmaster Vyacheslav Ragozin, the head of our delegation, every day, trying to prove again and again that Tal’s sacrifice was unsound.
No. 54, Caro-Kann Defense
M. Tal – V. Smyslov
1959, Bled/Zagreb/Belgrade, Candidates Tournament, round 8
1.e4 c6 2.d3
We had planned to use this modest move against Botvinnik at the 2nd USSR Spartakiad, but the game didn’t take place. The purpose of this move is to avoid known theoretical lines.
2...d5 3.Nd2 e5 4.Ngf3 Nd7
Editor’s note:
The modern main line is 4...Bd6, which is most often met with 5.g3.
5.d4! dxe4 6.Nxe4 exd4 7.Qxd4 Ngf6 8.Bg5 Be7 9.0-0-0 0-0 10.Nd6 Qa5
Black could simplify the position with 10...Nb6, but Smyslov isn’t thinking about a draw.
11.Bc4
I must admit that I didn’t like this move. “What is he doing?” I fumed. “He’s helping black to organize a queenside attack.”
Smyslov quickly made his move, and I was still angry at Tal for his carelessness.
11...b5 12.Bd2!
Nobody expected this move. White is planning to transfer the bishop to c3, freeing up the g5 square for the queen. Here, I “forgave” Tal.
12...Qa6 13.Nf5! Bd8 14.Qh4!
A sudden change of “decoration”. White goes on the offensive.
14...bxc4 15.Qg5
15...Nh5
The ex-champion returns the sacrificed piece and prepares a counter-attack. After 15...g6, Tal planned to continue the attack with 16.Nh6+ Kg7 17.Bc3 Qxa2 18.Rhe1.
16.Nh6+ Kh8 17.Qxh5 Qxa2
This seemingly active move is a mistake. It was later found that the correct move was 17...Bf6. Tal saw this and planned to play 18.Bc3! Bxc3 19.Ng5! g6 20.Nhxf7+ Rxf7 21.Nxf7+ Kg7 22.Qf3 Bf6 23.Nd6 with strong threats.
Editor’s note:
Actually, 18.Bc3 is a mistake, because after 18...Bxc3 19.Ng5, instead of 19...g6?, black has 19...Bxb2+!, and after the subsequent 20.Kxb2 Qb5+ 21.Kc1 gxh6 22.Qxh6 Qf5, white’s attack is repelled, and black keeps his extra pieces (found by Kasparov). And so, instead of 18.Bc3?, white should have gone for another line shown by Tal, which led to a somewhat better endgame: 18.Nxf7+ Kg8 19.N7g5 h6 20.Ne4 Qxa2 21.Nxf6+ Nxf6 22.Qa5 Qxa5 23.Bxa5 and a slight advantage for white.
18.Bc3 Nf6
Now 18...Bf6 didn’t work due to 19.Nxf7+ Kg8
20.N3g5 Bxg5 21.Nxg5 h6 22.Qg6! (found by David Bronstein).
Editor’s note:
This line contains several mistakes: 1) Instead of 21...h6, black should play 21...Nf6! 22.Bxf6 Bf5! (Kasparov), after which it’s white who has to scramble to save the game, because black threatens 23...Qa4, and his bishop is hanging; 2) Instead of 21.Nxg5?!, the correct move is 21.Qxg5! Rxf7 22.Rhe1, and the only approach that gives black chances to survive is returning the piece and going for a clearly worse endgame after 22...h6 23.Re8+ Kh7 24.Qh5 Ne5 25.Rxe5 Qa1+ 26.Kd2 Rd7+ 27.Ke1 Qxd1+ 28.Qxd1 Rxd1+ 29.Kxd1 Rb8; 3) Instead of 20...Bxg5?, the correct move is Kasparov’s 20...Qa1+! 21.Kd2 Bxc3+ 22.bxc3 Nf6! with better chances for black; 4) Instead of 20.N3g5?, white should play 20.N7g5, for instance, 20...Qa1+ 21.Kd2 Bxc3+ 22.bxc3 Nf6 (Kasparov) 23.Qf7+!? Rxf7 24.Rxa1, with a drawn endgame.
19.Qxf7!
This queen sacrifice wins.
19....Qa1+ 20.Kd2 Rxf7 21.Nxf7+ Kg8 22.Rxa1 Kxf7 23.Ne5+ Ke6 24.Nxc6 Ne4+ 25.Ke3 Bb6+ 26.Bd4 Black resigned.
The game was awarded a special prize.
Both I and Tal’s official second, Yuri Averbakh, were quite satisfied with his play in Bled. Tal was just half a point behind the leader, grandmaster Paul Keres. We were glad to see that living in Bled, which is located in a beautiful mountain region, was beneficial to Tal – he looked noticeably healthier and fresher.
The next leg was played in the capital of Croatia, Zagreb. The results of the first half of the tournament were evaluated differently by the players.
Fischer was disappointed because of his many losses and the organizers’ “tactlessness”: he received children’s soap in pretty wrapping as a gift.
Benko was sad to part with the fun casino and dancing. The grandmaster complained that he was unable to find a better coach. His second, Yugoslav master Maric, said, “Like player, like coach.”
Olafsson and his three coaches were especially unhappy. The Icelandic grandmaster sadly contemplated his return to the homeland, which had organized a special fundraiser to help him travel to the tournament.
Tal was secretly mad at me: I had asked the table tennis lovers not to play him because of his recent operation. However, film footage showed Tal waving the little bat anyway...
The cinema people were rather happy. They managed to film Petrosian as a gondolier during a boat ride on the lake with his second, grandmaster Isaak Boleslavsky. The cameras caught Olafsson and Gligoric in the swimming pool, and Keres out fishing.
And then, we boarded the bus heading to Zagreb. Fischer seemed to have left his worries behind. In answer to Tal’s question about whether he’d ever been to an opera, the American sang the smugglers’ march from
Carmen
in a soprano.
“Tell me, Bobby,” Tal continued his interrogation, “how do you like Volpert’s game style?”
Fischer thought for a bit, then answered very seriously, “She’s too cautious. But you have another woman player, Dmitrieva, I like her games!”
When the grandmasters disembarked at the fashionable Zagreb hotel Esplanada, they were immediately surrounded by a crowd of chess fans.
The first round in the Croatian capital yielded some unexpected results. Keres blundered in an equal position against Fischer and had to resign. Petrosian lost to Olafsson.
The influx of spectators was so huge that the organizers had to place a demonstration board on the balcony of the playing hall. Thousands of people watched the battle between the candidates, and one of the streets in the center was closed off for a few hours.
The game between Tal and Smyslov attracted the most interest.
It was broadcast in Riga by phone. At first, the people were happy, but then, when the middlegame started, they began to ask anxious questions:
“How do you evaluate Tal’s position? Will he be able to survive?”
I couldn’t say anything good. Tal’s loss seemed inevitable. However, he was still putting up fierce resistance in severe time trouble. Each of Smyslov’s moves saw an immediate, lightning-fast reply. And then, the ex-world champion, who usually keeps his cool even in the most difficult conditions, suddenly lost concentration. He got into time trouble as well, missed a simple tactical threat, and Tal saved the game with perpetual check.
Here’s how the finale of this dramatic game played out.
No. 55
V. Smyslov – M. Tal
1959, Bled/Zagreb/Belgrade, Candidates Tournament, round 15
At this point, Tal considered his position quite tenable, but just several moves later, he started to think a lot. As the subsequent play showed, he had every reason for that.
15.exd5 cxd5 16.b4 Nd7 17.Nb3! e5
Black’s active operations in the center don’t reach their intended goal. With several precise moves, Smyslov manages to attain a decisive advantage.
18.Bf5 e4 19.Rec1!
A typical “Smyslov” move – simple and yet very strong. The threat 20.Rxc8 and 21.Bxd7 doesn’t leave much choice for black.
19...Qd6 20.Nd4
After this move, Tal thought for 40 minutes. The threats 21.Rc6 and 21.Bxh7+ with the subsequent 22.Nf5 are very unpleasant.
20...Bf6 21.Rc6 Qe7 22.Rac1 h6
22...Be5 didn’t work due to 23.Bxh7+ Kxh7 24.Qh5+ Kg8 25.Nf5, and if 25...Qd8, then 26.Rxc8!. (
Editor’s note:
Instead of 26.Rxc8, white could win much more rapidly: 26.Rh6! gxh6 27.Nxh6+ Kg7 28.Nf5+ with a quick mate.)
23.Rc7!
Now black can’t avoid material losses.
23...Be5 24.Nc6 Qg5 25.h4
It seems that nothing can save black from a loss. Among other things, he had just two minutes left to make fifteen moves.
25...Qxh4 26.Nxe5 Nxe5 27.Rxc8
Of course, the simplest move was 27.Bxc8, and after 27...Nd3 – 28.Bb7. After the game move, black has some chances to continue the struggle...
27...Nf3+ 28.gxf3 Qg5+ 29.Kf1 Qxf5 30.Rxf8+ Rxf8 31.fxe4 dxe4
After getting an extra piece, Smyslov started playing without confidence.
32.Qe3 Rd8 33.Qg3 g5 34.Rc5
34.Qc3 f6 35.Qb3+ with the subsequent 36.Rc6 won immediately.
34...Rd1+ 35.Kg2 Qe6 36.b5 Kh7
37.Rc6
Editor’s note:
White’s last move is a mistake (the correct reply was 37.Rc3, controlling the third rank), which Tal could have exploited with 37...Qf5! (instead of 37...Qd5?), not just with the ideas 38...Qxb5 and 38...Rd3, but also forcing the opponent’s queen to guard the f3 square (38.Qh2?? Qf3#). After the subsequent 38.Rc1 Rd3 39.Qe5 Qg4+ 40.Kf1 Qh3+ or 38.Rc3 Qxb5 39.Qh3 Qf1+ 40.Kg3 Qb5 41.Kg2 (41.Bc1? e3! 42.Bxe3 Qe5+, winning) 41...Qf1+ (41...Qxb2?? 42.Rc6+–) 42.Kg3 Qb5, the game ends with a threefold repetition.
37...Qd5
38.Qe5?
Smyslov, who was in time trouble too at this point, misses a simple tactic. After 38.Qe3 Rd3 39.Qb6! e3+ 40.Kh2, black had to resign.
Editor’s note:
Actually, 38.Qe3 is no good either: it is met with 38...Qf5! (threatening 39...Qg4+ 40.Qg3 Qe2–+), and white has no way to win: 39.Rf6 Qg4+ 40.Qg3 Qh5 41.Qh2 Qg4+, 39.Qe2 Rd2! 40.Qxd2 Qf3+, or 39.Rc1 Rd3 40.Qe2 Rd2! 41.Qxd2 Qf3+, with a draw in all lines.
There was a good move 38.Rc1! – after the subsequent 38...Rxc1 (38...e3+ 39.Qf3+–) 39.Bxc1 Qxb5 40.Qd6 white should win after overcoming some technical difficulties. However, the strongest continuation was 38.Qh2!! (threatening to take on h6) 38...e3+ 39.Kg3, and black is defenseless.
38...Rg1+ 39.Kh2 Rh1+ 40.Kg2 Rg1+
Taking the rook results in perpetual check, and so the opponents agreed to a
draw
.
In round 16, Tal defeated Gligoric, and Keres drew with Smyslov. Thus, Tal overtook the Estonian grandmaster by half a point. It seemed that the Riga player, who had black against Keres in round 17, could just settle for a draw. But Tal declined the peace offer made by his opponent on the 18th move. This was a critical decision. The position was incredibly sharp, but Tal was going exactly for that. If he won, he could increase his lead further.
No. 56
P. Keres – M. Tal
1959, Bled/Zagreb/Belgrade, Candidates Tournament, round 17
19...Qd4!?
Of course, 19...Be7 was more solid.
19...Qd4!? 20.h4 Qxb2 21.Rd1 Bd4 22.Rd3 Qxc2 23.Rxd4! exd4 24.e5 Kf8! 25.exf6
Without a doubt, Keres saw that he could force a draw with 25.Bxf6 Qc1+ 26.Kh2 Qxh6 27.Bg5 Qg7 28.Bf6 Qh6 29.Bg5, but he preferred to play for a win.
Editor’s note:
Keres’ move is a mistake that leads to a lost position for white.
25...Qc3! 26.Qg4 Qe1+ 27.Kh2 Qxf2 28.Qh3 Qe1 29.Qb3 b5 30.axb5 cxb5 31.Qa3+ b4 32.Qb3 Qe5+ 33.Kh1 Qe1+ 34.Kh2 Qe5+
Both opponents were in time trouble at that point.
35.Kh1 Qd6 36.Kg1 d3
36...Qc5 was more cautious, and if 37.Bd2, then 37...a5.
37.Qd1
Many commentators pointed out that white had the move 37.Qc4 available. Then 37...d2 is bad due to 38.Bxd2!, and black can’t play 38...Qxd2 due to 39.Qc8+ Re8 40.Qc5+, mating. Further, after 37...b3 38.Qc8+ Re8 39.Qc4 Re6 40.Qc8+, white forces a draw. (
Editor’s note:
After 39.Qc4, black doesn’t have to play 39...Re6; instead, he can play 39...Re1+ 40.Kf2 Re2+ 41.Kf1 Qe6 42.Qb4+ Ke8 43.Qb8+ Kd7 44.Qb7+ Kd6 45.Qb6+ Kd5 46.Qxb3+ Kd4 47.Qb4+ Qc4 48.Qxc4+ Kxc4 49.Nxf7 Re6 50.Nd8 d2 51.Bxd2 Rxf6+ 52.Ke2 Kb5, maintaining winning chances, even though white probably should hold. However, instead of 39.Qc4?!, white has a much better move: 39.Qb7, forcing black to give perpetual check with 39...Qd4+ 40.Kh2 Qe5+ etc.) However, Tal’s intended 37...Qb6+ 38.Kh2 Qb5! 39.Qc8+ Re8 still won.
37...Qc5+ 38.Kh1 Qc2 39.Qf3 d2 40.Bxd2 Qxd2 White resigned.
In round 20, Tal won his third game against the U.S. champion.
No. 57
M. Tal – B. Fischer
1959, Bled/Zagreb/Belgrade, Candidates Tournament, round 20
Black played
20...dxc5
without much thought. The seconds of foreign grandmasters exchanged knowing smiles. They, like Fischer, thought that Tal had just lost a pawn. However, just a few moves later, it turned out that Fischer had lost a lot more...
21.Bd3! cxb4 22.Rae1 Qf6
22...Qd6 was better.
23.Re6
Editor’s note:
23.Na4 was a stronger move with the threats 24.Bxf5+ and 24.Qxc7 and a big advantage. Had Fischer now continued correctly, it would have turned out that the white rook’s position on e6 was not ideal: if the knight leaves d7, it falls under attack.
23...Qxc3
Editor’s note:
The only correct move was 23...Qg7, still protecting the h-pawn with the queen. It’s also important to note that the queen would have covered the f8 rook and attacked the c3 knight, and the c7 pawn was also protected after the knight left d7. There could follow: a) 24.Bxf5+ Kh8 25.Qxb4 a5! 26.Qd4 (26.Nxa5? Nc5!) 26...Qxd4+ 27.Nxd4 Nc5; b) 24.Qxb4 a5! 25.Nxa5 Nc5 26.Re3 (26.Qxc5? Bxe6 27.dxe6 b6 with a clear advantage for black) 26...Nxd3 27.Rxd3 Ra6; or c) 24.Rf3 Ndf6 25.Rg3 Qf7 26.Bxf5+ Kh8 – black should probably hold in all cases.
24.Bxf5+ Rxf5 25.Qxf5+ Kh8 26.Rf3!
Now black is defenseless. After
26...Qb2 27.Re8 Ndf6 28.Qxf6+ Qxf6 29.Rxf6 Kg7 30.Rff8 Ne7 31.Na5! h5 32.h4 Rb8 33.Nc4 b5 34.Ne5
, the
American grandmaster resigned.
Tal bolstered his tournament standing with wins against Benko and Olafsson. After round 21, he had scored 15.5 points, Keres had 14, and Smyslov and Petrosian were on 11.5 each.
On 15th October, the last, decisive leg of the tournament started in Belgrade. There was an eye-catching headline in the evening newspaper that reported on the tournament: “Seven grandmasters approaching Belgrade, Tal approaching Botvinnik!”
The interest in the Candidates Tournament in Belgrade was huge. The tournament hall couldn’t accommodate everyone. The games were demonstrated on the streets.
It was rather hard for Tal. Each evening, the grandmasters were surrounded by hundreds of chess enthusiasts at the exit. The supporters walked with Tal to the hotel, and the grandmaster had to answer questions and give autographs all the way.
Tal had every chance to gain first place, but the battle hadn’t yet ended. The games of the Zagreb leg drained much of his strength, and now, at the finishing line, Tal was noticeably tired.
In the game against Smyslov – the first round of the last leg – he played too riskily and lost a knight. Only Smyslov’s weak play in time trouble saved the leader from defeat.
Before the game with Keres, Tal resolutely declined advice to calmly play for a draw against the Estonian grandmaster.
“Keres has two and a half fewer points, and I have no moral right to avoid a fight.”
These weren’t just words. Even though Tal couldn’t play at his full strength because of his tiredness, he didn’t avoid complications. Keres exploited his opponent’s opening mistake and won convincingly.
Tal drew his next two games, against Petrosian and Olafsson, and then won his fourth game with Fischer out of four. We should point out that Tal barely avoided big trouble in this game: his risky opening play allowed Fischer to create a strong attack.
Before the last round, Tal had one point more than Keres. His opponent was Benko. As I said earlier, Benko came to the game against the leader wearing sunglasses – he had found his remedy against hypnosis!
Tal’s countermeasure caused a merry stir in the hall. He greeted his opponent, sat down and, before making a move, also put sunglasses on.
When Tal offered Benko a draw in a better position, the latter pretended not to hear his opponent. However, peace was agreed after some time. In a winning position, Tal forced a perpetual check. He had won the Candidates Tournament!
At the press conference, Tal said, “I have dreamed of playing at least one game against the world champion for quite a while, and I’m happy that I will get to play a whole match against him!”
Reactions after the Candidates Tournament
The results of the Candidates Tournament and Tal’s victory were discussed by chess players throughout the world. Despite the young grandmaster’s brilliant successes in recent years – two Soviet championship wins and first place at the Portoroz and Zurich tournaments – many chess fans were still surprised when the 23 year-old Riga player triumphed at the Candidates.
Tal’s ascent to the very top was truly rapid. In just nine years, the young player had progressed from first-category player to world championship candidate!
In his article “Modern Chess”, the ex-world champion Max Euwe wrote, “We cannot talk about modern chess without mentioning Tal’s name. The incredible combinations that arise in his games as though summoned by a magic wand are something more than just brilliant fireworks. This is a manifestation of the new style. With his efforts, Tal, despite his youth, has taken an important part in building the strong edifice of chess art, the foundation of which was laid more than a hundred years ago.”
In the article “M. Tal’s Victory”, published in
Shakhmaty v SSSR
, grandmaster Alexander Kotov highlighted that “...Tal’s example will help our chess youth to understand the beauty of chess, to understand how they should play.”
“What’s most characteristic of Tal’s playing style? The desire to create unclear, double-edged positions and seize the initiative at any cost, without regard to sacrifices,” said grandmaster Vyacheslav Ragozin, FIDE Vice-President, at the Candidates Tournament closing ceremony.
Here are some examples that illustrate these features of Tal’s creative style.
No. 58
M. Tal – S. Gligoric
1959, Bled/Zagreb/Belgrade, Candidates Tournament, round 16
It looks like both sides are going to castle and then begin long positional maneuvering. However, Tal’s next move changes the situation drastically.
16.d5!
Editor’s note:
This move could have led to a very unpleasant position for white.
16...Qe7
Gligoric refuses to accept the pawn sacrifice, because after 16...exd5 17.cxd5 Bxd5 (if 17...Nxd5, then 18.Be4!) 18.c4 Bc6 19.Nf5, white has a threatening initiative.
17.0-0 Nd7
Editor’s note:
After 17...0-0-0! black seizes the initiative. The point is, after 18.dxe6? (white wins an exchange this way, but the opponent’s bishop becomes rather dangerous) 18...fxe6 19.Ng6 Qg7 20.Nxh8 Rxh8, black pushes the h-pawn forward, creating a very dangerous (and probably decisive) attack. Instead of 18.dxe6, white has a stronger move 18.e4, but after 18...Nd7 with the subsequent Nc5 black is in the driver’s seat: the a4 and c4 pawns are weak, the idea f7-f5 is in the air, and white has no way to create any activity.
18.dxe6 Qxe6 19.Bf5! Qxc4
Editor’s note:
As it soon transpires, the queen is positioned badly here. The right move was 19...Qe5, with unclear play.
20.Rfd1 Nf6 21.Rd4 Qc6
It seems that the first wave of the attack is over – it’s unclear how white can increase the pressure. However, Tal finds an elegant combination maneuver, and black’s position soon becomes very difficult.
22.Be6!
Editor’s note:
Actually, this move allows black to consolidate, whereas after 22.Bd3! (not just threatening 23.Bb5, but with a lot of other ideas as well, such as, Rf4, Rc4 and Nf5), white has an overwhelming position.
22...Rg8 23.Bc4! Kf8
Editor’s note:
Analysis shows that after 23...0-0-0!, the position was dynamically equal. One example line: 24.Qf5+ Nd7 25.Qxf7 Rgf8 26.Qe6 h5 27.Bb5 Qxc3 28.Bxd7+ Rxd7 29.Ng6 Rxf2! 30.Qe8+ Rd8 31.Ne7+ Kb8 32.Qxd8+ Ka7 33.Kxf2+ Qc2+ 34.Ke1 Qxb1+ 35.Rd1 Qb4+ 36.Rd2 Qc3! 37.Nf5 Qc1+ 38.Rd1 Qc3+ with perpetual check.
24.Bb5 Qc5 25.Rc4 Qe5 26.Rxc7 Be4 27.Bd3 d5 28.Rc6 Rb8 29.c4
, and, gaining a great positional advantage,
white won
on move 64.
After Tal took the lead, he could have afforded to play quietly, but, even in the last rounds, he still preferred to take risks.
In another game against Gligoric, in the fourth leg, Tal chose a double-edged line in the King’s Indian. After 18 moves, the following position occurred on the board:
No. 59
S. Gligoric – M. Tal
1959, Bled/Zagreb/Belgrade, Candidates Tournament, round 23
18...Nxd5
Black doesn’t get enough material compensation for the sacrificed knight, but pressure along the e-file helps him maintain a long-term initiative. White faced many difficulties while neutralizing this pressure, and he ultimately cracked.
19.exd5 Bf5 20.Qc3 Bg7 21.Qc1 Bxb1 22.Bxg7 Kxg7 23.Qxb1 Nf4 24.Nde3 Qe5
Gligoric makes the right decision: he returns the piece to get some freedom in maneuvering.
25.Bxb5 Rxb5 26.Nf5+
Editor’s note:
The correct move was 26.g3 with a roughly equal position after 26...Kf8 (the king evades a possible Nf5+) 27.gxf4 Qxf4.
26...gxf5!
Editor’s note:
Objectively speaking, Tal’s move is questionable. 26...Qxf5 27.Qxf5 gxf5 28.Rxe8 Nxd5 was stronger, getting an endgame with a tiny material advantage. On the other hand, even though only black would have chances of winning, they were actually quite marginal because of his compromised pawn structure. Tal’s move leads to an equal but complicated position that suits his style much better.
27.Rxe5 Rxe5
Black gets enough compensation for the queen.
28.g3 Ne2+ 29.Kf2 Kg8?
The first consequence of time trouble. Black should have played 29...Nd4.
30.Ne3?
White, affected by the pressure, makes a mistake too. After 30.f4! Re8 (if 30...Re4, then 31.Nd2) 31.Qxf5, he got a material advantage.
30...Nd4 31.f4 Re4 32.g4??
Suicide! The necessary move was 32.Nxf5!, with equal chances.
32...Rxf4+ 33.Kg2 fxg4 34.Nc4 Nf3 35.Qc1 Rf6 36.Qe3 Kf8 37.Qe4 Rb8 38.Qxh7 Re8 39.Qh8+ Ke7 40.Qg7 Nh4+ 41.Kg1
Time trouble has ended, but white can’t do anything – his position is hopeless.
41...Nf3+ 42.Kg2 Rg6 43.Qc3 Nd4 44.b4 Kf8 45.Ne3 g3! 46.h3 Rf6 White resigned.
In addition to his attacking skills, Tal showed his great defensive technique at the Candidates Tournament.
No. 60
F. Olafsson – M. Tal
1959, Bled/Zagreb/Belgrade, Candidates Tournament, round 11
21...g6 22.Bxg6 Qe7!
Black sees through his opponent’s smart plan. After 22...hxg6 23.Qxg6+ Kh8 white doesn’t settle for perpetual check: with 24.Nf5!! Bxb2 25.Rf3!, he checkmates his opponent.
23.Bxf6 Nxf6 24.Qf3 Rb8 25.Bd3 Bb7 26.Qe2
White’s attack is repelled, but he still maintains a positional advantage. Tal slowly outplays the Icelandic grandmaster and goes on the counter-attack.
26...Kh8 27.Rce1 Rbe8 28.Nf5 Qb4 29.Qb2 Rc8 30.Ng3
White has wasted precious time on knight moves, and his opponent has managed to seize the c-file.
30...Qc3 31.Qe2 Rg8 32.Ne4 Qd4 33.Ng5 Rg7 34.Qe3 Qxe3 35.Rxe3 Rc3 36.Rh3 b4 37.Kg1
37...e5!
The signal for a counter-attack!
38.Rg3
Editor’s note:
Olafsson’s last move is a blunder. The correct reply was 38.Rh6, and after the subsequent 38...Ng4 39.Rxh7+ Rxh7 40.Bxh7 Ne3 41.Re1 exf4 42.g3 fxg3 43.hxg3, white should draw easily.
38...e4 39.Bc4 d5 40.Be2 d4 41.Bc4 e3 42.Rd1 Bd5! 43.Bf1 Re7 44.Be2 d3 45.Bxd3 Rxd3 46.Rxd3 e2 47.Rge3 e1=Q+
, and
black won.
The Candidates Tournament win didn’t go to Tal’s head. The laudatory articles that now and again appeared in the press didn’t impress him much. Tal remained the same as he always was. He understood the necessity of hard work and further self-improvement.
He realized clearly that his play was not free of serious drawbacks. It was obvious that in games against Soviet players Tal wasn’t always on top of his situation. Didn’t his three losses to Keres attest to that? In one of those games, he got carried away and sacrificed two pieces without good reason, the other was won by the Estonian grandmaster in a complicated, maneuvering struggle. The game against Smyslov in round 22 could also have ended badly for Tal, who disregarded the objective requirements of the position. And didn’t the games against Fischer (round 27) and Olafsson (round 11) show how much excessive danger Tal voluntarily went for?
Only Fourth Place...
After a short rest, we started preparing for the match against Botvinnik: we read the latest Soviet and foreign chess magazines and opening monographs and, of course, studied the world champion’s games.
Tal was very glad to hear the news that an international tournament would be held in Riga. The grandmaster preferred this way of training above anything else.
The tournament didn’t bring Tal any laurels. He finished only fourth, behind Spassky, Mikenas and Tolush. Nevertheless, the pre-match competition was beneficial: it was good practice, showing on what we needed to work.
Tal’s result in the Riga tournament was predetermined after losing to his main competitor, Boris Spassky. The distance was short, and it soon became clear that Tal wouldn’t be able to catch up with the Leningrad grandmaster.
No. 61, Ruy Lopez
B. Spassky – M. Tal
1959, Riga International, round 3
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 a6 4.Ba4 Nf6 5.0-0 Be7 6.Re1 b5 7.Bb3 0-0 8.c3 d6 9.h3 Na5 10.Bc2 c5 11.d4 Qc7 12.Nbd2 Bd7 13.Nf1 Rfe8 14.d5 c4 15.g4
Editor’s note:
This move never found any adherents among strong players. For instance, in the games Landa — P. Nikolic, Germany BL 2004, and Azarov — V. Kovalev, Minsk 2010, white preferred 15.Ng3 and then executed the plan Nh2 and f2-f4.
15...h5
This aggressive move is a strategic error. The correct reply was 15...g6.
16.gxh5 Bxh3 17.N3h2 Nh7
Editor’s note:
It was better to play 17...Nb7, intending Nc5 at the earliest opportunity. In the actual game, this knight didn’t properly enter the battle at all.
18.Ne3 Bg5 19.Kh1 Bf4 20.Rg1 Qd8 21.Qf3
Threatening to start an attack with 22.Nf5.
21...Qh4
Another “active” move. In actuality, black’s position is already critical.
22.Ng2 Bxg2+ 23.Qxg2 Qxh2+
A forced trade, because after 23...Qf6, white gets a strong attack with 24.Be3 and the subsequent 25.Qh3 and Ng4. (
Editor’s note:
Instead of 24.Be3, white had an even stronger move 24.Bxf4! exf4 25.Qh3 with various ideas, for instance, Ng4, Nf3, e4-e5, Rg1-g4 and Ra1-g1 etc. – analysis shows that in this line white has a decisive advantage.)
24.Qxh2 Bxh2 25.Kxh2
Spassky has got a noticeable advantage with simple means. The defense is going to be difficult for black.
25...Nf6 26.Rg5 Nh7 27.Rf5 g6 28.Rf3 Kg7 29.hxg6 fxg6 30.b4! cxb3 31.axb3 Nb7 32.b4
Fixing the backward pawn a6 in place and taking away the important c5 square from the knight.
32...Rec8 33.Bd2 Nf6 34.Bd3 Rh8+ 35.Kg2 Ng4 36.Kg3 Nf6 37.Kg2 Ng4 38.Rh1 Rxh1 39.Kxh1
39...a5?
Passive defense does not sit well with Tal, and he tries to activate his queenside pieces. However, the b-pawn is now severely weakened. It was better to transfer the knight from g4 to c7 first.
40.Kg2 a4 41.Rh3 Nf6 42.Bh6+ Kf7 43.Rf3 a3 44.Bb1 a2 45.Bxa2 Rxa2 46.Bg5 Rc2 47.Rxf6+ Ke8 48.Rxg6 Kd7 49.Rg7+ Kc8 50.Be3 Rxc3 51.Bb6! Rc4 52.Rg8+ Kd7 53.Rb8 Nc5 54.bxc5 dxc5
, and, without waiting for his opponent’s reply,
Tal resigned.
Vladas Mikenas defeated Tal as well.
Tal’s desire to go for complications no matter the cost brought him success in the next game.
No. 62, Modern Benoni
I. Niemela – M. Tal
1959, Riga International, round 7
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 c5 3.d5 e6 4.Nc3 exd5 5.cxd5 d6 6.e4 g6 7.f4 Bg7 8.Nf3 0-0 9.Be2 Re8 10.e5 dxe5 11.fxe5 Ng4
12.e6
A little explored double-edged move, involving a pawn sacrifice. Alekhine recommended 12.0-0 in this position. By parting with the e5 pawn, white retains good attacking chances.
12...fxe6 13.0-0
Editor’s note:
A bad move. There are two much stronger replies: 13.d6 (the most frequent move) and 13.Bg5 – according to modern theory, chances are roughly equal in both cases (white has fair compensation for the pawn, but no more).
13...exd5 14.Nxd5 Be6 15.Bc4 Ne5
Black provokes his opponent to sacrifice a piece. After 16.Nxe5 Bxe5, white has no compensation for the pawn.
16.Bg5! Nxf3+ 17.Qxf3 Qxg5 18.Rae1 Rf8!
Tal placed all his hopes in this move.
19.Rxe6?
Paul Keres wrote this about the move in the tournament bulletin: “It’s hard to master this complicated position even in home analysis. However, one thing is clear: the game move leads to a quick defeat. Perhaps there were two better move for white: firstly, 19.Qxf8+ Bxf8 20.Rxe6 Nc6 21.Nc7, and black has to give up another rook; secondly, just play 19.Qe4, and there’s no good move to defend the e6 bishop.”
Editor’s note:
Keres’ first line doesn’t help white: after 19.Qxf8+ Bxf8 20.Rxe6 Nc6 21.Nc7, there follows 21...Kg7 22.Nxa8 Qd8 (threatening 23...Qd4+/23...Qxa8), and black wins (Sassen – Mooij, corr 1974). However, 19.Qe4 is indeed a good move and the only possibility to continue the struggle, even though after the subsequent 19...Nc6 20.Qxe6+ Kh8 white has no compensation for the pawn.
19...Bd4+!
White missed this Zwischenschach. He expected 19...Rxf3, with a decisive attack after 20.Re8+. Now the black king can escape to g7.
20.Kh1 Rxf3 21.Re8+ Kg7 22.Rxf3 Qc1+ 23.Bf1 Nc6!
Launching a winning counter-attack.
24.Rxa8 Ne5 25.Rff8 Ng4 26.Rf3 Nf2+ 27.Kg1 Ne4+ White resigned:
after 28.Kh1, black has the elegant 28...Ng3+! (29.hxg3 Qh6#).
Tal made a fascinating intuitive sacrifice of two minor pieces for a rook and pawn in a game against a Norwegian master.
No. 63
M. Tal – S. Johannessen
1959, Riga International, round 6
12.Bxf7+!
Like 12...Nxe4 in the game against Averbakh at the 25th Soviet championship, this double-edged move caused much discussion.
“I think that such sacrifices actually don’t need any concrete calculations: it’s enough to look at the position to see that this sacrifice should be correct” – that’s what Tal himself said about the move.
Editor’s note:
From Koblenz’s subsequent notes, it’s clear that he wasn’t sure that Tal’s operation was correct from the analytical point of view (see his note to black’s 15th move), so the exclamation mark awarded by him for this move obviously describes only its practical strength. Computer analysis shows that white’s move was dubious.
12...Rxf7 13.Nxf7 Kxf7 14.Qb3+ Kf8 15.Rac1
The white pieces have assumed ideal positions, whereas it’s hard for black to complete his queenside development.
15...a6?
Preventing the move 16.Nb5. Now, however, black has no time to develop his queenside. Keres recommended 15...Qb6 16.Nb5 Ne8 here, which Tal intended to meet with 17.Qc4. In this case, it’s hard to determine whether white has enough positional compensation for the lacking material. One thing is clear: black would have still faced difficult problems. (
Editor’s note:
After the subsequent 17...Ne5 18.Qe2 Bg4 19.f3 Be6 20.b3 Rd8, black completes his development and gets an advantage: two pieces for a rook and pawn and the bishop pair. As for the game move, it’s certainly not good, but awarding a question mark to it was probably too harsh.)
16.Rfd1 Qa5
Editor’s note:
This is an error. The correct move was 16...Qb6 — for instance, 17.Qc4 Qxb2 18.Nd5 Nxd5 19.Qxd5 Qb5 20.Qe6 Qf5 21.Qc4 Qb5, with threefold repetition.
17.Qc4!
(preventing 17...b5)
Editor’s note:
White had a much stronger reply 17.Nd5! (threatening 18.Nc7/18.Nxe7), and black’s position becomes very difficult, for instance, 17...Nxd5 (after 17...b5?, the simplest is 18.Nc7 Ra7 19.Ne6+) 18.Rxd5 Qd8 19.Rd2 Qe8 20.Qd1 Bf6 21.e4 (threatening 22.e5) 21...Kg7 22.Rc7 Qf7 23.Rdc2+–.
17...Qf5
Editor’s note:
Black could resist better with 17...Qc5 18.Qe2 (neither 18.Qe6 Qb6 nor 18.Qb3 Qb6 leads to any progress) 18...Qf5 (or 18...Qh5) — the white queen is much less active on e2.
18.h3! Ne8! 19.Nd5 Qe6 20.Qb4 b5
Even after 20...Be5, recommended by Keres in the tournament bulletin, white still maintained a significant advantage with 21.Rc4. (
Editor’s note:
Instead of 21.Rc4?!, white has a much stronger reply: 21.Bxe5 Qxe5 22.f4 Qe6 23.e4 with an overwhelming position. However, black could have prolonged the resistance with 20...a5! 21.Qb5 b6, not fearing 22.Rc6? due to 22...Ba6!.)
21.Rc6!
Gaining a decisive tempo. Interestingly, white’s winning combination follows at the very moment when the Norwegian master seemed to have overcome his troubles.
21...Qf7 22.Nc7 Nxc7 23.Rxc7 Qe6 24.Rdc1!
The threat 25.Rxc8+ is unstoppable. After 24...Ne5, there’s 25.Qe4, winning.
24...Nb6 25.Rxe7! Nd5 26.Rxe6+ Nxb4 27.Bd6+ Black resigned.
The Draw is Made!
We’re in Moscow, in Max Euwe’s room in the National Hotel; the ex-world champion arrived from Netherlands a few hours ago for the opening ceremony of the Botvinnik-Tal match. The official part is scheduled for the next day, while this evening the draw is made to determine who’s going to make the first move in the match.
There’s a small crowd around the little round table: world champion Mikhail Botvinnik with his second Grigory Goldberg, Max Euwe, Harry Golombek (the Englishman is one of the arbiters), Lev Abramov (the USSR Chess Federation executive secretary), Mikhail Tal and yours truly – his second.
We’re waiting for the match chief arbiter – Swedish grandmaster Gideon Stahlberg, who got delayed somewhere between Stockholm and Moscow. To make the best use of the time, we discuss the match regulations. One of the clauses states: if the tournament hall becomes too noisy, the arbiters are obliged to move the game to another room if one of the players’ seconds requests it.
“Do you have any objections?” Botvinnik asked Tal.
“No, why should I?”
The other points are agreed upon just as quickly. Abramov looks at his watch. Stahlberg still hadn’t arrived.
Goldberg, who’s sitting beside me, tells some story. I’m trying to listen attentively, but understand nothing. In my mind’s eye, I involuntarily see episodes of days long past...
May 1935... I’m sitting in a London cafe with Emanuel Lasker, the man who defeated the first world champion, legendary Wilhelm Steinitz, in 1894. I’m talking to the man who deservedly bore the world champion’s crown for 27 years and, despite his age of 67, finished third at the Second Moscow International tournament without losing a single game!
The “iron” Lasker is polite and gentle in real life. Before expressing his thoughts, he looks somewhere far away, still holding a cigar in his mouth.
“Many wondered why I forfeited the match against Capablanca after just 14 games,” Lasker says. “The official version was that I was defeated by the tropical climate of Havana. But this is not the only reason. I didn’t want to die in poverty, like Steinitz, so I demanded appearance fees to play in tournaments, which angered the rich sponsors who organized the tournaments. I was constantly hounded by the press, which was in their pocket.”
Lasker fell silent and, exhaling another cloud of smoke, continued:
“With each day, I felt more and more isolated and lonely. I was so fed up that I decided to demonstratively relinquish the world champion’s title.
When thousands of spectators flocked to the spacious halls of the Moscow International tournament, my heart was full of joy. I didn’t doubt that the talented and hard-working Mikhail Botvinnik would move mountains – it just couldn’t be any different!”
...Hastings, an English resort town, January 1935. The traditional Christmas tournament had just finished. Late in the evening, a group of chess players surrounded a short young man in the lobby of a hotel. This is grandmaster Salo Flohr. He’s enthusiastically talking about chess life in the USSR, saying that chess has become a part of people’s culture, it’s played in factories and schools.
When Flohr said that he saw slogans such as “Overtake the capitalist countries on the chess front!” in clubs, his listeners exchanged looks. Many of them smiled ironically. This is a utopia! The West has great players such as Capablanca, Lasker, Euwe, Reshevsky, Fine! And what do they have? They only have Mikhail Botvinnik – a quiet, reserved young man who couldn’t show anything remarkable here in Hastings, only sharing 5th–6th place. An argument immediately started, and Flohr exclaimed in exasperation: “Botvinnik will show you all, you’ll see!”
The skeptics didn’t have to wait for long. Just a few months later, the Soviet grandmaster stunned the entire world by sharing first place with Flohr at the Second Moscow International tournament, ahead of the leading Western grandmasters, including Lasker and Capablanca.
...Moscow, 1948. The Pillar Hall of the House of the Unions, lit by crystal chandeliers. The spectators applaud Mikhail Botvinnik. Far away, on the stage, grandmaster Milan Vidmar, the chief arbiter of the World Championship match tournament, pulls the new world champion into a tight embrace.
As I remember all that, I sense the historical importance of this evening even more sharply. Knowing that tomorrow Misha Tal, a boy from Riga, is going to challenge the renowned leader of the Soviet chess school for the world championship title, makes my heart beat even faster.
And then, the tired, breathless Swedish grandmaster finally arrives. We can proceed with the draw.
The next day, at 4:55 p.m., the arbiters, players and their seconds arrive on stage at the Pushkin Theater, greeted by loud applause. Numerous photographers, both Soviet and foreign, click their camera shutters.
5 p.m. Stahlberg asks the photographers to leave the stage and starts white’s clock. The match begins.
No. 64, French Defense
M. Tal – M. Botvinnik
1960, Moscow, World Championship Match, game 1
After thinking for a minute, Tal moves his king’s pawn:
1.e4
Botvinnik, also after a minute’s thought, moves his king’s pawn as well, but only one square forward:
1...e6
Studying Botvinnik’s opening repertoire, we of course paid most attention to his signature opening – the French Defense – which he played very successfully with black. We considered the possibility that the world champion would play the first game in a sharp style, to prove to his aggressive opponent that his hopes for success in a combination battle were futile. Eschewing quiet continuations, we concentrated our attention on the line that occurred in the game Gligoric – Petrosian at the Candidates Tournament.
This was precisely the line that occurred in the first game:
2.d4 d5 3.Nc3 Bb4 4.e5 c5 5.a3 Bxc3+ 6.bxc3 Qc7 7.Qg4
7...f5
Editor’s note:
The move 7...f6 is now more popular at grandmaster level.
8.Qg3 Ne7
Editor’s note:
Modern strong players prefer 8...cxd4 or 8...Nc6 here, while the game move is considered dubious.
9.Qxg7 Rg8 10.Qxh7 cxd4 11.Kd1
Editor’s note:
Both actual games and analysis show that 11.Ne2! is more dangerous.
11...Bd7
The world champion uncorks a novelty in the very first game! Petrosian played a much weaker move 11...Nbc6, and after 12.Nf3 Nxe5 13.Bg5! got a difficult position. The purpose of 11...Bd7 is not just to complete development, but also, if possible, to launch a counter-attack with Ba4, threatening d4-d3.
12.Qh5+ Ng6+
Editor’s note:
A better move here is 12...Kd8!, as black usually played in subsequent games.
13.Ne2 d3 14.cxd3 Ba4+
It was better to play 14...Nc6 and then castle long.
Editor’s note:
1) From the analytical point of view, after 14...Nc6 15.d4 0-0-0 16.Qf3, white’s two extra pawns are more valuable than his opponent’s activity. Still, black definitely retains some practical chances. 2) The computer doesn’t consider the game move a mistake: it thinks that Koblenz’s recommendation 14...Nc6 and Botvinnik’s actual move are more or less equally good (but only if after 14...Ba4+, black plays 15...Nc6, which didn’t happen in the game).
15.Ke1 Qxe5
Editor’s note:
15...Nc6 with subsequent long castling gave black far better practical chances; instead of regaining one of two lost pawns, black should have placed his hopes on activity.
16.Bg5! Nc6 17.d4 Qc7
18.h4!
The pawn isn’t going to be promoted in the near future. The main purpose of this move is getting the h1 rook into play as soon as possible.
18...e5
Practically the best move.
19.Rh3! Qf7 20.dxe5 Ncxe5 21.Re3 Kd7 22.Rb1 b6
22...Bc6 was more resilient.
23.Nf4 Rae8 24.Rb4 Bc6
25.Qd1!
Now white’s attack cannot be stopped.
25...Nxf4 26.Rxf4 Ng6 27.Rd4 Rxe3+ 28.fxe3 Kc7 29.c4! dxc4 30.Bxc4 Qg7 31.Bxg8 Qxg8 32.h5 Black resigned.
Before game 6, Tal asked me, “For how long will I be kept in the stalls?”
This comparison was very apt for the second, fourth and fifth games. All these games were played in a quiet, positional style, and the Riga player had a hard time holding off the attacks of his formidable opponent.
In these games, Tal, as usual, strove for tactical complications, but Botvinnik decided, completely correctly, that trying to compete in line calculations with the young grandmaster, or, to put it simply, playing as his opponent wanted, was not exactly in his interests.
Botvinnik was 25 years older than his opponent. With age, chess “reflexes”, especially in positions rife with tactical opportunities, undoubtedly slow down. We also have to consider that the world champion had rarely competed in big tournaments in recent years. The lack of practice also showed most clearly in sharp positions.
This was convincingly proven in the third game, when after
1.e4 c6 2.Nc3 d5 3.Nf3 Bg4 4.h3 Bxf3
, Tal made a paradoxical move
5.gxf3!?
.
“You shouldn’t play chess like that,” Tigran Petrosian said the next day.
The commentators unanimously criticized the move as well.
But should we really have to prove that if a grandmaster plays such a move, it’s not because he “doesn’t know what’s he doing”? He knows the general positional considerations – any first-category player would decide against 5.gxf3 because of them. However, the move 5.gxf3 does have a purely positional foundation: it bolsters white’s center and opens the g-file to put pressure along it.
We prepared 5.gxf3 chiefly out of psychological considerations. The resulting position was hard to anticipate beforehand. Tal’s calculations paid off: the world champion spent 40 minutes on his first eight moves. In the ensuing battle, Tal needlessly decided not to execute his initial plan, got into a difficult position and had to give up a pawn. Still, Botvinnik ended up in time trouble – precisely at the time when there were great complications on the board. In the end, he had to struggle for a draw.
It became clear to us that, after this game, Botvinnik would avoid lines that led to unclear positions rich with mutual tactical chances.
Only in game 6 did the challenger finally manage to create a double-edged position with great complications on the board.
No. 65
M. Botvinnik – M. Tal
1960, Moscow, World Championship Match, game 6
14...Rfc8
Tal wrote about this move: “Black (partly out of psychological considerations) has decided to concentrate his efforts on the queenside and then, when his opponent lets his guard down, play f7-f5. I must admit that I already had the idea of sacrificing a knight on f4, but it was hazy at the time. Black put his king’s rook on c8 for two reasons: to keep the a7 pawn protected and to convince white that he wasn’t even thinking about the kingside.”
15.Rb1 Nh5 16.Be3 Qb4 17.Qe2 Rc4
Editor’s note:
From Tal’s comment above, it’s clear that the psychological aspect played an important role in his plan – as we shall see, black’s last move, which is aimed at a very complicated struggle, paid off completely from this point of view. On the other hand, had white found the best (but completely counter-intuitive!) reply a few moves later (see the editor’s note after his 19th move), he could have gotten a big advantage precisely because of the black rook’s position on c4. Objectively speaking, 17...a6 was stronger (to protect the pawn from possible future attacks), but then, after 18.Rfc1, white would have some space advantage on the queenside and just get a slightly better, quiet position. While had black played 17...f5?!, then, after 18.exf5 Bxf5 19.Rbc1 Nf6 20.Nb5, white would clearly have the initiative.
18.Rfc1 Rac8
Editor’s note:
According to the computer, the strongest move here is to return the rook to its previous position, 18...Rcc8, which Tal probably never even considered. On the other hand, the prophylactic 18...a6 (getting the pawn away from the attack) is bad due to 19.a3! Qb3 20.Bf3, threatening both to capture on h5 or play 21.Qd2, intending to trap the queen with 22.Bd1.
19.Kh2
White’s strategic plan is not hard to understand: he’s intending to trade queens with 20.a3 Qb3 21.Qd1! and transition into a better endgame.
Editor’s note:
Botvinnik likely made his last move to be able to play Bg2-f3 without worrying about the h3 pawn.
19.Bxa7 would have been met with 19...f5 with good compensation; in many lines, the resource Qb4-a5 is important, for instance: 20.Bf1 (with the idea 21.Qd2) 20...Qa5 21.Be3 (after 21.Qe3 Rb4, the white bishop on a7 is in big trouble) 21...f4 22.gxf4 exf4 23.Bd2 Qd8 with dangerous activity for the pawn.
However, the computer first forces black to commit to a queen move with 19.a3!, after which black’s position is difficult. After 19...Qb3, it’s now good to play 20.Bxa7 (much better than 20.Qd1? Qxd1+ 21.Rxd1 b5 22.Bxa7 b4 23.axb4 Rxb4 24.Be3 f5 with good compensation), getting a healthy extra pawn (20...b6 is not now a threat due to the simple 21.Qc2, while 20...f5 can be met with 21.Qd1 and a clear advantage or, even better, 21.Bf1 with the threat 22.Qd1). However, 19...Qa5 is even worse for black due to 20.Bf1! with a very strong threat 20...-- 21.Qe1 R4c7 22.Ra1!! and 23.b4, with no good defense in sight.
After Tal’s reply
19...f5!
, the hall buzzed like a beehive.
20.exf5 Bxf5 21.Ra1 Nf4!?
Editor’s note:
21...Nf6 is objectively stronger, but with the subsequent 22.g4 Bd7 23.a3 Qa5 (after 23...Qb3, the queen has no good squares and poses no threat) 24.Qd2 (intending Bg2-f1), white thwarts his opponent’s activity and remains slightly better because of his queenside space advantage.
Applause filled the hall.
Botvinnik’s second asked the arbiter to move the game to an isolated room.
Stahlberg was trying to quiet the public, while Golombek repeatedly pushed the button that lighted the electric sign “Quiet, please!”
Meanwhile, dramatic events unfolded on the board.
22.gxf4 exf4 23.Bd2
Editor’s note:
Black’s idea was refuted with 23.a3! (but, of course, not 23.Bxa7? Qa5) 23.a3! Qb3 (23...Qa5 24.Bd2+–) 24.Bxa7. Kasparov discovered that after the subsequent 24...Be5 25.f3! b6 (up until this point it was Tal’s analysis), white won with 26.a4!! with the ideas 27.a4-a5 and 27.Ra3 Qb4 28.Na2.
23...Qxb2
23...Be5 was stronger, but Tal decided against it because of the line 24.f3 Qxb2 25.Nd1! Qd4 26.Rxc4 Rxc4 27.Rc1 Rxc1 28.Bxc1 Qxd5 29.Bf1, thinking that the position was equal.
24.Rab1 f3 25.Rxb2
Botvinnik decided to trade queens while being a piece up, but probably missed Tal’s 26th move.
Grandmaster Salomon Flohr showed the following beautiful line several days later: 25.Bxf3! Bxb1 26.Rxb1 Qc2 27.Be4!! Rxe4
28.Nxe4!!
Now, after both 28...Qxb1 29.Nxd6 Rf8 30.Qe6+ Kh8 31.Nf7+ Rxf7 32.Qxf7 Qf5 33.Qxf5 gxf5 34.Kg3 Be5+ 35.Bf4 and 28...Be5+ 29.Kg2 Qxb1 30.Nxd6! Bxd6 31.Qe6+ Kg7 32.Qd7+!, white is better in the endgame.
25...fxe2 26.Rb3 Rd4! 27.Be1 Be5+ 28.Kg1
Here, Goldberg categorically demanded to move the game to another room.
The excitement in the hall had already subsided, so I thought that such a radical measure was not necessary. But the arbiter had already made a decision, and only demonstration boards remained on the stage.
28...Bf4
Here, probably as a result of this sudden change of environment, Tal missed a pretty combination: 28...Rxc3! 29.Rbxc3 Rd1 30.Rc4 Bb2, and black wins. However, after this move, white still can’t save the game.
29.Nxe2 Rxc1 30.Nxd4 Rxe1+ 31.Bf1 Be4
With active bishops and an extra pawn,
black won
easily.
This loss affected Botvinnik’s play in the next game. The world champion made a mistake in a good position, and the score was 5-2.
Now, having a three-point lead, Tal should have played more quietly, giving Botvinnik the opportunity to sharpen the position himself. However, Tal rejected such considerations right from the outset.
In game 8, Botvinnik gained a substantial advantage, but again got into time trouble and couldn’t find the right move.
No. 66
M. Botvinnik – M. Tal
1960, Moscow, World Championship Match, game 8
Botvinnik had three minutes left on his clock, while Tal had thirteen. White to move.
34.Rc1
A blunder. After 34.Rd4, white kept his advantage.
34.Rc1 Rbc8?
That night, during our analysis, we saw that black could have won with 34...Rec8. Tal had rejected it at the board out of general considerations – the rook could be useful to put pressure on the e-file.
Had Tal won, the score would have become 6-2, and the match would have been essentially over. This thought plagued us for the entire night. The well-known truth – a chess player should embrace his failures – wasn’t much consolation in this case.
But let’s come back to the game. Events unfolded quickly.
35.Na5 Bxe2 36.Rxe2 Nxc3 37.Rxc3! Rxc3 38.Nxb7
Here, Tal realized that the position had changed drastically, and I should say that he got confused. He automatically made a bad move, and suffered quickly from the consequences.
38...Rexe3
Black should have played 38...Rb8 39.Nxd6 Rd3 40.Nxf5 Rxd5 41.e4 Rxf5 42.exf5 Rxb6, and the game would have ended in a draw. (
Editor’s note:
Actually, after the subsequent 43.Kf2 Rb3 44.Re3, black loses. But instead of 41...Rxf5, black had 41...Rb5, after which it’s white who has to scramble for a draw. So, instead of capturing on f5 on the previous move, white should play 40.Rc2!, and after 40...Rxb6 41.Nxf5 Rxd5 42.Rc8+ Kh7 43.Rc7+, the game ends in perpetual check.)
39.Rxe3 Rxe3 40.Nxd6 Rd3 41.Nf7+!
Botvinnik’s sealed move. As soon as it was played on the board at the resumption,
black resigned
.
In game 9, Tal, after
1.e4 c6 2.d4 d5 3.Nc3 dxe4 4.Nxe4 Bf5 5.Ng3 Bg6 6.N1e2 Nf6 7.h4 h6 8.Nf4 Bh7 9.Bc4 e6 10.0-0 Bd6
, sacrificed a piece –
11.Nxe6!?
Tal’s own opinion about this sacrifice is quite interesting.
“I can’t agree with the numerous experts who criticized my opening choice, in particular, the knight sacrifice on e6. Tellingly, the majority of them don’t think that the knight sacrifice was absolutely incorrect. Most commentators agree that this sacrifice creates major difficulties for black. Concerning the psychological side, I think that if a chess player is convinced that his move is correct, he should have no ‘buts’ and no excuses if he deviates from his initial plan. Before game 9, we did overestimate the knight sacrifice a bit, thinking that black had to go for a difficult defense after the correct continuation, which is not that easy to find. We made a psychological mistake there. The point is not that the player who has a two-point lead (after just one-third of the match played) shouldn’t go for sharp lines. This is completely acceptable. Our mistake was underestimating Mikhail Botvinnik’s phenomenal analytical power, thinking that he wouldn’t expect the sacrifice. But the first moves after the sacrifice, and especially the fact that Botvinnik played rather rapidly, convinced me that my opponent had thoroughly analyzed the position that occurs after the sacrifice.”
When, after
11...fxe6 12.Bxe6 Qc7 13.Re1
Editor’s note:
An important alternative is 13.Nh5!?, which occurred, for instance, in the game Tal – Vukic, Bugojno 1978
13...Nbd7 14.Bg8+ Kf8! 15.Bxh7 Rxh7 16.Nf5
Botvinnik sacrificed a third pawn with
16...g6!
, it was clear to everyone that the world champion’s defensive maneuver was prepared beforehand.
White had enough compensation for the piece, but the Riga player failed to convert his chances, and
Botvinnik won
his second game in a row with precise play.
Tal’s mood after game 9 was aptly described by the man himself:
“Up until game 8, it was psychologically very hard to play: on the one hand, I had a subconscious feeling that the match was already almost over, but on the other hand, I realized that I still had a long and hard struggle ahead of me against such an unyielding player as Mikhail Botvinnik.
Now, after the match, I think that my losses in games 8 and 9 helped me to get out of that psychological dead end in the best possible way. After the score reached 5-4, the moment of weakness was overcome, and an equal struggle ensued; I believed in myself much more at the moment.”
Indeed, Tal played more quietly and confidently in the tenth game, and Botvinnik barely managed to draw.
In game 11, Tal defeated his opponent with his own weapon: giving the world champion no counterchances, he executed an attack in good positional style.
This defeat meant much more for Botvinnik than the simple loss of a point. The world champion had to admit that his opponent was able to outplay him strategically as well!
No. 67, Grunfeld Defense
M. Tal – M. Botvinnik
1960, Moscow, World Championship Match, game 11
1.Nf3 Nf6 2.g3 g6 3.Bg2 Bg7 4.0-0 0-0 5.c4 c6 6.b3 Ne4 7.d4 d5 8.Bb2 Be6
8...a5 was stronger. (
Editor’s note:
The strongest players of our time play 8...Bf5 most often.) After the game move, white gets a positional advantage.
9.Nbd2 Nxd2
Editor’s note:
Black could have gained more chances for counterplay with 9...f5!?, transposing into a line of the Dutch Defense. Another interesting move is 9...Qa5!?, which occurred in Sasikiran – Melkumyan, Bastia 2011.
10.Qxd2!
After 10.Nxd2, black had a strong reply 10...c5! After the game move, it’s not beneficial for black to win the pawn with 10...dxc4 due to 11.Ng5 cxb3 12.Nxe6 fxe6 13.axb3, and white has enough compensation for the pawn – pressure on both the queenside and the kingside. (
Editor’s note:
In the elite grandmaster game Vachier-Lagrave – Aronian, Saint Louis 2014, white, instead of Koblenz’s 13.axb3, played a novelty 13.Qe3!?, and after 13...bxa2 14.Qxe6+ Kh8 15.Qxa2, his compensation was more than enough.)
10...Na6
(
Editor’s note:
According to the MegaDatabase, this move was played only once, in this game)
11.Rac1 Qd6 12.Ne5 Rfd8 13.Rfd1 Rac8 14.Qa5
The queen on this square constrains black’s forces. He can’t offer a queen trade with 14...Qb4 or 14...Qc7 due to 15.Nxc6!. (
Editor’s note:
Actually, both these lines are OK for black: both after 14...Qb4 15.Nxc6 bxc6 16.Qxa6 dxc4 17.Ba3 Qb6 18.Qxb6 axb6 19.bxc4 Bxd4 20.Bxe7 Rd7 and 14...Qc7 15.Nxc6 bxc6 16.Qxa6 dxc4 17.bxc4 c5 18.e3 cxd4 19.Bxd4 Bxc4 20.Qa4 e5 21.Bf1 exd4 22.Rxc4 Qb8 23.exd4 Rxc4 24.Qxc4 Qd6 25.d5 Bf8 he has good drawing chances, despite being a pawn down.)
14...dxc4
Editor’s note:
The best move was probably 14...Bxe5!?, recommended by Tal and favored by the computer – black keeps his center stable, exchanges an important knight and intends to trade the queen as well. Kasparov came to the conclusion that after 15.dxe5 Qb4 16.Bc3 (up to this point – analysis by Tal) 16...Qxa5 17.Bxa5 b6 18.cxd5 Bxd5 19.Bh3 Ra8 20.Bd2, white has only a marginal advantage. Also, in this line, black can play 15...Qc7!? (instead of 15...Qb4). After the game move, black is also only slightly worse, but it’s harder to defend in a cramped position when there are still a lot of pieces on the board; in addition, white’s pawn center now becomes more dynamic. We should also note that there are some improvements for both sides in the next few moves.
15.Nxc4 Qc7
Botvinnik attempts to simplify the position with trades.
16.Qe1! Qb8 17.e4 Bxc4! 18.Rxc4 Nc7 19.Bh3 e6 20.Bc1 Qa8!
(
Editor’s note:
By defending the c6 pawn, black creates the threat 21...b5)
21.Bg5 Re8 22.Qd2 f5! 23.Bh6
Editor’s note:
This bishop exchange is not good, because now black manages to successfully regroup his major pieces and equalize. Instead of Tal’s move, the computer recommends the following plan: deprive black of the resource Nb5 with 23.a4, then play Re1, put the bishops on f4 and g2 and go for d4-d5 or h2-h4-h5.
23...Bxh6 24.Qxh6 Re7 25.Re1 Rf8 26.Rc5 Qd8 27.Re5
27...Ref7!
Grabbing the pawn (27...Qxd4) was no good: after 28.exf5 gxf5 29.Bxf5, white retained a minimal advantage. (
Editor’s note:
Kasparov showed that in Koblenz’s line, black has no difficulties at all: 29...Ref7 30.R5e4 Qf6 31.Qxf6 Rxf6 32.Bxe6+ Nxe6 33.Rxe6 Rxf2 34.Re7 – Tal erroneously thought that white was still slightly better here – 34...Rxa2 35.Rxb7 Rd8, with an obvious draw. Also, Kasparov pointed out 30...Qc5!?, after which black is all right as well.) Now white has to think about defending the d4 pawn.
The world champion managed to coolly defend and parry all the threats; however, to fully equalize, he still needs to make some precise moves.
28.Qd2 Qd6 29.Bf1! Rd7 30.exf5 Rxf5 31.R5e4 Rf6?
With 31...Rd5!, black could have forced a draw: 32.Bc4 Rxd4 33.Rxd4 Qxd4 34.Qxd4 Rxd4 35.Bxe6+. In the ensuing time trouble, Botvinnik was noticeably nervous.
32.h4! Kg7 33.h5 gxh5 34.Rh4 Kg8 35.Bd3 Rg7
Editor’s note:
After this move, black is in big trouble. One of the better continuations was 35...Kf8, getting the king to safety. If white plays 36.Re5, like in the game, then, after 36...Rdf7, he has nothing better than 37.Re2, which, of course, doesn’t sit well with his attacking plans.
36.Re5! Rff7
Editor’s note:
And now, it’s too late to double rooks: 36...Rgf7 37.Rexh5 Rxf2 38.Qg5+ Kf8 39.Rxh7+–.
37.Qh6
Editor’s note:
The queen will have to return after a couple of moves. The immediate 37.Rexh5 was stronger.
37...Qe7 38.Rexh5 Nd5! 39.Qd2! Nf6 40.Rh6 Qd6 41.Rf4
It was time to adjourn the game. After a 40-minute think, Botvinnik sealed the move
41...Qf8
.
Analysis showed that black’s position was very difficult. The queen move was the least expected for us. The main continuations we analyzed were 41...Ng4, 41...e5, and 41...Nd7.
After 41...Ng4, there was a strong reply 42.Bxh7+ Kf8 43.Rxf7+ Rxf7 44.Rg6 Rxf2 45.Rg8+ Kf7 46.Qd3, and black loses one of his pieces.
After 41...e5, we first looked at 42.Rf5. However, we decided against it because of 42...Qxd4, threatening 43...Rxg3+. So we quickly agreed on 42.dxe5 Qxe5 43.Rf5! Qa1+ 44.Kg2, and white wins quickly. 41...Nd7 couldn’t save black either due to 42.Rxf7 Kxf7 43.Bxh7 c5 44.Qe2!, and white’s attack is unstoppable.
42.Qe3 Nd5 43.Rxf7 Qxf7 44.Qe5!
With 44.Qxe6, white could win a pawn; however, after 44...Qxe6 45.Rxe6 Rg4 46.Bc4 Kf7, the position was easier for black to defend.
44...Nc7 45.Qc5! Qf3
A small trade combination follows.
46.Bxh7+! Rxh7 47.Qg5+ Kh8 48.Qd8+ Kg7 49.Rxh7+ Kxh7 50.Qxc7+ Kg6 51.Qxb7 Qe4 52.Qa6!
White is two pawn ups, but to convert his advantage he needs considerable technique. For instance, if white takes the third pawn with 52.Qxa7, black gets a strong passed pawn in the center after 52...Qe1+ 53.Kg2 Qe4+ 54.Kh2 Qf3 55.d5. (
Editor’s note:
Koblenz means that black will take on d5 with one of his pawns.)
52...Qe1+ 53.Kg2 Qe4+ 54.Kf1 Qb1+ 55.Ke2 Qc2+ 56.Kf3 Qf5+
If 56...Qc3+ or 56...Qd1+, then 57.Kg2, and white wins an important tempo.
57.Ke3 Qg5+ 58.Ke2 Qh5+ 59.Kd2 Kf6 60.Qxc6 Qa5+ 61.Qc3 Qxa2+ 62.Ke3 Kf7 63.d5! exd5 64.Qc7+ Kf6 65.Qc6+ Ke7 66.Qxd5
, and
black resigned
several moves later.
This tenacious battle lasted for seven and a half hours.
In game 12, Tal had the initiative as well. However, both players were quite tired and didn’t play too precisely. The game ended in a draw on move 72.
Quick bloodless draws upset the spectators, who expect exciting fights in every game. You can understand the spectators, but you should understand the players as well – the match is exhausting and requires total concentration. That’s why Tal wanted to make peace quickly in the thirteenth game, even though he played black. He needed to consolidate his success. In addition, the Riga grandmaster felt very tired.
After 50 minutes of play, Tal achieved a draw, but this game upset him a great deal.
Tellingly, Tal left the Pushkin Theater through the back entrance after the game – he didn’t want to face the fans...
A month of tenacious struggle, endless analysis of adjourned games that often lasted the whole night – all that was exhausting for both players.
The knowledge that a legion of chess fans is watching the match helped to overcome tiredness. Letters from the whole country flowed in every day. The young grandmaster was greeted by people he knew and people he didn’t, but everyone wished him victory. They gave advice, even offered their “proven” opening lines that “guaranteed” success.
“Keep going, Misha, we believe in you!” people of various ages and walks of life wrote.
Mikhail Fetisov, a Don Cossack, sent a touching, encouraging telegram:
“Mikhail, you will win, you have to win! The sons should be stronger than the fathers. If this wasn’t so, the world wouldn’t make progress!”
