The next three games were also drawn, but those weren’t “grandmaster draws”. Peace was agreed only after all possibilities to fight for a win were exhausted.
Tal turned reserved and reticent. I realized that there was no sense in advising him to “play coolly” and “stabilize the position”.
During lunch before game 17, we didn’t say a word to each other.
“This time,” I thought, “there would be no draw...”
At first, the game was peaceful, and it was hard to predict that it would end so dramatically. When the following position occurred on the board, I was in the press center.
No. 68
M. Tal – M. Botvinnik
1960, Moscow, World Championship Match, game 17
The grandmasters and masters, filling the room as usual, expected 12.Qd2 here and thought that the game would end in a quick draw.
But the clock was ticking, and Tal was still thinking. Finally, he played
12.f4
.
“This looks like suicide!” the oldest Soviet grandmaster, Grigory Levenfish, exclaimed.
“Now it’s clear that Tal will lose this game,” said someone at the other end of the room.
I quickly came on stage to evaluate the position independently of others’ opinions.
If Tal loses, everything starts anew... Yes, white did seriously weaken the center with his last move. Nevertheless, Tal’s plan was clear: avoid the queen trade at all costs and increase the pressure.
I often saw Tal go all-out and succeed, but in this game his position was growing increasingly worse. With simple, logical moves, the world champion was improving his position. And Tal tried to sharpen the game at any cost, regardless of material losses.
The battle reached its culmination. Both players were in time trouble. The first to lose his cool was... Botvinnik’s second, Grigory Goldberg. He closed his pocket chess set, stood up quickly and left the stage. Sometimes imminent victory makes you even more anxious than impending defeat...
Of course, I was anxious too. Will Tal still keep his fighting spirit, I thought, if the score turns 9-8?
The fate of the game was decided literally in the last seconds. Botvinnik missed a tactical blow – a rook sacrifice. While spectators, seconds and arbiters considered whether the sacrifice was correct, time trouble ended. The match arbiter gave Botvinnik an envelope, so that the world champion would seal his move. But he never sealed a move. Ten minutes later, after Botvinnik convinced himself that his position was hopeless, he resigned. This game decided the fate of the whole match.
There were many discussions about Tal’s “strange” twelfth move. In his book about the Botvinnik match, Tal shared his thoughts.
“The unfamiliar quiet match strategy turned out to be more difficult for me than I had imagined. And it suffered a total fiasco in game 17.
It’s hard to make yourself play every game with the same desire to win, but it’s even harder to move towards the goal with tiny steps. The goal of making seven draws in the remaining eight games seemed incredibly hard for me.
Before the game and during the opening, I was totally sure that I would follow the advice of my experienced coach, the advice that had helped me to keep the pleasant two-point gap. But at a certain time (I think this feeling is familiar to every chess player), I just couldn’t help but do what was forbidden. Very, probably excessively risky play completely unexpectedly brought me an important point. I realized that the quiet, waiting strategy wouldn’t work for me in the remaining games – I just couldn’t keep my nerves in check. I made my moves automatically – sometimes good, sometimes not so good, but playing according to the match score just wasn’t working... Such a foolish strategy almost led to a disaster in the next, 18th game. Only Botvinnik’s uncertainty in time trouble prevented him from punishing me.”
And now, let’s look at Tal’s concrete considerations for moving the f-pawn.
“Finally, my wandering gaze settled on the move 12.f4. At first, I felt somewhat uncomfortable, because the flaws of this move are quite obvious, but in such a position there are no moves without flaws. The merits of this move are less obvious, but they do exist, even if, if I may say, not strictly in the chess dimension. First of all, this move requires refutation, and this refutation involves a double-edged tactical battle, which, considering M. Botvinnik’s playing style in this match, was not desirable for him. Secondly, the weakness of white’s position could only be exposed with a break in the center, and after c6-c5 and e6-e5, the white bishops become significantly stronger. And, finally, black would be able to attack the kingside only after castling long, but this would allow white’s queenside pawn mass to move forward too. Perhaps black just had to play 12...0-0, but this is not a refutation of 12.f4.”
These explanations help to understand Tal’s unique creative methods. Objectively, 12.f4 may be a bad move, but against that specific opponent in that specific situation, it provided real chances.
After game 19, where Tal scored his sixth win in a strictly positional style, the world champion’s second said, “So, we’ll meet in the return match.”
To become world champion, Tal needed to score just one point in the last five games. Game 20 was drawn, and Botvinnik came to the 21st game without his customary bottle of vitamin drink – the fate of the match had been already decided. The world champion offered a draw himself on move 17.
The score turned 12.5-8.5. The match was over.
The very next day after winning the title, Tal came to realize that the burden of glory was indeed very heavy. His hotel room was literally “occupied” by his friends and journalists. Even the Riga newsreel film crew were there, with all their bulky equipment.
The closing ceremony and the crowning of the world champion took place on 10th May.
...Mikhail Tal, the Komsomol member and Riga City councilor, takes the stage. FIDE Vice-President Marcel Berman places a gold medal on his chest and crowns him with a laurel wreath.
“On behalf of the International Chess Federation,” Marcel Berman says, “I declare Mikhail Tal the world chess champion.”
The Soviet anthem plays triumphantly...
Gens Una Sumus
“We are one family”. These words from the ancient language of Latin comprise the motto of the International Chess Federation. We’re at the 14th Chess Olympiad in Leipzig. People are talking in different languages – German, Russian, English, French, Spanish, Mongolian, many others – but chess players need no translators: the universal language of chess can be understood by everyone.
At the reception organized by the East German government in honor of the chess players, Paul Keres spoke on behalf of the Olympians:
“Despite our opposing interests, there’s cordial friendship at the tournament of nations. I think that many should learn how to maintain the spirit of international solidarity here!”
The last words are drowned in applause. The American chess players approach the Soviet team’s table and raise their glasses in friendship – friendship between the chess players of all countries, friendship between all nations.
Bobby Fischer offers to tell Tal’s future.
“OK. Tell me, Bobby, how will the return match against Botvinnik end?” Tal asks.
Fischer looks intently at the world champion’s palm and says, “You don’t like to lose!”
“Who does?”
The loud laughter stopped Fischer’s palmistry session.
We made a lot of new friends in Leipzig. Chess people from many countries asked the Soviet players for advice, and shared their joys and sorrows with them.
We learned that free Cuba had revived the glorious chess traditions of Capablanca, that chess was becoming widely popular among the people.
“We are proud that our country gave chess to humanity,” said the Indian journalist Chandragupta Agrawal in a short interview. “After winning independence, our team is taking part in the team world championship. We are obviously weaker than players from many other countries, but we’ll do everything in our power to catch up.”
Bombay student Manuel Aaron proved that these weren’t empty words. In the match against the Netherlands, he defeated the ex-world champion Max Euwe, while in the Argentina match, he drew with grandmaster Miguel Najdorf.
The English and French chess players had a hard time believing that the Mongolian government had assigned a comfortable three-story building to a chess club.
Concurrently with the Olympiad, Leipzig organized an exhibition entitled “Chess Through the Ages”.
It was quite a challenge to view all the exhibits sent by 150 museums and many private collectors from all over the world over such a short period of time.
The Soviet exhibition opened with a stand “Lenin and chess”. In the section “Chess is the people’s game”, foreign chess players could find the answer as to why new chess talents keep coming up every year in the land of socialism. The showcase of Soviet chess literature stunned the foreigners with huge print numbers: 30,000, 50,000, 100,000 copies...
In the same showcase, I saw our
Sahs
magazine, both in Russian and Latvian, as well as books published by the Latvian State Publishing House.
Everyone marveled at the chess pieces made by Russian artisan Shushkanov and inspired by the story of Sadko. These fairytale pieces were earlier awarded a Great Gold medal at the Brussels World Fair. The Central Art Workers’ House poster for the David Oistrakh versus Sergei Prokofiev chess match attracted much attention as well.
The average attendance of each round in Leipzig was around 5,000 – it’s a record for chess competitions.
The last round couldn’t change much in the tournament table. The Soviet team had already won, the US team had finished second, and Yugoslavia had taken third place.
We are accustomed to the success of the USSR national chess team, which always wins gold at the Olympiads. When the event is already won, the days of difficult battles get slowly forgotten. Those who follow the competition only in newspapers may think that the victory of our chess players, who are stronger on average than the foreign masters and grandmasters, was easy, almost a foregone conclusion. But those who witnessed the Olympiad in person know how difficult the decisive games truly were. The results were only settled literally in the last minutes...
The Soviet grandmasters were happy to write a new page in the history book of Soviet sports victories with their Leipzig win.
Mikhail Tal was, without a doubt, the most popular player at the Olympiad (world champions are always the center of attention). In the last round, Tal made sure that there was a sensation – he lost to the English player Johnathan Penrose.
“Now I have enough work for a year,” Penrose said after the game with a smile. “I’ll be going from town to town and showing my game against the world champion!”
Tal’s game against the Hungarian grandmaster Laszlo Szabo was rather interesting.
Szabo, an aggressive, attacking player, got a position to his taste. It seemed that Tal wouldn’t be able to withstand the onslaught of the black pieces. But in the close-quarter street fight, Tal suddenly launched a counter-attack and crushed black’s position.
No. 69
M. Tal – L. Szabo
1960, Leipzig, 14th Olympiad Final, round 4
Szabo gets rid of his double pawns and seizes the initiative.
22...b5 23.Bc5 b4 24.Na4 Rxe4 25.Bxd6 cxd6 26.Rxd6 Qe7
(planning to bolster the attack with a pawn sacrifice)
27.Rd2! Nh5
Editor’s note:
27...Re8 posed more problems for his opponent, activating the rook and retaining various options for the knight.
28.g3 Bh3 29.Ng2 Re8 30.Nc5
Tal thought for long on each move, while Szabo played quickly and assuredly: he thought that black’s attack would suggest itself. The next move was made by the Hungarian grandmaster without much thought either.
30...Re2
A mistake. The correct move was 30...Bxg2+ 31.Kxg2 Re3, with an equal position.
Editor’s note:
Actually, after the game move the position is equal as well.
31.Rxe2 Qxe2 32.Kg1 Bxg2
(white threatened 33.
R
e1)
33.Kxg2 Qg4
Editor’s note:
33...Qb5! was a much stronger move (on this square, the queen protects both the a6 and c6 pawns and completely nullifies the idea Qd4-c4+) 34.Qd4 Ng7=.
34.Qd4!
White goes on the counter-attack. Now it was Szabo’s turn to think for a while.
Editor’s note:
34.Qf3 was probably stronger – on the one hand, white is targeting c6, while on the other hand, he offers to transition to an endgame which will be better for him – black’s a- and c-pawns are too weak.
34...a5 35.Rf2 Kf7
Editor’s note:
This move is a mistake, and black’s position is now very hard. He should have preferred 35...Qf5, improving the queen’s position.
36.Ne4 Kg7
Editor’s note:
36...Re7 still gave black some chances to save the game.
37.Qa7+ Kg8 38.Nd6 Rf8 39.Qxa5 Qe6 40.Qxb4 Qd5+
(a “death-bed” check!)
41.Kg1
Szabo sealed his 41st move, but then resigned without resuming the game.
We were sitting in the Astoria hotel restaurant, eating our lunch. Several hours remained until the USSR – Argentina match. Suddenly, the opposing team’s leader, Miguel Najdorf, appeared before our table, holding a napkin.
“I’ll accept all your sacrifices,” he jokingly assured Tal. “You see, I’ve successfully competed with world champions Capablanca and Alekhine!”
“I’ll make a sacrifice for sure. I hope you’ll keep your promise,” the world champion answered.
In the following game, you shall see if they kept their promises.
No. 70, Sicilian Defense
M. Tal – M. Najdorf
1960, Leipzig, 14th Olympiad Final, round 4
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 d6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 Nf6 5.Nc3 e6 6.Be3 a6 7.f4
Editor’s note:
This move is not popular in modern times. The main line is 7.f3, protecting the e4 pawn and intending Qd2 and g2-g4.
7....b5 8.Qf3 Bb7 9.Bd3 Nbd7 10.0-0
Editor’s note:
10.g4 is usually played in this position.
10...Be7 11.a3 0-0
Black has a somewhat cramped but solid position in this line. It’s not easy for white to overcome his defenses.
Editor’s note:
As a rule, modern strong grandmasters don’t castle in this position, instead playing 11...Nc5 (putting pressure on the e4 pawn) or 11...Rc8 (activating the rook, with a positional exchange sacrifice on c3 possible in many lines).
12.Qh3 Qc7 13.Rae1 Nc5 14.Bf2 d5
If black manages to play d6-d5 in the Sicilian, it usually means that all opening difficulties are over. So, after making his 14th move, Najdorf went for a walk around the hall, smiling contentedly.
Editor’s note:
In this position, 14...d5 is a mistake. One of the possible alternatives is the prophylactic 14...g6, as played in a number of subsequent games.
15.exd5 Nxd3 16.cxd3 Bxd5
16...Nxd5 is bad due to 17.Nxe6!
17.Nxd5 exd5 18.Nf5 Bc5 19.d4 Ba7 20.Bh4 Ne4
21.Rxe4! dxe4 22.Bf6!
White’s attack is now unstoppable. After
22...Qb6
22...gxf6 23.Qg4+
23.Bxg7 Rfe8 24.Be5 Qg6 25.Nh6+ Kf8 26.f5, the Argentinian grandmaster resigned.
After 26...Qg5 there’s 27.Nxf7! winning, while after 26...Qc6 white plays 27.Qh5 Re7 28.Qg5. (
Editor’s note:
In the last line, 28.Qg5 doesn’t work because of black’s reply 28...Rxe5–+. White should play 28.f6 instead, winning immediately.)
Mikhail Botvinnik’s play left a very good impression. He didn’t suffer from time trouble that had plagued him so much in the match. He managed to achieve that by quick opening play. It seemed like the ex-world champion had set some kind of schedule for spending time. Botvinnik again showed his superior endgame technique and great opening erudition. It was clear that he hadn’t lost confidence after his defeat in the match.
Botvinnik’s Revenge
And again, like just a year ago, we’re in Moscow, in the National Hotel. It’s almost ten o’clock. The draw for the return match starts in a few minutes.
Two weeks ago, the medical commission determined that the world champion’s health wouldn’t allow him to start the match as scheduled, on 15th March. The Council of the USSR Sports Society and Organization Union granted the request of the Latvian Chess Federation to start the match in mid-April.
But something unexpected happened. The world champion said that doctors weren’t prophets, and that they couldn’t predict how he would feel in a few weeks!
After that, I became completely convinced that Tal totally lacked any self-preservation instinct, and this could eventually lead to serious consequences.
I still tried to convince him to listen to the doctors; however, unlike Tal the world championship challenger, Tal the world champion didn’t listen to me. He wouldn’t budge.
“You should understand,” he assured me, “I’ve been so anxiously waiting for the chance to play Botvinnik again...”
The draw was held in old style: Stahlberg held a white and a black pawn in his closed fists. The world champion got black.
When grandmaster David Bronstein was asked who would win the return match, he replied, “The one who wins the first game.”
And so, on 15th March 1961, Botvinnik and Tal sat at the chess board again. This time, the games were held in the Variety Theater.
Of course, the result of just one game is not important in a marathon competition, but a win in the first round is encouraging, it makes you believe in yourself more.
The opponents make their first moves quickly. The game transposes from a Nimzo-Indian Defense into a line of the Ragozin System.
Botvinnik chooses a rather passive continuation, and Tal seems to be seizing the initiative.
The ex-world champion Max Euwe, who sat beside me, was perplexed. Where is Botvinnik’s renowned opening erudition? How did he manage to get a worse position with white?
The Tal fans are obvious to spot in the hall: they are beaming.
“So, it begins. Misha will show him!”
But Misha doesn’t “show him”. After some poor maneuvering, he gets a worse endgame.
No. 71
M. Botvinnik – M. Tal
1961, Moscow, World Championship Match, game 1
Here, black could still save the game with 30...Bf5! For instance, 31.Nd4 Bd7, then the black king heads to the center, and white can’t convert his small advantage.
However,
Tal played 30...Bd5 and quickly lost.
Editor’s note:
After Botvinnik’s
31.Nd4
, Tal, to prevent the threat 32.e4, was forced to play
31...c5
, and after
32.bxc5 bxc5 33.Nb5
, the white knight’s activity, coupled with black’s compromised pawn structure, became the decisive factor.
In his career up until then, he was capable of holding even tougher endgames, so this loss was an alarming symptom.
The second game. Botvinnik plays the Caro-Kann.
Before the start of the match, Tal half-joked that if Botvinnik “dared” to play the Caro-Kann, he’d develop his pieces according to the setup that he’d used successfully the previous year (Bc4 and Ne2) and “checkmate him quickly”. However, Botvinnik stopped white’s attack with his 9th move and equalized. Still, he made several mistakes in time trouble, and the game was adjourned in a position that looked won for Tal.
The next day, the newspapers reported: “When the game resumed, Botvinnik resigned after a few moves.”
So laconic! But Tal had to spend so much energy and work so hard to analyze this won ending! The analysis took almost all night and the morning of the next day. Here’s what Mikhail Tal tells us about this analysis.
No. 72
M. Tal – M. Botvinnik
1961, Moscow, World Championship Match, game 2
“There was no doubt that black would seal the move
41...Qa5
, because all other moves lost immediately.
For instance, 41...Nc3 42.Qd8+ Kh7 43.Qh4+ Kg8 44.a7.
41...Qa5
After 41...Qa5, white had an important choice. It’s easy to see that after 42.Qa7 b4 43.Qb8+ Kh7 44.a7 Nc7 white wins a piece, but black’s passed pawn was a great hurdle for converting the material advantage. There could follow 45.Qxc7 Qxc7 46.a8=Q b3 47.Qa3 Qc2 48.Qa4 Qc3 49.Qh4+ Kg8 50.Qd8+ Kh7 51.Qd3 Qb4, and white’s win is quite problematic.
Editor’s note:
The move 48...Qd1 (instead of Tal’s 48...Qc3) removes all doubts, for instance, 49.g3 e5 50.Qb5 (50.Kg2 Qd5+ 51.Kh2 b2 52.Qc2 Qd4 53.Bd3 b1=Q 54.Qxb1 Qxf2+ 55.Kh1 Qxg3=) 50...Qb1 51.Qd3 Qc2 52.Kg2 e4, and white can’t improve his position.
In our search for ways to win, we analyzed the natural move 42.a7 the most. (Interestingly, Botvinnik mostly focused on lines after 42.Qa7.)
And so,
42.a7 b4
(the only move that repels the threat 43.
B
xb5).
Here’s the initial position where white had to search for the winning move. It turns out that it’s hard for white to activate his queen. 43.Bb5 attracted our attention: 43...b3 doesn’t work due to 44.Qa4. But then it turned out that after 43.Bb5, black had 43...Nc7, and if 44.Bc6, then 44...b3 and it’s a draw, as white can’t win with 45.Qd8+ Kh7 46.a8=Q due to 46...Qe1+.
Alternatively, if instead of 44.Bc6, white plays 44.Qd8+ Kh7 45.a8=Q, winning a piece, then 45...Qxa8, and the black pawn can move again.
And so, if white wins a piece while the black pawn hasn’t moved beyond b4, then white should win without much technical difficulty.
Editor’s note:
Tal’s last assertion looks questionable and is not confirmed by computer analysis. In a similar vein, below Tal gives the line (after the game move 43.Bc4) 43...g5 44.g3 g6 45.Kh2 Kh7 46.Kg2 Kg8 47.Bb5 and writes that 47...Nb6 is bad, “because white wins a piece in the most beneficial situation” – without a doubt, he means 48.Qxb6 Qxb6 49.a8=Q+ here. But, despite several hours of analysis, the computer fails to show a way to win for white after the subsequent 49...Kg7 or 49...Kh7 – and if such positions are indeed impossible to win, then the adjourned position is drawn too!
Coming to this conclusion, we started to analyze the position after the quiet
43.Bc4
, which was the move I played. The moves 43...
K
h7 or 43...
K
f8 lead to the same position.
Editor’s note:
He means that after 43...Kh7 44.Bxd5 exd5 45.Qh4+ or 43...Kf8 44.Bxd5 exd5 45.Qh4 black is forced to continue 45...Kg8 (in the first case it’s the only legal move, while in the second it’s the only one that continues resistance), as a result of which both lines lead to the same position analyzed in the next sentence.) After 43...Kh7 44.Bxd5 exd5 45.Qh4, black has only one move – 45...Kg8.
Now, by playing 46.Qe7 Kh7 47.Qb7 Qa1+ 48.Kh2 Qe5+ 49.g3 Qe2 50.Qxf7 Qa6 (if 50...Qa2, then 51.h4 b3 52.h5 b2 53.Qxg6+ Kh8 54.Qe8+ Kh7 55.a8=Q Qxa8 56.Qxa8 b1=Q 57.Qxd5 with a won endgame for white) 51.Qe7 g5 (white threatened 52.Qh4+ and 53.Qd8+) 52.Qf7!, white wins.
And so, we found a way to exploit the weakening of black’s kingside, who now has no remedy against Qh5+ and Qe8+, or Qf8. In the case of 52...Kh6, 53.g4 is winning.
It’s clear that king moves won’t help black, so now we can analyze the other lines after 43.Bc4.
The queen’s best position is on a5, so we ignored moves such as 43...Qa3: white wins easily with 44.Bxd5 exd5 45.Qb6 Kh7 46.Qb8. Therefore, black can only make moves with his knight or pawns.
I. 43...g5. This move takes away the important h4 square from the queen; however, it also weakens the kingside.
Editor’s note:
Unfortunately, Tal’s subsequent analysis contains a number of inaccuracies that lead to erroneous evaluations. It would be impractical to point out all such moments – this would make the text, already heavily saturated with lines, even harder to read. So, we’ll show just two critical points:
A) As the reader shall see, Tal gives the line 44.g3 Kh7 45.h4 gxh4 etc. in his subsequent notes, coming to the conclusion that white should win. But instead of 45...gxh4 (which is a mistake), black saves the game with the study-like 45...g6!! (planning 46...Nb6; the immediate 45...Nb6? is bad due to 46.hxg5 Qxa7 47.Qh4+ Kg8 48.g6! Qa1+ 49.Kg2 Qf6 50.Qh7+ Kf8 51.Bb5!) 45...g6!!
46.hxg5 b3!! (luring the bishop onto a bad square) 47.Bxb3 Nb6 48.Bxe6 (48.Qxb6 Qxb6 49.a8=Q Qxb3 – this line shows the point of black’s move 46...b3) 48...Qe1+ 49.Kg2 Qxe6 50.Qxb6 Qxb6 51.a8=Q Qd4, and black holds easily, despite white’s extra pawn. The computer’s first line 46.Bb3 (instead of 46.hxg5) doesn’t win either – after the subsequent 46...Nb6 47.Qxb6 Qxb6 48.a8=Q gxh4 49.gxh4 (or 49.Qh1 Qd4 50.Qxh4+ Qxh4 51.gxh4 g5 52.hxg5 Kg6=) 49...Qd4, white cannot convert his material advantage.
B) After 44.g3 g6 45.Kh2 Kh7 46.Kg2
black also immediately achieves a draw with 46...b3!! (instead of 46...Kg8) 47.Bxb3 Nb6 48.Bxe6 (after 48.Qxb6 Qxb6, the bishop is hanging) 48...fxe6 49.Qxb6 Qxb6 50.a8=Q Kg7. So, instead of 45.Kh2, white has a much stronger move 45.Bb5, getting, after 45...Nb6 46.Qxb6 Qxb6 47.a8=Q+, an endgame with a piece for two pawns and the black pawn still on b4, which white wanted from the outset. That said, as we pointed out earlier, this endgame might be unwinnable after all!
Now back to Tal:
White can just play 44.g3, and black has nothing better than 44...g6 (after 44....Kh7, there’s 45.h4 gxh4 46.Qxh4+ Kg8 47.Bxd5 exd5) 45.Kh2! The idea of this move will soon become clear.
Black should play 45...Kh7 and meet 46.Kg2 with 46...Kg8, after which 47.Bb5! wins.
For instance, 47...b3 48.Qa4; or 47...Nc7 48.Bc6 Kh7 49.Qf6! (49.Qd7 is weaker because of 49...Kg7). If now 49...Qxa7, then 50.Qxf7+ with the subsequent Be4, and white’s attack is unstoppable. 49...Qf5 can’t save black either after 50.Qe7.
There’s a question why white had to make two king moves. If the black king is on h7, white’s plan doesn’t work, because after 43...g5 44.g3 g6 45.Kh2 Kh7 46.Bb5
black has the reply 46...b3, and the endgame after 47.Qa4 Qxa4 48.Bxa4 b2 49.Bc2 Nc7 is not completely clear (
Editor’s note:
Analysis shows that this position is drawn). So, the black king should be on the eighth rank.
And what’s the difference between the white king’s position on h2 or g2?
Let’s return to 43...g5 44.g3 g6 45.Kh2 Kh7 46.Kg2 Kg8 47.Bb5 Kh7 (47...Nb6 is bad because white wins a piece in the most beneficial situation [in the line 48.Qxb6 Qxb6 49.a8=Q+ see the editor’s note three pages earlier]) 48.Bc6 Nb6. If the white king were instead on h2 now:
(to the line)
the position would be drawn because of 49.Qxb6 Qxb6, threatening f2.
If the king is on g2, however:
white wins with 49.Qxb6 Qxb6 50.a8=Q, because black has no time to play 50...b3 due to 51.Qb7. After 51...Qxb7 52.Bxb7 f5 53.Ba6 white’s victory is assured.
That’s the way to win in the line 43...g5.
II.
43...f6
.
Botvinnik chose this move. Black wants to bring his king closer to the passed pawn, but he weakens his kingside. White, without wasting time on prophylactic moves, immediately played
44.Bb5
. After
44...b3 45.Qa4 Botvinnik resigned.
The key idea of the move 44.Bb5 is clearly shown in the main line – 44...Nc7 45.Bd3! f5 (if 45...Kh7, then 46.Qh4+ Kg8 47.Bxg6) 46.Bc4. Now the white queen controls all the dark squares, and so black’s position is hopeless.
Can we say then that the adjourned position couldn’t have been saved anyway? It’s hard to answer this question. After 43...Nc7 44.Qd8+ Kh7 45.a8=Q Qxa8 46.Qxc7 Qa1+ 47.Kh2 Qc1!, black got good counterplay. 47...Qc3 is worse due to 48.Qf4 b3 49.Qh4+ Kg8 50.Qd8+ Kh7 51.Qd3.
Had Botvinnik played 43...Nc7, white would have needed to prepare to win the piece with 44.g3 and Kg2, but black’s kingside wouldn’t have been compromised. To be honest, we couldn’t find a forced win in the last line, but after losing a piece, it would be hard for black to avoid defeat.”
Despite this important win, Tal’s mood didn’t improve. At that time, we received an additional opening card index from Riga, covering more than 5,000 games. Looking through it, we found the game Uusi – Makogonov from the Soviet team championship, where black successfully tried the defense 9...Nd5 (precisely the move Botvinnik played in the second game).
Tal never said a word, but I felt that he was upset. Playing in the Stockholm tournament and a simultaneous display with Czechoslovakian youngsters several months before the return match was of course useful for training, but it couldn’t replace deep theoretical preparation.
In the third game, Tal got a good position, but missed his opponent’s unexpected move, and Botvinnik gained a substantial positional advantage. The world champion defended valiantly, but he couldn’t save the game. That was an impressive Botvinnik victory in a logical, positional style.
In the fourth game, Tal gave his opponent complete freedom in the center and launched a quick pawn onslaught on the queenside.
Grandmaster Boris Spassky said in the press center, “Misha should be punished for such anti-positional play.”
International master Lev Aronin disagreed: “Tal is bold, and I like his original plan!”
This was the first game of the return match where Tal got a position to his taste. But this time, Botvinnik played very precisely. With a subtle, deeply considered exchange sacrifice, he repelled the attack and went on the offensive himself. Tal’s position noticeably worsened.
In general, Botvinnik didn’t suffer from time trouble in the return match, but in this game he did lack time to think and failed to find the strongest move. As a result, Tal managed to salvage the game.
After game 4, Tal thought that Botvinnik’s strategic weapon, like in the previous match, wouldn’t play a decisive role. However, the very next game showed that these hopes were unfounded. The ex-world champion again imposed a quiet, maneuvering struggle on Tal. The position was approximately equal, but Tal played without inspiration, got into time trouble (he had four minutes left for 16 moves) and ultimately lost a pawn. Still, he managed to avoid defeat in the resumption.
In the sixth game, the young world champion gained a promising position. Then his opponent equalized. In a position where neither player was able to upset the balance without dire consequences for themselves, the grandmasters agreed to a draw.
In the seventh game, Tal deployed the Nimzo-Indian Defense as black. We anticipated that Botvinnik would play the Samisch Attack and prepared a rare line. After nine moves, a well-known theoretical position occurred.
No. 73
M. Botvinnik – M. Tal
1961, Moscow, World Championship Match, game 7
Here, white usually played 10.e5, and after 10...Ng8, continued 11.Ne2 Qd7 12.Kf2 Nc6 with the subsequent 0-0-0. Chances are roughly equal in this case.
But Botvinnik, after a short think, played
10.Bg5!
After
10...h6
, there followed the stunning
11.Qa4+!
, and
Tal couldn’t equalize. He resigned
on move 33.
Late that night, when we analyzed the game, Tal was wondering, “Did Botvinnik anticipate that I’d play this half-forgotten line? It’s the first opening ‘knockout’ in my life!”
Of course, it was unpleasant to see that the line we had specifically prepared for the return match hit us back like a boomerang.
“I saw the true Tal’s play again,” the ex-world champion Max Euwe wrote to me from Amsterdam after game 8.
No. 74, Caro-Kann Defense
M. Tal – M. Botvinnik
1961, Moscow, World Championship Match, game 8
1.e4 c6 2.d4 d5 3.e5 c5
3...Bf5 is considered the strongest move, but Botvinnik avoids this exhaustively-studied continuation, preferring to interpret the position in the spirit of the French Defense. When he finally risked playing 3...Bf5 in game 10, Tal caught him by surprise with his ultra-original move 4.h4!?
4.dxc5 e6 5.Qg4
Editor’s note:
In modern practice, white usually plays 5.Be3, 5.a3, or 5.Nf3.
5...Nc6
Editor’s note:
5...h5 is good here, for instance, 6.Qg3 h4 7.Qg4 Nh6 8.Bxh6 Rxh6 with unclear play.
6.Nf3 Qc7 7.Bb5 Bd7 8.Bxc6 Qxc6 9.Be3 Nh6
10.Bxh6
Tal went for this trade without hesitation, giving his opponent the “proud” bishop pair. The two bishops usually yield a certain positional advantage. By playing 10.Bxh6, Tal committed to a concrete strategic plan where the white knights play a major role.
10...gxh6 11.Nbd2 Qxc5 12.c4!
It’s dangerous to take on c4, because the white knight might get to e4, targeting d6 and f6. White also puts the c-file to good use.
12...0-0-0
Editor’s note:
12...dxc4 13.0-0 Bc6 (or in a different order – 12...Bc6 13.0-0 dxc4) was much more solid, for instance, (12...dxc4 13.0-0 Bc6)14.Ne4 Bxe4 15.Qxe4 Rc8 16.Qxb7 Bg7 17.Rfc1 0-0 18.Qe4 Qd5, with a roughly equal position.
13.0-0 Kb8 14.Rfd1 Qb6
15.Qh4
Controlling the dark squares and attacking the h6 pawn.
15...a5
Black prevents b2-b4, but seriously weakens his queenside. 15...Rg8 or 15...Rc8 were worth considering.
16.Rac1 Rg8 17.Nb3! a4
Here, black could have also put up more resistance with 17...Rc8. After the game move, white’s attack develops very quickly.
18.c5! Qc7 19.Nbd4 Rc8
19...Bxc5 was dangerous due to 20.b4 axb3 21.Nxb3 b6 22.a4!
20.b4 axb3 21.axb3 Qd8
Even the queen trade doesn’t save black from a deadly attack.
22.Qxd8 Rxd8 23.b4 Rg4 24.b5 Rc8 25.c6! Be8 26.Rc2 Bg7 27.Ra1 Bxe5 28.Nxe5 Rxd4 29.Nd7+! Black resigned.
After 29...Kc7, there’s 30.b6+ Kd8 31.cxb7, while after 29...Bxd7, white plays 30.cxd7 Rd8 31.Rc8+! Rxc8 32.Ra8+.
Botvinnik was still in the lead, but it looked like Tal was slowly recovering his form.
Suddenly, the world champion had to deal with another dangerous opponent – flu. The ninth game didn’t take place on schedule – it was only played a week later.
After Botvinnik’s first move 1.c4, Tal decided to use a system recommended by Paul Keres, with an aggressive early queen lunge.
Before the match, we decided to avoid excessively sharp lines with black, but this time the world champion wanted to turn things around. Such a strategy wasn’t entirely safe, but, on the other hand, it did bring Tal his greatest successes.
Events unfolded in a very unpleasant way for the Riga player. Botvinnik’s seventh move showed that even in this rare line he had prepared a novelty. Tal fell victim to a theoretical surprise again!
Leaving the kingside pieces undeveloped, Tal launched a risky queenside attack. The ex-world champion easily repelled it and already had a won position by move 16.
After play resumed, Tal tried to create some complications, but nothing came of that: the score turned 5.5-3.5.
Tal’s opening play in the tenth game was quite esoteric. He made his first seven moves with pawns! “Tal probably forgot that he could move pieces in chess as well,” grandmaster Tolush joked.
Indeed, Tal’s position looked quite strange. “Reckless”, “Bad theoretical preparation”, I heard from every direction. Only Bronstein said with a smile, “Original idea!”
Tal’s strategic plan wasn’t as flawed as many commentators thought. This is evidenced, among other things, by the fact that Botvinnik didn’t risk repeating the opening in games 14 and 16. Although the strategic plan wasn’t erroneous, Tal’s two “experimental” moves, ninth and tenth, were. They ruined everything. Those were perhaps Tal’s weakest opening moves in the whole match. Botvinnik exploited his opponent’s mistakes in quite an instructive way and added another important point to his score.
I must admit that I was surprised when Tal, after a short think, suddenly chose the Slav Defense in game 11. Botvinnik could have gone for the exchange variation that led to a symmetrical position.
It was clear that Tal needed some rest, and he wanted to take a small break. But purposefully playing for a draw is a special skill. Tal lacks that skill, and I hope that the chess world will forgive this flaw of his.
Tal got a difficult position out of the opening and lost again. After the game, Botvinnik told us that he’d analyzed this line 17 years earlier...
By now, Botvinnik had increased his lead to four points. The gap was already big, but Tal still had some hopes – there were 13 games left to play!
In game 12, Botvinnik played the French Defense for the first time in the match. Tal attacked energetically, and his win was quite convincing.
What conclusion could we reach after the first half of the match? How to explain Tal’s collapses?
With a deeply thought-out strategy, Botvinnik managed to avoid positions that were rich in mutual tactical opportunities, those positions where Tal’s combinational talent could fully manifest itself. Tellingly, in many games, Tal got into adverse strategic situations while still in the opening.
Game 13 was a shining example of Botvinnik’s strategic mastery. He didn’t allow his opponent to fan the flames of complications.
No. 75, King’s Indian Defense
M. Botvinnik – M. Tal
1961, Moscow, World Championship Match, game 13
After
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 g6 3.Nc3 Bg7 4.e4 d6 5.f3 0-0 6.Be3 e5
, the following position occurred.
Botvinnik played
7.dxe5
The queen trade doesn’t promise white much, but it’s psychologically sound: it’s hard for black to organize counterplay. In other circumstances, the capture on e5 would mean a refusal to play for an advantage in the opening, an intention to play for a draw. But each draw got Botvinnik closer to his aim!
7...dxe5 8.Qxd8 Rxd8 9.Nd5 Nxd5 10.cxd5
This position had occurred in the game Boleslavsky – Najdorf (Candidates Tournament 1953).
10...c6 11.Bc4 b5
In the aforementioned game, Najdorf played 11...cxd5 12.Bxd5 Nc6 and achieved a draw relatively easily. But Tal can’t be satisfied with such a result, so he tries to complicate the game.
12.Bb3 Bb7 13.0-0-0 c5
The struggle becomes more complicated, but white now has a strong passed pawn in the center.
Editor’s note:
In subsequent games, black usually played 13...cxd5, and then, after 14.Bxd5 Bxd5 15.Rxd5 Rxd5 16.exd5 Nd7, black is all right.
14.Bc2! Nd7 15.Ne2 Bf8 16.Nc3 a6
This seemingly logical move almost turns out to be the decisive mistake. It was better to play 16...b4 and then Nb6.
Editor’s note:
If white meets 16...b4 with 17.Na4, then, of course, not 17...Nb6 (this would lose the c5 pawn), but, for instance, 17...c4 with a roughly equal position. After 17.Nb5, black should avoid 17...Nb6 as well (white has an unpleasant reply 18.d6) and play 17...a5 instead, with the idea of a5-a4 and Ra5.
17.b3! Rac8 18.Bd3! Nb6 19.Be2! Rd6
The rook is a bad blockading piece, but the b6 knight needed some protection.
20.Kb2! f5
Black is trying to activate his rook, but he weakens the e5 pawn.
21.Rc1 Rf6
22.a4! bxa4 23.bxa4 a5 24.Kc2 c4 25.Rb1 Bb4 26.Na2! Bc5 27.Bxc5 Rxc5 28.Nc3 Bc8 29.Rb2
Editor’s note:
29.f4! is much stronger – after the subsequent 29...Nd7 30.fxe5 Nxe5 31.Rb5 Rxb5 32.axb5, it’s very hard for black to defend.
29...Bd7?
After 29...fxe4 30.fxe4!, white would still have needed some effort to win the game. Now, black just falls apart.
30.Rhb1 Bxa4+ 31.Nxa4 Nxa4 32.Rb8+ Kg7 33.R1b7+ Rf7 34.d6 Rxb7 35.Rxb7+ Kf6 36.Rxh7 Rc8 37.d7 Rd8 38.Bxc4 Nc5 39.Rf7+ Kg5 40.Bb5 fxe4 41.fxe4 Black resigned.
In game 14, Tal got a dangerous initiative, but Botvinnik managed to hold. The game was drawn.
An unfortunate mistake cost Tal a full point in game 15.
No. 76
M. Botvinnik – M. Tal
1961, Moscow, World Championship Match, game 15
Tal, who had black, played
28...Rd7?
“I wrote down 28...Rc7 on the score sheet, but mistakenly put the rook on d7,” he explained after the game. “When my opponent played 29.g5!, I played 29...h6 automatically, because I was totally sure that my rook was on c7.”
28...Rd7?
There followed
29.g5! h6? 30.gxh6 Bxh6 31.Ng4
It turns out that due to the threat
N
f6, black has to part with his bishop:
31...Bg7 32.Nf6+ Bxf6 33.Bxf6. Botvinnik won on move 63.
Botvinnik misplayed the opening in game 16, and Tal got good attacking chances.
“It will end in ten moves,” the spectators decided.
But Tal played with hesitation, and Botvinnik, who sacrificed a pawn, managed to organize some counterplay. In a complicated major-piece endgame, Botvinnik played very precisely and drew the game on the 90th move, after an eleven-hour battle.
Each draw got Botvinnik closer to the goal, so Tal had to go for complications at any cost. Game 17 is an example of such an all-in approach. Tal turned a completely equal position into a lost one. However, Botvinnik’s time trouble helped Misha: the challenger made several mistakes, and Tal managed to win in the resumption.
Tal was always eager to try some unbeaten paths, but now he decided to improvise. In game 18, the world champion made moves that no grandmaster could understand. Even Tal himself couldn’t explain them in the post-game analysis. To cap it off, the Riga player also blundered a pawn. The next day, he resigned without resuming the game.
This was Tal’s worst game in both matches against Botvinnik.
It seemed incredible that, after such painful defeats, Tal still had the strength to continue the match. After a ten-hour battle, Botvinnik lost the nineteenth game.
In game 20, Tal slowly outplayed his opponent.
No. 77
M. Tal – M. Botvinnik
1961, Moscow, World Championship Match, game 20
After the game resumed, there followed:
42...Ne6 43.Ke3 Rd7! 44.b4 Kf7 45.Nc5?
The waiting move 45.Rc1! would have won the game. If 45...Ke7, then 46.Nc5! Nxc5 47.bxc5 Ra8 48.Rg1! Kf7 49.e6+!, or after 45...Ra8 there was 46.Nc5 Nxc5 47.Rxc5 with the subsequent 48.a4 and b4-b5.
45...Nxc5 46.bxc5 Ra8!
We missed this move in our analysis.
Later, Botvinnik made a mistake, and the game was adjourned for a second time.
Tal sealed the move
89.Ka2
.
Our plan seemed to lead to a win: 89...Rb5 90.a7 Ra5+ 91.Kb3 Kb7 92.Rf8. Here, we automatically continued 92...Rxa7 93.Rf7+ Kb8 94.Rxf5, and after 94...Ra1, there’s 95.Kb4 Rd1 96.Ka5 and Kb6, winning. A more complicated, but still won endgame arose after 92...Kxa7.
Editor’s note:
In the line 92...Rxa7 93.Rf7+, instead of the erroneous 93...Kb8, the correct continuation is 93...Ka6 94.Rxf5 Kb5, getting enough counterplay, for instance: 95.Rf8 Ra8 (black can also put the rook on e7, g7, h7 or a1, activating it) 96.Rf7 (capturing on a8 is stalemate) 96...Ra7 97.Rf6 Ra4 with a draw.
The first moves were made quickly:
89.Ka2, 89...Rb5 90.a7
Editor’s note:
90.Rf8 won, for instance: 90...Ra5+ 91.Kb3 Rxa6 92.Rxf5 Kd7 93.Rf6 Ra1 94.f5 Rd1 95.Kc3 Rc1+ 96.Kd2 Rf1 97.Rf7+ Kd8 98.Kc3 Rb1 99.f6 Rf1 100.Kb4 Ke8 101.Rh7 Rxf6 102.Ka5 Rf4 103.Kb6 Rxd4 104.Kxc6 Rg4 105.Rd7 d4 106.Kc7+–
90...Ra5+ 91.Kb3 Kb7 92.Rf8 Rb5+!
The first unexpected move. Tal quickly played
93.Ka4
, not noticing that after
93...Kxa7 94.Rxf5 Rb1
[which was played]
95.Rf7+ Ka6! 96.Rc7 Rb4+! 97.Kxb4, the black king is stalemated.
A draw was now unavoidable. [The game ended in a
draw
after 121 moves.]
Meanwhile, 93.Kc3 still gave white some practical winning chances.
The last ray of hope was extinguished. The score was 12-8. There was no doubt that Botvinnik would score the necessary half-point in one of the next four games.
In game 21, Tal tried to complicate play for one last time, but Botvinnik launched an attack himself and won.
Winning the return match 13-8, Mikhail Botvinnik regained the title of the world’s strongest chess player. With his convincing win, he wrote a new, brilliant page in the book of the history of world championships. Regaining the title was a triumph of the will, the result of a well considered training system, deep psychological approach, incredible strategic mastery and precise technique.
(back to the text)
If the white king were instead on h2 now:
the position would be drawn because of 49.Qxb6 Qxb6, threatening f2.
Afterword
With his brilliant victories in difficult tournaments, Mikhail Tal proved that he was one of the strongest chess players of our time. He successfully progressed through the numerous qualification stages, overtaking famous grandmasters, and earned the right to face the world champion. He won the ensuing battle convincingly.
Yet just a year passed, and Tal suffered an equally crushing defeat from the seemingly vanquished opponent.
Discussing the reasons for Tal’s loss, V. Vasilyev wrote in the
Yunost
magazine:
“As sad as it sounds, Mikhail Tal, who always stood out as a pointedly humble man, who was always reverent towards his grandmaster colleagues, lost his ability to soberly, critically evaluate himself, his own strength and his opponents’ strength after winning the world champion’s title. Tal’s friends worriedly and then anxiously saw that he stopped listening to advice, even if it was given by renowned people of the chess world. The constant victories dulled his sense of danger.”
These harsh words correctly describe the reason for the catastrophe that the young world champion suffered in the return match.
Indeed, Tal lost his sense of danger – but wasn’t it facilitated by the atmosphere of adoration created around the young world champion, the excessive praise thrown around by his overexcited fans?
“Genius”, “crazy boldness”, “the ability to play without regard for the logic of the position” – that’s only a small part of the things said about Tal!
Didn’t all those admiring songs of praise contribute to Tal’s feeling of complacency and recklessness?
Losing the return match was a timely, severe life lesson for Tal.
When Tal returned to the hotel after game 21, he sat wearily in a chair. He had a lot to think about, many things became much clearer. The thought that he had deeply disappointed everyone whose care and support he felt at every step weighed heavily on his mind.
The first person who tried to help Tal with friendly advice and restore his self-belief was... Botvinnik! After the last game ended, Botvinnik invited his opponent to dinner. Raising his glass, he said, “Yuri Gagarin flew into space after long, intense training. If Mikhail Tal follows Gagarin’s example in this regard, I’m sure he’ll reach cosmic heights!”
Several days later, at the closing ceremony, Botvinnik continued his thoughts: “If Tal manages to ‘program’ himself and prepare for tournaments more carefully, he’ll probably be unstoppable.”
Many didn’t pay much heed to these words. Everyone knew that Botvinnik would remain a tough opponent. Would Tal be able to regain his self-belief and reach the right conclusions from his loss?
Tal put in only a modest performance in the European team championship, sharing third-fourth place, but at the big international tournament in Bled he again shone with brilliant play. The strongest grandmasters of the world took part: Petrosian, Keres, Fischer – the most likely world championship candidates. Tal won the tournament.
This was a brilliant victory made possible by colossal will-power. Most importantly, he overcame himself.
In Bled, Tal displayed the diversity of his talent. He crushed Fridrik Olafsson, Bruno Parma and Ludek Pachman in a sharp attacking style, and made Miguel Najdorf, Borislav Ivkov and Arthur Bisguier resign after a subtle positional, maneuvering struggle.
The victory in Bled showed again that Mikhail Tal, a true chess artist, remains one of the world’s strongest grandmasters.
I remember the words of one English journalist who said after Botvinnik’s world championship win in 1948: “We respect the new world champion deeply, but we aren’t sure that Botvinnik would have become such a brilliant chess player if he lived in another country.”
These words are fully applicable to the 1960 world champion as well. No other country provides such a wide array of opportunities for the comprehensive development of human skills and flourishing of great talent as the country that builds communism.
And Mikhail Tal’s creative path is a shining example of that.
Riga, 1961
